THE RED PAGE.
ON GOING TO LONDON
FIRST, remember that you are not wanted
in England. As Stead said to me, “ What
are all you Australians doing here at all ?
Why do you come across here and expect
us to hand over our billets to you ? Have n’t you
got a country of your own ? Don’t you grow
newspapers and publishers out there ? What do
you mean by dumping yourselves down in a
country that is already overcrowded with appli-
cants for work ? Why don’t you get your living
in your own land ? ”
And he is right. England is overstocked with
dead men’s shoes. For every billet on the press
there are three persons competent and waiting—
and you are an outsider. The London editor
doesn’t know you, doesn’t want to know you, has
never heard of you or your work. And he does
know your London rival. He was at Oxford with
his father, or a bishop has mentioned his name,
or they both come from the same county, or he’s
a friend of somebody in the same club, or a nephew
of somebody on the Stock Exchange. And they
understand each other. Englishmen .stick together
and keep each other’s mangers warm. Very natur-
ally. For the Australian on arrival is quite use-
less for ordinary journalistic purposes. He
doesn’t know his way about. As Harmswortli
told me when in' my first fortnight in England I
asked him for a lordly position on his staff, I was
“no use whatever to ” (and his voice became sud-
denly reverent) “ The Daily Mail.” I didn’t know
who was the Gold Stick in Waiting. And in
England it appears that they attach considerable
importance to the Gold Stick in Waiting.
Besides, no London editor wants you on his
staff. The trend- of the new journalism is to use
a man’s brains for three months and then deposit
him on the mat. They want you to contribute, to
give them your best and freshest, and then make
room for somebody more audacious. True, there
is room for one Australian on most of the staffs of
the London journals ; but there is only room for
one. You have a letter to him ; he is polite, but
you cannot expect him to offer you his own sub-
editorial chair. It will only seat one.
J*
In most London offices there is a battalion of
subordinates whose business it is to prevent you
seeing the editor. Herein comes in the value of
clothes. A silk hat will save you three snubs. I
got in touch with llarmsworth by clinging to
Seddon’s skirts. He arrived about the saine time,
and after the usual rebuffs, I pencilled on my
card, “ From Maoriland —know all about Seddon.”
Immediate result—interview with Harmsworth,
request for interview with Seddon, suggestion for
article on London, and commission for article on
Maoriland for his New Liberal Review. The Daily
Mail pays three guineas for its special article
(1200 words), a guinea better than the usual price
for contributed matter in the London press. Once
The St. James’s Gazette paid me 12s. fid. for an
article of 1200 words. I communicated to the
editor the result of iny conjectures about his
parentage.
The most annoying experience of a contributor
at the other end is his inability to repeat a
success. After my first article in the Mail I wrote
a dozen articles. Iwo got as far as proof-slips,
and came back in that form after many days ;
one disappeared altogether ; one was accepted
and has never appeared yet; several I disposed
of in the provincial press ; the rest I still treasure.
An Australian friend, who is now war-editor for
The , and has worked his way from
actual starvation to GOO/. a year, on his arrival in
London tried every paper in turn with a half-
column item. When it had gone the rounds,
rejected by even the halfpenny rags, he sent it
to The Times. They printed it, and gave him
10/. for it. He has never got another line into
The Times.
The chief trouble with the Australian in
London is the time it takes to acclimatise him.
He is a foreigner there ; the Englishman does not
think the same thoughts; the parent-stock and
the. colonial descendant belong to different gene-
rations. In brain processes the Australian is
more akin to the American ; they take each other
for granted, allow each other’s points of view,
which the Englishman never does. It took me
exactly eighteen months to get down to the
level where I could see things from the English
point of view. It is n’t necessary to write from
that point of view ; it is essential to find out
what it is, and allow for it. And till you do, it is
useless to attempt to interest the Englishman,
save as a critical outsider. And the Englishman
does not think much of the outsider. The only
thing for the new-chum in England, while he is
being acclimatised, is to catch and pin his first
impressions of London and the English, and serve
them up still wriggling to London editors. Write
on London as it strikes you, London cats, London
fogs, London humour, London editors, London
butlers—anything, as long as it is London. For
the Londoner cares only for London ; that is the
most valuable lesson to be learned in England.
J*
But Australians are so popular in England just
now ! Are they ? Ask the first man you meet
on the top of a ’bus. He smoulders with a dull
resentment against Australia. What do they
mean out there? What are they after with those
labour laws and that socialism ? What do they
want to keep the Japs out for? Why can’t they
be quiet ? Why can’t they be like Us ? That is
really the basis of the Englishman’s distaste for
a continent. The untravelled Englishman con-
ceives of the Empire only as a series of Little
Englands. Australia, by being Australian, annoys
him,. And London is too busy with its own affairs,
its street improvements, its latest musical play, its
missions, its elaborate pretence of politics, its
trams and its tubes and its week-ends, to care
much for a vague abstraction called the Empire,
still less to concern itself about a mere flat section
of King Edward’s Dominions Beyond the Seas.
The ambitious Australian journalist ought to
come well-prepared for the siege. I took enough
to last me for three months. Take enough to last
you for eighteen months—say 150/., and you’ll
survive, You start in London quite, unknown.
If you’re twenty-five, you’re exactly five years ,
behind the man who came down from Oxford at
the age of twenty. It is merely beginning one’s
life again. And if you mean to write articles,
read all the papers you can and clip and file any-
thing that bears on your particular subjects.
If you get one article accepted out of every five
you write “on spec.” you’ll do well.
And there is more to fight in England than the
tardiness of the recognition of such ability as you
possess. There is the almost physical depression
that the immensity of London produces—the con-
viction that one is but a useless and unconsidered
atom in a jostling aggregation of stranger atoms,
the utter despair of ever effecting anything in that
old grey city that has lost its enthusiasms, the
terrible dread that one human being can’ never
move anything in this placid centre of a stable
civilisation. There is the cold, blank stare of the
Englishman; even the English houses have it —
miles and miles of bleak, blank, grim, expression-
less houses fronting each other: as I went into
London between those dull hostile visages, on a
day when the air looked like a sediment, I felt my
individuality shrinking to the insignificance of the
hero in one of Sime’s nightmares. The feeling of
loneliness, of monotony, of friendlessness, must be
daily fought. You will want the sun, and want it
badly. But it’s no use crying for it—in London.
&
But perhaps you, as I, will be beguiled into the
paths of literature. I had forgotten the short
stories you wrote for The Otago Witness, the para-
graphs in The Bulletin , and, oh, yes, that slim
volume of trickling verse; You have your novel,
perhaps your comedy. Don’t be in a hurry to
publish your novel ; you’ll make more out of two
short stories than out of any first novel. After
your sixth novel, so an experienced novelist told
me, you may expect an income. But there is The
Name. As A. P. Watt remarked to me, nobody
remembers the name of the writer of a good short
story ; ten short stories haven’t the cumulative
effect in advertising the writer that three novels
give ; but there is little money result (save to the
publisher) in the first three novels. And your
novel is bound to be about Australia or Maoriland.
London readers and critics don’t particularly
desire to know about either place. Too far away.
Fisher Unwin told me that for a colonial novel
there was only a limited circulation—the sale that
there was for a translated continental novel, or
for an unbooihed American book. Australia is a
foreign country to the average English reader. I
sent Tussock Land to two publishers before Un-
win took it. I had five 6n my list, and when it
came back from the fifth I had decided to re-write
it and try it on them again. Sometimes I
wonder . . .
In short stories there is a possible living for the
budding author. He won’t get fifty pounds a
story at first; but by good work he may creep up
from three guineas to twenty-five forafive-thousand
word tale. This result is achieved by re-sales. I
wrote short stories for nine months in London
without getting one accepted. I went through
the ritual of sending them round to every editor
in London, and sometimes the punctuality with
which they returned was almost appalling. The
house in which I had rooms possessed no letter-
box—merely a slit in the door; and I was often
awakened in the morning by the heavy, sickening
thud of a returned manuscript reverberating along
the hall. But out it went again.
Then I tried a literary agent. It seems to be
the opinion here that literary agents ask for a fee
in advance. I merely state the fact that no
literary agent has asked for any fee from me, nor
from any writer I know in London, except their
commission after the story is sold. My literary
agent tried my stuff on English editors with the
same punctual results ; then he suggested America.
I told him he could do so at his own expense of
duplicating the MSS. He did so, and in a month
Leslie's Monthly took a story oi mine, for which I
got, I think, three guineas. Other American
magazines followed, and my prices gradually rose.
The latest sale of a five-thousand word story
brought me 15/. in America, and 8/. more
for its > re-sale to a London magazine. The
commission charged by my agents was 20 per
cent, for American sales. and 10 per cent, for
English sales. It is these re-sales and the opening
up of new avenues for the disposal of one’s work
that testify to the value of a literary agent.
There is also the serial to be considered. This
is Dead Easy ; you write ten or twenty thousand
words a week, and you never stop' to think.
There is an enormous market for cheap sentimen-
talism and impossible adventure, and the caterers
for that demand can keep their motor-cars. It
may serve to boil your billy.
There are also the serious monthlies. But a
young author can hardly keep alive on the
monthlies. The Nineteenth Century kept an
article of mine for four months before accepting
it. I got 20/. for it, which I understand is their
invariable minimum price. But the other monthlies
do not scruple, on occasion, to pay miserably—as
compared with the current rates for magazine fic-
tion. Never let your short story Exceed five
thousand words, and the nearer to two thousand
you can keep the better the chance of its consider-
ation. Editors are always polite in the printed
slip that comes back with your rejects. The Smart
Set will gently indicate exactly the sort of stories
they don’t want, and ask you to try again.
Pearson’s go to the most trouble. I reproduce
here their advice to authors—a signpost to the
royal road of magazine literature set up by a pur-
veyor of fiction for the populace.
The Editor has considered the enclosed MS., but regrets
that he is unable to make use of it. The reasons which,
in the Editor’s opinion, make the story unsuitable for the
pages of Pearson’s Magazine are indicated by crosses in
the appended form.
fis too long.
is not clear and bright enough,
is too tragical.
• .1 is too emotional.
I contains too strong a religious element.
\contains too much of the supernatural.
/ is too slight.
The Plot J is not sufficiently plausible.
I is not sufficiently novel.
(Js too complicated.
That is Pearson’s Magazine’s idea of literature.
There are other magazines—and other ideas.
J*
Oh, and I had forgotten all about that little
book of unpublished verse ! These words are
written upon the fog that is the gate of London,
“ Abandon ye all verse who enter here ! ” There is
not one publisher in all London who would touch
verse commercial speculation. Yet poetry is
occasionally published. As a matter of curi-
osity, I used to investigate the conditions of
publication of every little poetry book that peeped
above the grass in Spring. I simply sent in my
little book of verse, and asked the publisher why
he published ***** and not Me? The answer was
“commission.” The little dream-child had been
paid for, advertisements and all, by its father.
There were a few exceptional cases, where the
publisher was a personal friend of the poetess’s
husband. And there was always the willingness
to handle my stuff “on commission.” I have been
fortunate enough in getting my two books of
verse published without cost to me—though also
without, so far, the least cash result; but a poet
who expects to be paid for his fun—and it is fun !
—should try the Stock Exchange. The edition of
men like Watson and Yeats in London seldom
exceeds four hundred copies. Compare Ogilvie s
or Paterson’s successive thousands. As a London
publisher wrote, “ To produce poetry and make.it
pay is, at the present moment, practically im-
possible.” Ncr; don’t worry about that little book
of verse. Show it to the girl— she ’ll lilt© it ; or
send the poems to the London magazines. Only
they won’t boil the billy. For “ Marama : the
Melon God ” in the Monthly Review I got five
guineas; for my recent “Bond Street’ in the
same review, one guinea. Compare Australian
prices, and sell your little, seed pearls here.
Yes, go to London and learn things. It’s a
good training for a journalist ; I should say a
most necessary training. It’s worth while ; and
you can come back. For myself, I mean to he
back in London in eighteen months. In London
one meets editors and publishers, one learns much
about one’s market; and it pays to keep in touch.
And it is worth while going merely to see. how
Australia looks upside down at the far end of the,
telescope. And I don't think you will think less
of Australia —even upside down—in England.
AURTHUR H. ADAMS.
Sorry to say it; but we are Commencing to skip
G. B. Shaw’s currencies.: his wit is fraying.at -the,'
edge. Per contra, Ounninghune Graham is more
admirable t.hdn ever. But the old spontaneous
G.8. -jester has become a jester with intention : a
jester'damned. From his last lecture—on Shake-
spear, e.g.:
Shalcespear was not a vulgar and illiterate man. He
<was'of a respectable class, for his father had been a, bank:
rupt. (Laughter.)
A little thin ? un peu passe ?
He himself regarded Shakespear in two ways. One of
Ms (Mr. Shaw’s) eyes was the eye of an artist, and the
other the eye of a plumber. With one he saw the art,
with- the other the artifice, (Laughter.)
The reporter put it in the past tense, dr we would
have doubt about the first eye.
Who shall find a virtuous Scotchman? for his
worth is far above... bawbees, at least, the New
Proverbs declare. When found, to be sure, he
would deny that he was Scotchman—that being
an English term of opprobrium invented by the
scoundrels who likewise vilified Scotch mist,
Scotch chocolate, Scotch fiddle, Scotch greys, and
Scotch seamanship. He would be Scot, like the
noble Sir Thomas of that ilk, who, being quar-
tered in England yet holding pristine valour
unimpaired, raised at one day’s notice 4000 men
and packed them to Dover to fight the Great
Armada, being therefor fitly commended by his
epitaph :
He—being call’d to special place
Did what 'might best behove him.
The Queenk o/ Engla.nd gave him grace ;
The King of Heav’n did love him. ..
He kept tall men, he rydd great hors ;
He did indite most finelye ;
He us’d few words, bat cold discours
Both wisely and dyvinelye.. .
Ambition he did not regard,
No boaster, nor no braggei;
He spent, and lookt for no reward,
• He cold not play the bagger.
Whereat the panegyrist rests and makes an end,
feeling that there is no more to be said in praise
even of the noblest Scot. "He cold not play the
bagger ” —testifying his worth far above bawbees,
as the New Proverbs declare.
This, then, was in his generation a virtuous
Scot, and in Cunnirighatne Graham, traveller and
writer of to-day, envisage another. There may
have been others interim : this deponent knoweth
not. But from the evidence of the epitaph one
would take Sir Thomas to belong to the type of
Scot with high cheek-bones, eyes slightly slanted,
and the curious squeezed skull that looks as if it
had been rounded by the attrition of ages of
hard and humble living—approximating the
Mongolian type, and as laborious and tenacious
as the Mongol. If so, he was the paragon of
his class. Graham is of aristocratic rank, as
different from the other as a laird from his
gillie. In his mental air you find that dry,
bracing savour so peculiarly Scotch, as if
gathered on a keen, wide moor not without its
heather and hint of honey. Hume had it, for
example—another aristocrat whose zeal for the
house of Stuart was his disgrace, says Bishop
Hurd, himself born merely of “plain, honest,
and good people,” arid owning all the defects
of those perilous qualities. And one may believe
there is nothing quite like it in other European
literature : always a little warmth of emotion or
mist of imagination comes to alter the chill glow
of the heatless Scottish sun.
The Academy, with an embarrassment ot nega-
tives, heads a review notice “Tales from the
Never Never NEVER Land ” —three “ Nevers.”
We have been accustomed to do with two, and
don’t know how we get even those. Morris’s
dictionary finds a quotation in 1857.
With the aid of three stock-keepers, soon after my
arrival at lUawarra, I had the cattle mustered, and the
draft destined for the Nievah Yahs ready for the road.
From Wild Adventures in Australia , p. 63 ; by 1. JJ.
Brebant Cooper, who adds in a footnote : “ Nievah Valis,
sometimes incorrectly pr nounced ‘Never Nevers, a
Comderoi term signifying unoccupied land.”
Well, it may be so and thus ; and then agin
A writer in The Fortnightly says:
Even Gorky’s physical type is maligned by most of the
photographs published. In these photographs he looks
nervisits, amemic, hunted, sentimental. The Maxim
Gorky whom I left a week ago ... is a tall, straight,
deep-chested, large-honed man, who towered like a <nant
over the squat Germans and stunted Lettish peasants who
are now struggling for racial dominion on the Livonian
coast. In features, he is as far removed from the refined,
weak-faced intelligents as from the submissive, apathetic
muzhik. The forehead is broad, furrowed deeply when he
talks, and surmounted by a inop of dark hair ; the eyes
grey, serene, slightly defiant; the nose ’big, not
unlike Tolstoy’s, but even more shapeless ; the mouth
big, somewhat grim ; and the jaw, now fringed with
a scanty red-brown beard grown in gaol, square, massive,
and resolute. You feel.at once that this’is a self-
possessed, masterful man, a man in whom character is
even more remarkable than intellect, who, even bad he
been born without the instinct for the natural and the
dramatic, and that verbal deftness which have raised him
among, his countrymen to unexampled fame, would not
the less surely have broken the bonds of birth and penury.
In tlie same review a Japanese poet’s dictum is
translated thus:
What is called poetry arises from the heart: man must
put into words that which lie feels. How diverse are our
emotions ! Well, emotion, in verse, is poetry.
Did n’t we say so ?
The Studio has a new story of Whistler.
Coming up to the easel of a new pupil, he looked
at the work in profound silence; then directing
his monocle and his business eye towards the
pupil, he began: “From New York?” “Yes.”
“Pupil of Chase?” “Yes.” “Um-111! I thought
so. Why did you paint a red elbow with green
shadows?” The young lady protested, “[ am
sure I just paint what L see.” He nodded his
head in grave appreciation of the case, adding,
“ But the shock will come when you see what you
paint.” ,
The same magazine quotes Menzel on impres-
sions : “Itis a mistake'to try to be impressionistic.
The right thing is to use your eyes and to render
everything as well-defined as possible—with its
atmosphere. The impression will look out for
itself.”
Jerome Hart, ed. of S.F. Argonaut . has been
travelling and collecting impressions of
opera. Here they are, Paris leading :
PariS: —Fine artists in. the leading rdles ; good artists jn
the secondary roles ; perfection in the ensemble.
London—A few famous vocal artists ; ensemble, medi-
ocre ; ballet, poor ; orchestra, fair.
Dresden— Ensemble, admirable ; vocal artists, admir-
able, but not rising to greatness.
Mugdph— The same.
Vienna*- Mediocre.
Berlin- Medi owe.
Rome— lnferior.
Naples —lnferior.
New York— The. greatest artists in the leading rfiles;
those in the secondary roles not always excellent;
orchestra, admirable ; stage setting and scenery, only
fair ; ballet, very poor.
Sydney? Melbourne? These are barbaric vil-
lages without culture. Sydney is the city which
boasts that it owns “the largest organ in the
world,” and once owned one of the finest
organists : it permitted its municipal donkeys to
dismiss him, and now purveys ? -rate
recitals to ? -rate audiences. Mel-
bourne is the city which permitted a clique
of Prejsbyterian bigots with heads stuffed
with 17th-century cobwebs to oust, from its
University the single musician of original genius
and enthusiasm it possessed. “If this should
meet the eye ” of any ardent Australian, let him
resolve to put off sloth and petty vices and devote
himself to breaking the images of pompous stupi-
dity enthroned in so many Australian high
places, and beslavered continually by sycophants
as ignoble as themselves. One ardent young
man, picking his mark and attending closely
to the business of publicity, should -be able
to drive one last-generation fossil into private file
in order that public offices may be filled With men
level with the spirit of the age. There should be
no living on one’s laurels in a public or semi-
public position : every holder should be compelled
to give every year new proof of energy and com-
petence. The wisdom of experience is valuable
truly ; but how often do we look for Minerva and
find only her blinking owl.
A.G.S.

Another Convincing Proof.
THIS TIME AT MERRYLANDS.
The Case of Mr. H. ALMOND, I
(By a Local Reporter.)
Whilst much of the anguish and grief
which exist in some famdies as the re-
sult of ill-health is hid from the gaze
oi the outside world, and is known
only to those who are immediately
concerned, sufficient transpires to ex-
cite the sympathy of every genuine
philanthropist, and to bring forth
strenuous efforts on behalf of suffering
humanity. Here is an instance in which
the reader will recognise a desiro on
the part of Mr. Henry Almond, store-
keeper, Park-road, Merrylands, to ex-
tend a helping hand to any who may
be afflicted as he was some years back.
“it was when I was living in Tam-
worth/' said Mr. Almond, “that I
first knew what bad health was, and
that must be a good while back, for i
have been living here these fourteen
years past."
“In. what way was your health af-
fected V' enquired a man from the
office.
“Well, for two years previous to
coming here, and for a good while af-
ter starting this business, I was al-
most mad with neuralgia, and you
may guess that having a complaint
like that so long must have had a
direful effect upon my system general-
ly. Really, sometimes I was fairly rag-
ing, owing to the agony i was in, and
many a time wished that I was dead
and out of the world altogether. Oh !
it was a life of fearful misery, and I
pity any poor soul in similar straits.
iou may rest assured that I resorted
to pretty well every known treatment
in the hope of gaining some relief, buff
success evaded all my efforts. My
gums, my jaws, the cheek bones were
the parts where the torture principally
existed ; but, curiously enough, there
were days when one side of my face
and head was affected till nightfall,
and then the pains shifted over to the
other side. And as for sleep—well, do
you think anybody could sleep under
such adverse circumstances? I know l
couldn’t, as a rule, though, at long
intervals, when nature could not hold
out any longer, I lapsed into a state
of unconsciousness that might be called
sleep, and whilst it lasted I had the
worst dreams you could think of."
“I don’t suppose you were usually
lit to do much work ?’’
“Indeed I was not, and T was also
so low-spirited that I did not seem to
care whether I was ever able to work
again or not. As a matter of fact I
had no interest in anything, excepting
that T wanted to get better if it were
possible, which didn’t seem very like-
ly, tor instead of getting better 1 got
worse as the months wore on. Short I v
after the neuralgia attacked me. I no-
ticed that my digestion was getting
bad, and so it continued to fail until
eventually my internal organs were so
completely upset that even the lightest
of food could not be assimilated. A
heavy sensation in the chest, which
made it impossible to draw a deep
breath, was one of my daily discom-
forts, and after eating a little mv
stomach became inflated, and caused
considerable discomfort until the wind
was all belched away. My tongue had
a dirty coating, and the taste in mv
mouth of a morning w’as most dis-
agreeable—clearly indicating the bi-
lious state I was in; and another
thing that troubled me was my sight
—very poor indeed sometimes, while
my eyes felt twice their proper size,
and as heavy as lead. Between my
shoulders I had more pains to bear,
and I ached so much across the loins
that it was painful to walk even a
short distance. But you’ve no idea of
the fearful state of nervousness I got
into —used to give little jumps and
twdtches, as though I had St. Vitus’
dance sometimes, and. the unsettled
frame of mind I was in gave rise to
much, mental misery also. Simple mat-
ters irritated me, and caused unduo
excitement, and 1 tell you candidly 1
couldn t find a moment's peace or hap-
piness anyhow.”
After so much ineffective treatment
bow did you manage to get well
again?'’
‘‘ibis way : A new customer came to
the shop one day, and, seeing how ill
1 was, said ‘Did you ever try Clements
Tonic V 1 said : ‘.No ; and I’m sick of
taking physics.’ He came in again, and
asked if I had got any yet, to which
1 answered in the negative. He said :
‘Well, try a bottle; it won’t break
you/ and at last 1 did, for which I
am devoutly thankful. Oh ! it was like
something you read about in a novel,
the way Clements Tonic served mo;
but it was a joyful reality neverthe-
less. To think that I had suffered for
years, and only then had got a re-
medy to do me good was something
to be thankful for, and 1 was, too.
Why, Clements Tonic began to sootho
my nerves when I had only taken two
or three bottles, and you couldn’t cre-
dit the amount of ease it had given me
already ; it roused the weakened ener-
gies of my system l and stimulated my
appetite to such a degree that before
long I was eating as well as ever—
when the neuralgia had entirely gone, of
course you can guess how grateful 1 was.
It is also in regard to indigestion that
Clements Tome is deserving of recogni-
tion at my hands, for that was, after
all, one of the principal things from
which I suffered. Yes ; my last remedy
fairly opened my eyes as to what me-
dicine really can do when it is of the
right kind, for in spite of everything
else failing, Clements Tonic set to
work, and put me right in no time. A
few weeks’ use of it made such an al-
tered man of me that T had nearly
forgotten what it was like_ to get. up
feeling half-dead with weariness. My
breath was sweet, and I had no un-
pleasant. taste in my mouth of a
morning, my eyes were bright and the
sight good, I slept soundly each-
night, every pain had gone, so what
more, could any man wish for ?”
“And what has your health been
like since V”
“Splendid, excepting on an occasion
when I contracted influenza through
getting up all hours at night during
mv wife’s illness. She was suffering
from influenza, too, and soon there
was a pair of us, so we had to close
shop for a while. My word, we did
have a bad time ! Wife got like a bag
of bones, but. Clements Tonic made her
eat four or five meals a day, and she
was soon well again l . I was awfully
weak and miserable ; mv nerves broke
down- again, and once more I suffered
the tortures of neuralgia; but not for
long though, as I knew what would
cure me, and so it did. Yes, Clements
Tonic showed its wonderful properties
again, and I’ve such belief in its cur-
ing capacities that I recommend its
use to all. More than that, you can
publish my story in any manner that
your mind may suggest.”
STATUTORY DECLARATION.
T, HENRY ALMOND, ot Park Road, .
Merrylands, In the State of New Soutli
Wales. Commonwealth of Australia, do
solemnly and sincerely declare that I have
carefully read the annexed document, consist-
ing- of fiv* folios, and consecutively numbered
from one to five, and that it contains and
is a true and faithful account of my illness
and cure by Clements Tonic, and also con-
tains my full permission to publish in any
way my statements—which I give voluntarily,
without receiving any payment ; and I make
this solemn declaration conscientiously believ-
isg the same to be true, and by virtue pi
the provisions of the Oaths Act, 1900.
Subscribed and declared at Merrylands this
twenty-third day of September, one thousand
nine hundred and four, before me,
GEORGE J. FINLAYSON, J.P.

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Sweetest Eyes.
Grey Eyes ’neath a veil! did she know that I
watched her;
I lonely, she lovely, the ship faring restward ?
Grey Eyes, grey-veiled, pensively set on the
distance,
To mark the sea splendor that purpled to west-
ward.
Grey Eyes doubly-veiled, you had tears in your
vision—
A vague, other-world look that troubled the being ;
A shadow that hinted of grief ages-ancient,
Perchance a God’s sorrow set there for man’s
seeing.
The dark night descended, the stars lit the
heavens,
The ship drew to shore and the years came to
sever;
I heard the world’s war-cry, I sought the world’s
battle.
Grey Eyes, ah Grey Eyes, you will grieve for me
ever !
Brown Eyes ! well she knew in her heart that I
watched her.
Brown Eyes! madly merry and flooded with
laughter.
O, nymph of the past, do you mind the green
wood-place,
The chase, and the plea, and the kiss coming after ?
Brown Eyes! you were wicked and fickle and
shallow,
Else needless those rose leaves where rose lips
had wooed me.
Your lances left scars —lances launched at me
fleeing,
While you coming after, noose-flinging, pursued
me.
O huntress, heart-hunting, you showed me your
trophies.
Hearts red, and so many a huntress had never !
My soul scowled its scorn, and I left, but I
hunger:
Brown Eyes, oh Brown Eyes, you will tease mo
for ever!
Blue Eyes ! how she blushed and grew pale as I
watched her;
Truth’s windows, where Truth sat with Love at
his shoulder,
And Thoughts paused to look out, quaint Thoughts
bearing lilies,
Or Lights that lit sister-lights in the beholder.
Blue Eyes, you remember the winds in the garden
That set a torn bloom on a laughing girl's bosom ;
You know not, you guiltless, that other winds
famished
To set ’neath the world’s feet a daintier blossom.
I conquered, proud victor, but thorns for the
victor!
Where gain spells such loss, is it wise to endeavor ?
I know not, I spared you, I sit in the ashes !
Blue Eyes, ah Blue Eyes, I will love you for ever !
N.S.W.,
RODERICK QUINN.
Within Four Walls.
Far off the flaring crimsons of the morn
Light up the eastern sky ;
Near by the ghostly mists trail all forlorn,
The creek’s brown water softly sings and calls.
The world awakes to light and life, but I
Must watch the dreary days go drifting by
Within four walls.
Across the yellow plains that stretch in haze,
Dimly toward the range,
The heat-rays dance and quiver in noon’s blaze,
No happy bird or busy insect calls,
Serene the silence, wonderful and strange,
While I must lie, with never hope of change.
Within four walls.
Fade the last beams, the blue hills far away
Lights from the sunset borrow ;
Deep fall the shadows of the dying day,
The starry night in dusky purple falls.
What hope of change from nights and days of
sorrow—
“ To-morrow, and to-morrow, and to-morrow ”
Within four walls?
vic.
KATHLEEN WALKER.
The Bell-Rock.
Mine eyes are dry and old,
Fire-kisses have died away;
The swan of youth hath sung his song,
The world and the river are grey;
Over the brow of the dim blue hills
The marble palaces stand.
Or was it in dreams I passed along ?
Alas, for my song ! I cannot say;
I know the world and the river are grey,
And the journey hath been long.
My voice was a silver bell
When the princess came to me ;
I feel I answered the old nurse well
As she held me on her knee ;
Now I float by the Corsair rock,
And the tide begins to flow;
Soon now will the last wave swing the clock,
Alas ! for the silver bell ’twould mock,
With its music soft and low.
The journey it hath been long,
And the sunsets swim and play :
Through the swoon of the far-off shadows come
The dreams of another day.
Yes, better to pass along,
Nor the old world seek to stay ;
There’s a charm in the call of the tide-swung
gong,
Though the palaces stand in their marble throng,
And the world and the river are grey.
vie.

THE WHITE HALF PAGE.
An advertising writer knocked
Upon the pearly gate,
St. Peter came, and, looking, asked
His business, age and weight.
When he knew him for a writer
He sadly shook his poll,
And said, “ Alas 1 you cannot pass,
My friend, the Heavenly toll.”
The writer gently smiled and said,
“ Peter, old boy, I’ve heard
A little yarn they spin of you
And a certain Barnyard Bird—
Surely to write advertisements
Can be no greater sin... ? ”
“ Forget it," snarled the worried saint—
“ Forget it, and come in.”
Voice of the Sphinx.
NAILS AND TIN CATS
This space has, for over two years, been devoted
to extolling the merits of a certain roofing. If the
following pars, give that particular brand of
roofing a free advertisement they may, like the
sailor caught praying, excuse themselves on the
plea that it occurs so seldom.
The columns of The Bulletin have been used
for advertising the merits of all kinds of roofings,
except stringy bark, the boilers at Circular Quay,
and the rocks of the Domain, but for downright
cussedness the following takes the cake.
THE TRAGEDY OF THE GREAT
In a narrow street in the city of Cologne,
within a stone’s throw of its magnificent cathe-
dral, stands a mean, low weather-beaten house—
a, land-mark of history, romance and sorrow, for
in that place one of the world’s most famous
women, Maria de Medici, died in abandonment
and misery. Driven out of France by her son,
she became a fugitive and a vagabond. England,
Germany, and Switzerland drove her out. She
wandered to Cologne, and there, in a wretched,
leaky tenement, starved and neglected, she per-
ished—the wife, mother and daughter of a King.
What would she not have given in her last ex-
tremity for the rpof to have been covered with
Ruberoid?
Pick this flower, and the garden is turned into a
howling wilderness. A big saw-mill proprietor
Somewhere between North Head and Bourke
“ did not want any imitations of Rub-beroid, not
even Roo-beroid,” but when it was pointed out to
him that his mill was already roofed with Ruberoid,
he not only apologised, but bought four more rolls.
The man who sold him the Ruberoid called it
Rub-beroid, and he was so scared of the imitator
that he thought Ruberoid was an imitation of
Rub-beroid.
&
Here is another, and the man who wrote it
should have his Little Mary encircled with
Anchor Fence, and be compelled to wear a Giant
Paper chest-protector to keep the damp out of his
lungs :
“ National Park is one of the beauty spots of New South
Wales. Standing at the massive gates of that huge
reserve, one beholds a scene of great magnificence. But
it is not alone the view that repays the visitor ; the des-
cent to the valley is fraught with the profoundest interest.
The road curves round the hill-side, and at every turn one
gets a glimpse of a beautiful stream winding its silvery
way through verdure to be found only in this evergreen
land. National Park is a place to dream one’s life away,
for, apart from the peacefulness, the magnificence of the
scenery, the hills
‘ Bathed in the tenderest purple of the distance,
Tinted and shadowed by pencils of air,’
the six picturesque shelter-houses, all roofed with
Ruberoid, offer a resting place such as can only be offered
by a shelter-house roofed with Ruberoid."

When the earth is advertised over,
And postered from zone to zone,
When there isn’t another inch of room
The agent may call his own ;
When the roofs are covered with “ Ruberoid,”
And the land sprouts “ Anchor Pence,”
The walls all lined with “ Giant Paper,”
And the farmer is saving his pence,
It is then that the advertising agent
Will feel most awfully mean
Because he omitted to take this space
To advertise “ Ruberine."
Voice of the Sphinx.
P OOR RIIjTj
It is not often that a man is unlucky enough to
have both legs broken through shaking dice, and
yet that is what happened to poor Bill. His other
name is known to all our readers, but, thank
heaven, his face has escaped their memories. Both
legs had to be amputated at the knee, and, as the
result of a “ generously-responded-to ” subscrip-
tion list, he was presented with a pair of wooden
legs. Last week Bill wrote to E. Rich and Co.,
Ltd., stating that he was free from corns, bunions,
and chilblains, but would like a tin of Avenarius
to keep the white ants out of his stumps.
&
Ah, well, we can only say with Jerrold
** u^_i es t trades have their moments of
pleasure. Now, if I were a gravedigger, or even a
with?t”’ f h H re w S0 ? le pe °P le 1 eould work for
with a great deal of enjoyment/*
Here he goes again—just a bit higher in price:—
“Coupons are not given with Avenarius.
But we know a woman up in the Northern Terri-
tory who Was choosing a coffin to bury her dead
husband. She looked at two kinds, one was priced
£3, the other £4, Now the £3 coffin was just the
required length for the late lamented, while the
£4 one was a trifle short. She decided on the £4
casket, and when asked for a reason stated that
she was collecting coupons, and required just £4
worth to fill her book.”
We don’t see anything very funny in that
story. In fact, we don’t believe the writer. Had
he said straight out that Avenarius would
not only protect even coffins being destroyed by
white ants we would have believed him. For we
know that white ants and Avenarius can’t
occupy the same paddock.
If you prefer your steak rare, just try this little
bit of underdone :
“ The streets of Heaven may be paved with
gold, but the floors of the Public Works Depart-
ment are covered with Ruberoid floorcloth, and
countless millions of deputations have failed to
make an impression on it.”
The floor of Paddy’s Market is just as likely to be
inlaid with liquid diamonds as the streets of
Heaven to be paved with gold.
#
Here’s another hors <Voeuvre that is not alto-
gether out of place :
“ You cannot make a good roofing out of an inferior
imitation. If it is not good, take it not: if;it is not
Ruberoid, use it not.” — Muldoon’s Wife.
We have heard of Goodge, Brady, Lawson and
Tennyson, and have seen a few of Souter’s cats,
but never before have we heard of Muldoon’s
Wife.
Here’s a cut to square leg off Bill Shakspeare’s
bat:—
Doubt that the stars are fire,
That vacuum’s a void;
Doubt Truth to be a liar,
But .don’t doubt Ruberoid.
That tit-bit is in a class by itself, but we will
refrain from saying what class it is in. We believe
Bacon fielded the ball well, and quickly returned
it to Oliver Cromwell, who was keeping wickets,
otherwise old Bill and John Knox would probably
be running yet.
And yet, wherever one goes, Potts Point with
its mansions, Mosman’s Bay with its pretty villas,
or the humble weather-board cottage away in the
heart of Australia, the engine houses at Singleton <
Newcastle, Hamilton, Goulburn, Murrurundi, the
huge factory buildings in Sydney, the same old i
Ruberoid meets and gives you welcome. The best;
is not too good for the rich, consequently they will i
have Ruberoid on the Roof or Ruberoid floorcloth!
on the floor. The man in the bush very often has;
Ruberoid not only on the roof and floor but has;
tanks made from it, silos covered with it, sheds;
built entirely of it, dams lined with it and his boots
soled with it. The Railway Commissioners select
it because no other roofing will stand like Ruberoid.
How’s this for a pearl of great price :
Notwithstanding the fact that over a hundred shearers
were seated in the woolshed, the silence was terrible.
Just then Ringer Joe stepped forward and played the
banjo with one hand, the mouth-organ with the other,
and the cornet with his feet. The silence was broken
with deafening shouts of applause which resounded and
re-echoed beneath the Ruberoid roof.
What an imagination for an advertisement-
writer !
OUR JOKES
The writer of the advertisements for that well-
known roofing, Ruberoid, not only objects to
Bulletin readers writing to Messrs. E. Rich &
Co., Ltd., for an explanation of his jokes, but is
of the opinion that if the reader does not see any-
thing funny in what comes out of his boil, and
writes asking where the humor escapes, it simply
shows lack of appreciation on the part of the
reader, and incidentally causes the writer to
swear.
The last stroke makes the horseshoe :
Lost to shame is that woman who takes no pride in her-
reputation, who does not value her good name ; but sunk
to the lowest depths of degradation is the builder who
neglects to use Ruberoid Dampcourse.
He must have taken a trip to the new railway
station, and pondered over the block of ages.
There is evidently but one dampcourse, and that
one Ruberoid. Now, will you be good ?
&
Anybody would think that Ruberoid was the •
only roofing worthy of the name, and yet, when
you come to think of it a second time, it certainly
is the best roofing for Australia : cool in summer
—warm in winter —any handy man can lay it —
cheap, but durable —and the man who wants more
for his money should go and get his bumps felt.
At least, that is what we think.

ing articles for the Sydney Mokning Herald, .
but nevertheless each carries a moral— ,
Avoid an “oid ” that is not Ruberoid.

THURSDAY, JUNE 22, 1905.
Prior 6d.
THE BENEFACTOR.
In the course of an address on Socialism to a meeting of the Women’s Liberal League, at North Sydney, the other evening, Mrs. Osborne said that there were many different
kinds of Socialism but they were all alike, inasmuch as the object was to repress individuality and to increase the power of the State. The lecturer quoted Milton’s line: “To
disenthrone the King of Heaven, we war, if war we must,” in support of the theory that Satan was the first Socialist.— Newspaper Item.

Correspondents to The Bulletin would oblige the paper
(and, to an extent, themselves) by taking some care as to
methods of address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions, advertise-
ments, etc.) should in no case be addressed to the Editor,
but to the Manager. To send them to the Editor is to
invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters to
anyone by name, “ The Editor, Bulletin,” is the proper
superscription for “ copy.” Delay, and even loss, is
sometimes caused by addressing to persons instead of
officials. The person may not be there; the official
always is.

THURSDAY, JUNE 22, 1905.
THE FEDERAL LABOR PARTY AND
THE STINKING FISH PARTY.
When the next all-Australian Labor conference
gathers together, it will find awaiting discussion a
question which is not on the programme, but
which, none the less, will rise up like a butchered
citizen’s ghost, and enforce attention. That
question is : “ How far has the Labor party been a
political failure?” It cannot be denied that a
very large section of the outside world does regard
Australian Labor-in-polities as a failure—a deadly
and, at the same time, a ridiculous failure —a
failure so vast and so obvious as to be a jest for the
human race at large. Thanks to the Stinking
Fish party in Australia—that bitterly Tory and
mostly Freetrade organisation which supplies the
globe in general with Ananias and Sapphika
yarns re Australian affairs —it is widely believed
that there is nothing wicked, childish, or
suicidal that it is in the power of any political
party to do which the Labor party hasn’t done,
or isn’t just going co do. The writer knew a
great deal of this in times past, from close study
of British, South African, and other papers, but
it wasn’t until he took a recent holiday trip in
Southern Asia that he realised into what un-
expected places the Stinking Fish organisation
had spread. Then he found that Australia and
its Labor party—the latter being generally sup-
posed to run Australia, and to be responsible for
all the things that have happened, and very many
that haven’t—were more or less known, and
always known unfavorably, on the borders of
Afghanistan, on the edge of Cashmere, and on the
southern frontier of China. Even the smallest,
maddest rag published in all that area seemed to
have, at some time or other, .been supplied with
a profusion of Australian Stinking Fish literature,
and to have been misinformed about the Petriana
and the Six Hatters. It appears to be time for the
Federal Labor party to come out of its shell and
hit back.
* * * *
The only possible way to refute the charge of
failure is to succeed. Success covers everything,
while failure is the Stinking Fish party’s oppor-
tunity. And the world has a very simple measure
—and, on the whole, a reasonably true measure—
of what constitutes success. If what is commonly
called a “new” country can show that its indus-
tries are growing fast, that it can supply abun-
dance of employment for its own people, and that
its policy has made it so good a country that it is
rushed by desirable immigrants and can find work
for these immigrants, then the world respects it,
and no Stinking Fish party can destroy that
respect. If it can’t show these things, then the
world sets it down as a failure, and is ready and
willing to believe almost any slander that George
Reid, or William M‘Millan, or Bruce Smith, or
George Pile, “land speculator, of Balmain,”
may utter concerning it. Canada and the United
States have more rigid laws against the importa-
tion of contract labor than Australia has, and
they enforce these laws, which the Commonwealth
doesn't; yet while the Six Hatters, whom Aus-
tralia didn't exclude after all, have become a
bye-word and a jest and an insult, no one
points the finger of scorn or condemnation
at the exclusions actually carried out by
Canada and the United States. The United
States, it is reckoned, will, despite whole-
sale shutting-out of undesirables, attract a million
immigrants this year, and Canada about 200,000 ;
and their growing industries hold out a reasonable
prospect of work for all these comers. When
their policy is called in question by old-world
critics, the lofty retort of these countries is : “If
our methods make our land so desirable as this, no
poor, wretched, worn-out old country like yours,
that can’t find work for its own people and has to
get rid of them by export, has any right to
open his mouth. Go to; you are a Has-Been ! ”
And the answer is so overwhelming in its rude
vigor that the critic usually slinks into the nearest
public-house, and is silent.
* * * *
It is much the same with Maoriland. That
country has been under Labor rule for about 14
years, and has done practically everything that
the Australian Labor party has managed to do,
even in the State where the Australian Labor
party has made most progress. Also it has
done many other things that Labor.-in-politics has
hardly attempted yet on this side. Yet it is
rather regarded as a bright and shining light than
as a fearful example and a very bad joke. It has
gone in for Arbitration laws, Old Age Pension
laws, Workmen’s Compensation law's, preference
to unionists, immigration restriction, State fire,
life, and accident insurance, State banking, a
Public Trust office, State coal mines, and other
things, many of which are as yet only wild social-
istic dreams this end of the Tasman Sea. It has
done these things and commanded the world’s
respect, while Australia has hardly got beyond
proposing to do them, yet already almost every
paper on earth that takes any note of Australian
affairs reckons this country to be a dangerous
lunatic. The mere mention of graduated land-tax,
absentee-tax, and compulsory resumption of large
estates for closer settlement is, to much of the outer
world, a proof that Australia is a crazed com-
munity ; the actual existence of these things in
Maoriland excites scarcely an adverse comment.
The fact that a Labor Government with a
Labor majority may arrive in Australia is
seriously and honestly regarded in many quarters
as the impending climax of all calamity; yet
the fact that such a Government has actually
existed in Maoriland for 14 years causes not one
Jeremiah to turn a hair. The difference is that
between success and failure as the world, not
unreasonably, judges these things. The six
Commonwealth States have, in the last six years
for which statistics are available (1898-1903), sent
away 8582 more persons than they received ; their
net immigration has not only been nil, but less
than nil. Maoriland, though six times more
crowded than the Commonwealth, has gained 29,799
people by excess of immigration, and barren as
Australia is in many regions, its barrenness isn’t
sufficient to account for this difference. In 1903,
the last year for which full statistics are available,
the Commonwealth lost 7249 people by excess of
departures; Maoriland, already six times as
densely peopled, gained 11,275 by excess of arrivals.
Maoriland has flaunted a big surplus every year
for about a dozen years; the federated States,
taken in the aggregate, have shown six deficits in
10 years. Betw'een 1895 and 1903 the number of
hands employed in Maoriland factories increased
by over 92 percent.; those of the Commonwealth
States by only 47 per cent., and in 1903 the
Commonwealth manufactures were actually dimin-
ishing while those of Maoriland were still' on the
up-grade. The world judges by such evidences
as these; therefore the democratic policy which
makes Maoriland admired as an especially bright
jewel of the empire makes Australia the world’s
butt, the sport of the Stinking Fish party, and a
synonym for failure and incapacity.
* * » *
The. difference does not lie in the democratic
features of the Labor party’s policy, but in its
ridiculous decision to “sink the fiscal issue.”
When it came to that resolve it was in the position
of a ship s captain who resolved to sink the navi-
gation issue. It laid down the rule that it was no
part of its business to find work for the people,
except by occasionally asking Ministers to provide
them with temporary employment at unproduc-
tive public jobs, paid for out of ruinous loans. It
concluded that the question of raising up a great,
prosperous, progressive white community by the
only means that could raise such a communitv
—i.e., by creating self-supporting industries
which would furnish men with employment
*—was not its affair. No “new” country
in the world is rising to-day to wealth and
prominence, and attracting multitudes of the best
class of white immigrants, save under Protection,
but even this palpable evidence didn’t drag the
Labor party away from its fiscal-sinking supersti-
tion. Close to Australia’s door there is the example
of a country which shows that an advanced demo-
cratic programme, with high Protection, means
rapidly growing industries, a great influx of
desirable immigrants, work for those immigrants
to do, and national honor and credit; the Austra-
lian Labor party has gone on fiscal-sinking, and
as a result it has linked the democratic programme
in Australia with declining or stationary indus-
tries, an outflow of population, and general dis-
credit. And just because it, as a party, has no
national and patriotic fiscal policy, the Federal
Labor party remains still a comparatively weak
party—not strong enough to take a firm hold of
the reins of government, not strong enough to
carry its graduated land-tax or absentee-tax, or to
provide land for the people by breaking up the
big estates —not strong enough, even, to secure the
honest administration of the law which is the only
hope of keeping Australia a white man’s country.
* * • *
Australia is now more Freetrade than Protec-
tionist. It has a small tariff on some things and
a stupendous and incredible free list. For this
the Labor party is so far responsible that it could
have prevented the evil and didn’t. As a party it
was passive. But not being a Freetrade party—
only a fiscal-sinking party—it hasn’t even the
satisfaction of feeling that it won a victory when
the present tariff came into existence. By its
fiscal-sinkmg policy it gratified and enriched
M Millan, of N.S. W., who repays it with slander
and abuse. It further bloated the bank account
of Cameron, of Tasmania, who turned it out of
°L, ® b J r wny °f repayment. It did the dirty work
of Lruce Smith and Joe Cook, whose command
or language in its denunciation is something too
vast to be described in the space here available.
Above all things, it furnished material for the
members of the Stinking Fish party, whose
slanders upon Australia are a discredit to their
color and their race. Under the pitiable semi-
Freetrade Federal tariff Australian industries are
diminishing or stationary, and the Stinking Fish
press and the Fish news correspondents—an
organisation far too wide to be coped with by the
agencies at the Labor party’s command—explain
to a listening and astonished globe how “revolu-
tionary Labor legislation ”is the cause. For want
of work, people are leaving Australia—it is proba-
bly the only “new” country on earth which
is in that position. They are leaving because
factories and workshops have been closing up
through the operation of the new tariff, and
thereby depriving the workers, who are the
backbone of the Labor organisation, of employ-
ment and bread ; and that closing up makes more
room and profit for the importer, and furnishes
him with money to support the Stinking Fish
propaganda. And the Stinking Fish party repays
this benefit by explaining to the world that
population is fleeing from Australia to escape
“the tyranny of the unions’’and things of that
kind. The deficits, the big loans, the unpro-
ductive works—in the bright lexicon of Stinking
Fish the Labor party is responsible for them all.
The remedy for all this is to make Australia a
country that has no deficits and which doesn’t need
loans, a country with rapidly growing industries,
and with a constant influx of desirable
white immigrants, and with abundance of work
for these immigrants to do, and to make these
results the accompaniment of a vigorous Demo-
cratic policy. But that can only be done
by substituting local industries for imports,
and if the experience of Canada, the United States
and Maoriland goes for anything, the only road to
that consummation is by High Protection. At all
events, the fiscal-sinkers have found no other road.
Of course, there are serious matters to. be con-
sidered before the Federal Labor' party can take
any such step as the one here proposed. It is
cordially advised by M'Millan not to. become a
party of High Protection ; and George Reid,
Joseph Cook, Bruce Smith,, Pumpkin Smith,
Cameron of Tasmania, W ilks, Conroy, the
Argus, the Sydney Telegraph, and other dis-
interested frienas tender it; the same counsel. So
much disinterested advice deserves consideration.
If the party is of opinion that these people and
these papers are reliable well-wishers it will go on
fiscal-sinking. But, if it fancies that these people
are not its friends,, and that they want it to
remain what it is—a comparative failure and a
butt for the Stinking Fish organisation—it will
fiscal-sink no longer.
* *, * *
Even to supply Australia’s present population,
there is something like £30,000,000 worth more
goods to be made annually in the country than are
made at present—goods that are now imported,
but which could be made here just as soon as the
necessary works, could be established, if importa-
tion were resolutely checked by High Protection.
To make these goods herd might be an act of un-
kindness to, such as William M‘Millan, a gentle-
man to whom the Labor party, judging by its
fiscal policy, seems to fancy that it owes a stupen-
dous debt of gratitude ; but it would mean supply-
ing good work and good wages to some 200,000
workers and their families, and that is at
least something. It would mean an in-
flux of population that would do a great
deal to hold this continent against an
Asiatic invasion. It would mean that instead of
being surprised to find six hatters at its doors
Australia would be crying aloud for 600 hatters.
It would mean putting an end for a very long
while to any legitimate unemployed, to relief
works, and to the necessity for the N.S.W. State
Labor party to harass itself about the petty pro-
blem of Bill’s “dirt-money.” It would certainly
mean a very great accession of strength to the
Labor party, for it would be an immense building
up of the unionist class which is that party’s'
strength and substance. It would probably inean
a necessity for the resumption of assisted'‘immi-
gration, and, if it did, that would be a point
in its favor. There is no objection to assisted
immigration when the immigrants are sturdy white
citizens of the right class, and provided there is
abundance of work for them to do. Every 1000
capable adult white immigrants who arrive
here and find profitable work to do, shoulder
(they and their families) some £220,000 mf the
burden of our public debt, and start at once
to pay some £12,000 a year in taxes for them-
selves and their families, and to supply (in-
cluding their families) some 4000 new cus-
tomers to the farmers and tradespeople, and
they mean also 1000 more men to carry a rifle
apiece in the day of trouble. If it cost £lO per
head to import such people, it would be money
well spent—so long as there was well-paid work
for them to do on arrival, and the cost of their
importation was defrayed out of revenue. Also
when the Labor party resolved to be the party
which built up great industries, and which
stood like a rock for honest finance and
national solvency—when there were no more
dying manufactures in the land and de-
jected artisans fleeing out of the land to make
a text for the slanders of Stinking Fish—there
would be a great slump in the stock of Labor’s
enemies. William M‘Millan would not be here
then —he would have fled away to import some-
where else. But Bruce Smith would be a target
for the late lamented cat of scorn, and Joseph
Cook for the mud of contumely, when they tried
to explain, in the face of visible prosperity, how
the country was being ruined by Labor legislation ;
and the Sydney Telegraph would by that time
have run out of damaging statistics, and the
Argus would probably have gone off its head.
They would all be empty guns without ammuni-
tion, and Rozhdestvensky’s fleet wouldn’t be a
circumstance by comparison.
* * * *
But meanwhile this is all a vision of the future.
For the present there is nothing for the people to
do but to resolve at next election to give first pre-
ference to the Labor candidate who is definitely a
High Protectionist; to give its second preference
to the High Protectionist who isn’t a Labor man ;
and to let the Labor Freetrader and the other
Freetrader herd together at the tail-end of the
poll.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
A Party that Forgets its Principles.
In the no-confidence debate with which the
N.S.W. Assembly commenced its session, the
Labor party managed to forget a considerable
part of its principles. Questions of the size of
Bill’s dirt-money had a tremendous amount of
attention; but a question of the size of keeping
the country from going further into pawn had no
attention at all. Yet the State Labor party has
made some claim to be regarded as the honest
party in finance. It, anyway, nailed hard and
fast in its platform a plank declaring against
further . borrowing, except to carry out
works which would pay interest and pro-
vide a sinking fund. It professed to be
satisfied that N.S'.W. had over-borrowed—a
pretty easy thing to be satisfied about, seeing
that, even in an unusually “good’’ year, the
State’s public works had earned only £1,778,016-
out of the £3,114,760 called for by way of interest
and charges, leaving a balance of £1,330,744 to be
provided out of revenue. This doesn’t look at all
as if the works paid interest, let alone sinking fund.
The Labor party professed to be satisfied (and,
outside Parliament, anyhow, the Labor party was
satisfied) that the yarn that Cohen’s money was
sunk in permanent and reproductive works was a
hollow lie ; and the fact that in the last two years-
the works didn’t earn interest and charges by over
£2,500,000 proved it to be a lie. The Labor party,
therefore, said that this drift must stop. More
than that, the party outside the House resolved
that the party inside the House should fight
to stop it. It put the matter, not in its
general statement of principles, but in its
fighting platform. And yet, when the leader of
the party brought up a no-confidence motion,
against a Government which has rushed the State
into pawn with the old disastrous rush, and built
works without any regard to whether they will,
pay or not, and has provided no sinking fund on
its new works, and has even cut down the old
sinking fund, the Labor party in the House hadn’t,
a word to say about the matter. It got its-
. instructions, and coolly and calmly ignored them.
Any interference with the sacred rights of J.
Smith in connection with his lease of a western
sand-patch was regarded as a subject for severe:
language ; and the fact that Smithson instead of
Bkownson was appointed boss of a public school,
was viewed as a matter which fully justified the
Deputy-Governor in shedding all the confidence lie
ever had in J. Carruthers ; but the hustling of the
country a further £2,000,000 into debt during the
year was a small matter not worth mentioning. On
Carruthers’ own showing he had borrowed
£1,833,700 in 11 months, which is at the rate of
£2,000,400 a year—a sum much greater than was
spent for many years previous to the Owe’See
financial drunk. The Labor party made no in-
quiry as to-whether this money was being put into -
undertakings that would return interest and:
sinking fund qr not; it knows that the average:
undertaking paid for out of borrowed money
hitherto has only paid about half the interest, let-
alone sinking fund, and there was no evidence-
whatever that the new undertakings were any.
better than the average of the old ones. But,
good or bad, leader McGowen didn’t call upon the -
Premier for any explanation with regard to them—
he treated the drift into another two millions of .
debt as a matter of course, and not a subject for
grievance even if Carruthers had no satisfactory
explanation handy. And yet McGowen was-
hard-up for bricks to throw at Carruthers. If .
he had contented himself with aiming one brick—
the Government’s vile treatment of the Arbitra-
tion Act —silence with regard to the finances would
have been understandable; but he made his
motion general, and was therefore expected to
throw all the bricks he knew of; and he didn’t.
even mention the financial brick, except to remark
that, if Carruthers got a surplus, it would be :
through not keeping certain works going. It
therefore looks as though the N.S.W. Labor Party
doesn’t remember that the first thing for the :
honest party to see to is, not that works are done,.
but that the money is honestly available to pay
for them when they are done. And it also looks ■
as though it doesn’t remember that its “objective”
is “the encouragement of Australian national i
sentiment; ” anyway, it is hard to see how it -
proposes to encourage Australian national senti-
ment by giving Cohen a further mortgage over the
country. Further, as a mere matter of party tac-
tics, the financial brick was a good and handy one
to aim. For Carruthers’ party includes at j leastt
half-a-dozen men who pledged themselves in the;
most definite manner possible to oppose fur-
ther borrowing, and Carruthers has borrowed!
£2,000,000, and therefore made it impossible fort
them to support him any further and still keep-
their pledges, provided their support is challenged
“on that ground. That half-dozen or more of votes
would have about given McGowen a majority;
but he tnrew v away the chance of getting them at
the same time, as he threw away the chance of
showing that a fighting platform is a platform to
be fought for, and not simply a substance to
stand upon and make speeches about at election
time.

Bent’s Boom Finance.
Several years ago, a stout young man named
Bent grew cabbages on several acres of land at
Moorabin (Vic.), and obtained his living by keep-
ing his expenditure within the limits of his
revenue. Later on, Bent thought that money
could be made more quickly by buying and selling
blocks of land, but after making large profits by
this process, he awoke one day to find that his
affluence had disappeared like gas, and he had to
move from his big mansion to a smaller house.
Bent returned to work again, and in another
five years contrived to he comfortably off
once more. Then it came to pass that he was put
in charge of a larger cabbage garden. With
Bent’s experience, it might have been supposed
that he would avoid the errors which had led to
his undoing in his middle age, but this was not
the case. Instead of growing the cabbages, and
living within the margin of profit that the garden
returned, Bent found a second time that more
money could be made by booming. Accordingly,
he fenced off a strip at the end of the cabbage
garden and advertised it for sale. The proceeds
amounted to something handsome, and Bent
waved his hat and cheered vociferously. The
people spent the money thus liberally handed out
to them, and waved their hats, and cheered also.
ThomAs Bent straightway fenced off another strip >
and sold it. This deal was even more successful
than the last, and every year he continued selling
pieces of the cabbage garden until the time came
to sell the last piece, when he had himself to move
into the house of a benevolent neighbor because;
there was no other place for him. Then he told:
the people who employed him that there was no >
more money, and when they suggested that he:
might with advantage be lynched, he was insulted,
and tendered his resignation. This is a parable,.
but a correct one. Even in his most scintillating
moments of land boom Bent’s finance was not
more short-sighted than it is to-day. His most
glorious method of raising revenue is still the-
old method of selling off the assets. Already he;
has done considerable things in this respect, but;
now he is going a step further, and proposes to
dispose of ail the country commons and the edu-
cational endowment. The commons, certainly,,
unless they are better managed, might almost
as well be disposed of. The stock grazed,
upon them very often belongs to rich pas-
toralists well able to pay for agistment, and,,
where action has been taken against these illegal
occupiers, the dummying of stock has been ram-
pant. But the commons should certainly not be
sold, at any rate by auction. As State property,.
they should be leased, but' if Parliament is deter-
mined to alienate them, there is no valid reason
why they should not be added to the areas
at the disposal of the Closer Settlement


Board Regarding the proposed sale of
vnT area of metropolitan land forming
the* educational endowment, such an aet
would be nothing short of criminal. Years a o,
Parliament, in a lucid interval, decided to provide
for the maintenance of the educational system by
retaining two large areas, one between the town
of Port Melbourne and the River Yarra, and the
other between West Melbourne and F ootscray. No
use has hitherto been made of this land, partly
because the population has not expanded
as it should, partly because manufacturing
industries have not developed as they should,
and partly because influential land boomers
didn’t want the property developed, lest
it should compete with the properties they
themselves were putting on the market. 1 ie
result is that the land is of little immediate
value ; but, when Melbourne’s population doubles
it should be worth from £5,000,000 to £10,000,000.
It is close to the city ; its nearest point is not a
i mile and a quarter from the General Post Office,
rand its furthest point not three miles distant. It
is dry land, and with an insignificantexpenditureon
improvements and communication, might Vie turned
i into two new suburbs, all State-owned. At the
present time, it yields no interest, and as it will
i he sold on the basis of its capitalised earnings, i.
Bent will probably raise from this magnificent
asset fust enough to pay the expenses of the
next Vampire Day. Then he will have the
satisfaction of seeing some syndicate cut up the
; land, make streets, build houses, and with a,
i motor or tram service to the city, reap a revenue
which would probably pay the whole annual cost
of the Victorian Education Department, and a
> little over. Like many other statesmen, Thomas
Bent is bitten with the microbe of selling Ins
; assets and making merry with the proceeds, just
: as the shearer sells his horse at cat’s-meat price at
: the tail-end of his great drunk, and has one more
■ “ horror ” with the money.

(Giving the Children’s Bread to the Animal
Disrespectfully Mentioned in Scripture.
Probably the saddest contribution of all to the
■discussion about Australia’s want of population
\was a recent column-long letter to S.M. Herald
by Dr. Arthur, M.L.A. That gentleman pro-
poses a wholesale importation of immigrants,
.whose passages are to be paid with loan money.
To use his own words, he is of opinion that “ a
ilarge proportion of the loan expenditure of the
future should be devoted to this scheme of State-
raided immigration.’’ This is indeed a painful
.case of the animal which Hazael of Syria said
that he wasn’t (at least he asked indignantly if he
was) returning to the offensive substance that
ought not to be left lying about. Dr. Arthur
lent his countenance at last State election to the
anti-borrowing party, and now he has so com-
pleteiy forgotten all about it that he takes
future loan expenditure as a matter of course,
and he already begins to portion out the
proceeds of the I DU. When the money
has been‘borrowed, Dr. Arthur wants to rake
Great Britain, Ireland, Finland, Scandinavia,
Germany and Switzerland for agriculturists, bring
them out to N.S.W., give them land free, release
them from all land-tax for three or five years, and
possibly—though he only hints at this lend them
money in addition. The yearning to import
things sticks out here in a regrettable fashion.
About five years out of every ten N.S.W. sends
away more people than it receives, and a good part
of the loss arises because the State doesn’t supply
land for its own growing country population. It
has no graduated land-tax to break up big estates,
■ and no effective system of resumption for closer
settlement. Its good coastal country is blocked
iby big uncultivated holdings, and what isn t
'blocked is mostly inaccessible for want of roads.
When the State offers to sell at £1 or more per
acre —not to give away—a decent bit of accessible
land, without paying the prospective farmer’s
passage to the spot, and without any exemption
from land-tax, it gets applicants by the hundred.
Onlv a few days ago a N.S.W. citizen complained
in the Sydney papers that he had spent £ldo
attending at places where there were scraps ol
respectable soil available, and each time the
names of the multitude of applicants were put m
a hat—and he had never the luck to be the man
whose name emerged from that receptacle. He
didn’t get enough land to bury a dog m, and the
hat became to him a pot of doom. Ihe only
place where the N.S.W. settler has any reasonable
certainty of getting a patch of Government land
nowadays, even when he pays a reasonable price
for it, is out on the fringe of Sheol—or further out,
in Sheol itself. If N.S.W. would set to work in
dead earnest with a heavy graduated land-tax and
a vigorous resumption policy —paying for the
resumed land out of the proceeds of.the graduated
lanu-tax —to make available all the reasonably
good coastal lands, and would open up Government
lands with roads and railways paid for out ot
revenue, in addition to compelling the opening up
•of private estates, and thus make room for
its own people who are ready to pay for the soil,
and who ask for neither pauper passage-tickets
nor tax exemptions, it is quite possible that it would
get new settlers by thousands and thousands. Ihe
•experiment has never been tried, and no one knows
how the settlers might pour in. But Dr. Arthur,
M.L.A., makes no suggestion about giving our
own people a show firsthand giving the second
show to the person who might, if only he knew he
could get land, be willing to come here at his own
expense and pay his way. The doctor’s great in-
spiration is to borrow a devil of a lot of money
wherewith to import foreign deadbeats; then to
sacrifice the much-needed land revenue by giving
away the land; to sacrifice much-needed tax-
revenue by exempting the foreign deadbeat from
the imposts that the native Australian has to pay ,
and finally, if it can be done, he hints at lending
the foreign deadbeat actual cash to start with. As
for giving away land to the Cornstalk citizen who
is now driven out in search of elbow-roorn else-
where, or even supplying him with good land at a
reasonable price, that isn’t so much as hinted at.
The craze for importing something swallows up all
other considerations. Yet Dr. Arthur represents
quite a large class. At a time when the local in-
habitant is still praying for access to a decent
scrap of soil the State is full of loud people who
want to know, in a voice like that of the itinerant
bottle-person, why the State doesn’t give land
away to all comers, after the fashion of Canada.
The One Blind Tourist and the Six Hatters.
Last Monday Thomas Bent in Melbourne, and
the Herald in Sydney, both broke out on the
subject of the necessity for attracting immigrants,
and both set to work to clear themselves and their
own policy of all responsibility in the matter by
babbling about small side issues. There was not
one word in the Herald’s eloquent leading article
about the fact that immigrant workers in iron,
steel, brass, copper, silk, cotton, woollen, lmen,
and many other things, won’t come to N.S.W.
because there is no work for them at their own
trades, practically all the goods they make being
-imported. And Bent said not a word about
the fact that his own State, with its miserably low
Protection, was, and long had been, in much the
same position. Also, neither said anything about
the fact that agriculturists are clearing out instead
of coming in, because the best lands are inaccess-
ible, or locked up by private monopolists against
settlement. Instead, the S.M. Herald did this
little wail:—
We will persist in giving wide publicity to unsavory
advertisements of our exclusiveness, as, for_example, our
unseemly treatrfient of the blind tourist, when we really
at heart are a hospitable people. We desire the company
tof our friends, but we keep an ugly-looking bulldog
chained up near the gate, and Chen express surprise that
our visitors are so rare.
And Bent did this large roar: —
People talked about inducing population to come
here. What was the use of offering inducements when
such things as the six hatters business was possible/
General Booth, instead of having gone to Mr. Reid with
his immigration scheme, should have come to him (Bent).
He hoped to learn how far the General would help the Vic-
torian Government to settle people on the land.
In the red-herring doctrine of Bent in Victoria
and Reid and the Herald in N.S.W., the Six
Hatters and the one blind tourist (all seven of
whom were admitted) is a full and complete ex-
planation of everything. And, in reply, I HR
Bulletin presents these figures from Coghlans

and asks an explanation :
