Bent and Ruin and the S.M. Herald and
Sydney Telegraph and Melbourne Argus hold
that the disease, trouble, anguish and dry-rot
all lie in the third column. All the thousands
•of people who voluntarily went out of the two debt-
laden States, with their land-monopoly, their huge
imports, and their few native industries, don t
matter; and the entire damage lies in the fact
that, after ten years of this disastrous exit, seven
people had some questions put to then! as they
came ashore. The Roman emperor who was
worried only about the drooping aspect of his
favorite chooky-hen when the Goths were besieg-
ing his capital was a wise naan by comparison
with Bent and the S.M. Herald..
Minister Swinburne has Hopes.
Minister Swinburne, of Victoria, probably the
most creditable member of the Government that
isn’t Straight, intends to make another effort
next session to urge his Water Bill through Par-
liament. The story of the causes which led up to
this Bill, if any wealthy Australian Tory of the
Stinking Fish party told it in London, would
hardly be believed ; and when it was believed
the probabilities are that London would refuse to
look at any more Victorian loans until the House
of 34 Rich Landlords was abolished. It would
reckon that its money wasn’t safe while that
extraordinary institution was above ground. Vic-
toria has spent several millions of loan money on
irrigation works, and by this means the value of
land in many districts has been increased four
and five fold. The landowners approved most
cordially of the expenditure and the increased
land values, and when the works were handed
over to local Trusts they cheerfully undertook to
keep them in order, and pay interest and a small
sinking fund on their cost. Then they proceeded
almost unanimously to swindle the State by a
process of falsifying the accounts which, if there
were the same law for the dishonest landowner as
for the dishonest clerk, would have landed many
of them in gaol. By carefully putting down in the
rate books as worth, say, £5 an acre land which
was easily saleable at about £25, and by levying
the rate on the false valuation, the swindlers of
the Trusts started out to show that they couldn t
raise enough to pay their liabilities. They de-
faulted wholesale, like any other bankrupts, on
their interest payments and their sinking fund
payments, and in course of time they persuaded
the State to write off the great bulk of the arrears.
Also they persuaded the State to write off a
huge proportion of the original cost of the works,
and then promised solemnly, on their honor
as spielers and three-card men, to pay up
re<mlarlv the interest and sinking fund on the
small liability left to them. Of course, they
promptly broke this promise also ; the promises of
the Victorian landowner are, on the whole, a shade
worse than the oath and covenant of the pony-
racing shark. Last of all, the State made the un-
pleasant discovery that even maintenance wasn t
being paid for. The works which represent
millions of Victoria's hard-borrowed money, and
which have been bestowed on its land-owning
class almost as a gift, are going to pieces—are be-
coming leaky, rotten, silted-up, and superannu-
ated. The irrigation system, in fact, is dying out
again The landowners, of course, do not intend
that it shall really die out, for the loss to them
would be too vast; but they evidently reckon that,
when things come to the crisis, the State will
rather pay for repairs than see its great and costly
scheme collapse, and already the demand that
the State shall pay for repairs is being heard.
The Financial Reform party—the wealthy land-
owners and defaulters of Kyabram and elsewhere—
at times almost live on the Treasurers doorstep
demanding that the working classes shad be taxed
to supply them with free water. Minister Swin-
burne finally revolted at this imposition, and
drafted a Bill providing .that the State was to
resume control of its own water-works—the works
it had paid for - and keep them m repair, and
charge the people who use the water sufficient to
cover the expense. The Assembly passed the
Bill but the Upper House was staggered at the
idea of the class which it represents, and to which
its members belong, being asked to even party
■pay its way, and the Rich Landlords threw the
measure out' with contumely and mud. SWlN-
burne is going to try next session, xiis
chance is probably not worth much, but at least
there is nothing like perseverance.
Something Thai Might Have Been Better.
Butler, Jenkins’ mournful successor in S. Aus-
tralia has resolved to hang on to office till the
House meets, in a month’s time, and thereafter as
long' as it will let him. He has practically no
following, and his Attorney-General was beaten
at tlio elections ; but he knows that nobody else
has an absolute majority either, and so he stays in
the hope that he may pull through ]ust as well as
the other people who haven’t a majority. Brice s
nartv is largest ; but even Price stands at the
head of a partv which can only hold office by the
grace of some other party which he was recently
fighting and which he must be prepared to fight
again presently. There is, of course, better reason
why Price with his party of 15 should be Premier
than Butler with his party of three. In the
average Parliamentary Election, witli half-a-dozen
men running for one seat, it generally happens
that nobody gets an absolute majority ; but even
Butler would probably denounce the colossal
impudence of the man who got less votes than any-
body else and still insisted that he was the proper
person to have the seat. And yet, though Price
has so much better right to be Premier than
Butler has, the friends of the Labor Party can t
view either wliat has happened or what is likely
to happen with much satisfaction. The party
never had such a chance before, and if it gets so
good a chance again certainly isn’t likely to get a
better one. For consider, not so much what they
had to do as what their opponents had to do.
Those opponents had to go to the Assembly
electors and tell them that five-sevenths of them
weren’t fit to have a vote for the Leg. Council, and
they had to ask the five-sevenths to roll up at the
poli and declare their own unfitness in the usual
manner. It was about the hardest row that a
political party had ever set itself to hoe ; and it
should have made things dead easy for the other
side—the side that asked the five-sevenths to roll
up and say that they were fit to vote for the
Council. Yet the Laborites couldn’t score a win
—anyway, couldn t score a majority, which isn t at
all cheerful under the circumstances. And the
outlook isn’t cheerful, either. Without a majority
of its own the party will have to go slow and com-
promise, as the State party has done m Wes-
tralia, and do infinite damage to its good repute
and its ideals in the process; or else go slow
fuse to compromise, as the Federal Labor party did,
in which case it will soon have to go out. And the
Labor party doesn’t want to go out—it wants to
stay in, where it can do things, and let its enemies
stay out. But the thing that sticks out farthest
of all for the moment is the failure of the party to
get a majority notwithstanding that it had the
very best case with which a party could possibly
go to the electors, notwithstanding that the public
was heartily sick of the old Jenkins Butler
crowd, and had so little confidence in Darling
that it threw him out, and notwithstanding that
there was hardly any organisation on the other
side, and that it had itself the help of the most
widely-circulated daily paper in the State, it it
couldn't win by a handsome majority in these cir-
cumstances, how long will it be before it can
expect to win ? Or does its position point to the
fact that its basis is even yet too narrow, and
that there is no real, practical hope until it
takes in all the Liberals who are now drifting
about more or less unattached. And the outlook
for the Federal party is even less satisfactory than
it was for t{ie State .flirty. The State party
didn’t have to “sink ,the fiscal lsspe, there bfemg
no fiscal issue in State politics to either sink, bury
or otherwise dispose of ; and so it got support
which the fiscal-sinker can t reasonably expect to
get, there being such a vast number of people who
have got sick pf pretending that the thing
in Australian national politics is a corpse.
Some Practical Socialism.
State Premier Carruthers has announced him-
self as convinced, after reading CoGHLAN S reports
from London, that “the sooner *.S.W. can
finance its own loans within the State the better
it will be.” In saying this, Joseph is certainly
much more accurate than usual. Local borrowing
is far preferable to foreign borrowing in every
way, and N.S.W. has already done a great deal ot
it: in fact, it has already done enough to make it
seem more than probable that the State corn
already rel' wholly on the local market—it it only
cared to try. But while going thusfar, it seems a
pity that Joseph didn’t exercise his brilliant
imagination a little further, and consider the
advisability of doing without loans altogether.
Foreign borrowing is utterly and irredeemably
bad, and local borrowing, while not so bad, is a
wildly ridiculous thing when followed as a
general practice. If a country builds its
own public works out of taxation,, it simply
builds them out of its own money and is done with
it. If the same country builds its public works
out of internal loans, it also builds them out of its
own money, but it does so by a crazy process of
circumlocution. It doesn’t pay itself the money
to build the works, but it borrows the money
from itself, and gives itself its own 10 U whereby
it promises solemnly to tax itself to pay itself the
interest, and also to tax itself sufficiently some day
to repay itself the principal, lben it builds the
works out of its own money which it has lent to
itself. The country that taxes itself to pay tor a
£2,000,000 railway to the North Coast, or else-
where, has simply got a railway to the good when
the work is finished. The country that borrows
£2,000,000 internally for the same work has,
when the work is finished, a railway wort 1
£2,000,000 and £2,000,000 worth of saleable securi-
ties—its own lON payable by itself to itselt
—floating about the share market, and against
these it owes £2,000,000. The community as a
whole, and viewed as if it were a single individual,
isn’t a bit further forward than if it had paid for
the whole work directly out of taxation and the
community, viewed as a collection of human
units, is a great deal further behind. It the
£2 000,000 worth of railway is built out of taxation
the valuable asset thus created is common pro-
perty ; the poorest producer of the Labor party
owns as large a share of it as the most affluent
middleman at Darling Point. But when it is
built out of loans the handful of rich men who
advanced the money own it all till the 1 O U is
redeemed, and in N.B.W. the IO U never is
redeemed. If N.B.W. had never borrowed, but
had constructed its public works out of revenue,
some £80,000,000 worth of works would now be
common property —representing about £ol P el
head As it is they are nominally common pro-
perty but are really the property of a compara-
tively small crowd of capitalists. Every time the
Labor party gives its assent to a new loan, instead
of demanding that public works be paid tor-
out of revenue, it is throwing dirt on the most
sacred principle of Socialism; it is voting for an
extension of the power of Capitalism; and it is
helping the small oligarchy of the Rich to extern
tlieir power over the vast multitude of the Poor.
It is a sorrowful thing to see Socialism thus be-
trayed by its own advocates, just because the
betrayal saves a little present effort, and a little
present thought, and a little immediate responsi-
bility The central idea of Socialism is equal
opportunity for all, and there can be no equal
opportunity where 1000 of/Are children of the
poor are born owing a debt of £55 each, and
one child of the rich is born to inherit
£55,000 worth of their I OU s, and to collect
from them all their lives some £2OOO a year
in interest, and to invest the tribute carefully
so that the burden will go on permanently in-
creasing. The first-the very first—step towards
practical Socialism is to give every person an equal
share-equal in reality as well name--in the
State assets, and to put an end to the accursed
system whereby the child of the Many, born in
Red fern dr Paddington, is pawned before its
birth to the child of the Few, born at Darling
Point or Toorak, or in the west end of London.
No min who is not opposed to the borrowing
durse, root and branch, is worth a straw as a
Socialist, no matter how loudly he may cry out m
vague and whirling eloquence for the recon-
struction of society upon a new basis. Ihe test
of the Sincere believer lies in his willingness to
take up the burdens and responsibilities of his
faith, as well as to “ holler ” for it.

An Anomalous Position.
This paragraph, from a Sydney daily, seems to
vaguely remind The Bulletin of something it
has heard before : ,
An unemployed demonstration, organised by the
Sydney Labor Council, was held yesterday at the Queens
Statue. Spe ches were delivered by eaden, in
eluding Mr. Holman, M.I,A„ and a was eax..-
calling on the Government to find work for <OOO men.
Now Holman, M.L.A., is, if not a Freetrader by
conviction, at least a man who has done a good
deal in the way of promoting the arrival of 1 tee-
trade on the ground that the fiscal issue is a tin
of no importance. In view of his fiscal attitude,
therefore, it seems a fair thing to mention to him
that any share he had in the resolution quoted
above amounts to amazing assurance. Ihe 1' ree-
trade doctrine is that the city artisan, if he can t
hold his own on equal terms against cheap foreign
competition, should be unemployed. Australia,
thanks largely to the Freetrade politicians
and the Freetrade voters of Sydney, has got
Freetrade in an immense number of industries,
and it seems that 7000 men in Sydney have tailed
to hold their own under these conditions I lie
Freetrader says the conditions are all right m
fact, that they should be made worse. Therefore,
the lack of employment, by the same logic, is all
right. Not one Freetrade artisan in all Australia
is out of work, save through the inability of the
Australian workman to maintain Ins ground under
the present tariff against his cheap outside com-
petitor. The market for his work is here, as is
evidenced by the £37,000,000 worth of goods which
foreigners poured into Australia last, year , but
while these come in the local man can t get a job
at supplying the market. In Sydney a good pro-
portion of him votes for keeping the ports open
and letting these £37,000,000 worth of foreign
goods in, and regards the policy of open pmtsj as
one of the world’s Great Truths. It is difficult,
therefore, to see what reason he has to object to
the consequences of his Great Truth, the -
tectionist artisan has a legitimate ground o
complaint, and deserves all possible help and
sympathy, but it is hard to understand where the
Freetrade one has any right to object. To denounce
the protected industry, which supports say, 1000
men, because it is “ artificial,’ and then to demand
that the State shall find artificial employment for
7000 men is anomalous. And the Freetrade poli-
tician who holds that it is right to shut OP indus-
tries, or to prevent industries starting, unless thej
can compete on even terms against the cheap
foreigner, and then demands that the general tax-
payer shall support the men thus thrown out, or
kept out, of employment, is a fair object to throw
mud at. He advances his doctrine of free imports
as something that will make the country rich, and
if! instead "t results in there being 7000 men un-
employed in and around Sydney, while N-b.W .
alone is giving nearly £12,000,000 worth of work
per annum to the foreigner, he ougbteitherto
explain these 7000 men away, or else feed them
himself.

If I were King- of Australia, with absolute power and
thimrs all round stood just as they do now, I think that I
would invite a million Chinese, a million Javanese, a
million Hindus, and some other colored people, and all
the white men who choose to come inbo tro P' cal o^ f
of Australia, to grow cotton, rice, sugar, coffee,
and all other kinds of tropical produce M.Reason f l
invitino- many colored races, instead of a chosen one,
would be to prevent an objectionable clannish or national
feeling growing up, and becoming too stiong tor the
public welfare.— Bookseller E. W. Cole.

for the blackened Coles.
I’d look for a Black Man’s Book Arcade, and

grease in your hair !

Man Who Made Things Black.

dirty, stinking street,

pleasing Hindu tongue ;

coal-black hangman hung.

Opera House Rickards’ Vaudeville Co.
Town Hall Hugo Heermann.
On Saturday night, at Her Majesty’s,
Sydney, writer viewed “Tom Moore” and his
friend, Andrew Mack, through a hollow tooth into
which a cold wind had blown seven devils; and,
so far as he was concerned, Moore and Mack had
to fight the seven through four rounds. The result
reflected considerable credit on Moore and Mack.
I here were odd times when at least six of the
devils were on the floor taking their ten seconds,
?; n d, generally speaking, two of them were down.
But never the lot. On Mack-Moore’s ballad repu-
tation, writer had hopes of laying the entire bag
of devils when it came to the time for Thomas
s ! kut there was at least one that hung out
to the end—a yellow devil, no doubt, and from
Derry maybe, who owned up that Mack had
his points, but that he marred his ballads by
trying to get too much light and shade into
them. In the play, when Tom finds things
going against him, he lifts his alto-falsetto in
a song of ould Ireland, and the result is always
just as Tom wants it to be. If the lordly villain
is on the point of robbing Thomas of his colleen,
Thomas, as a last resort, sings, and the colleen
comes straight back into the path of Thomas,
which, of course, is the path also of virtue. When
Mrs. Malone demands the rint in a brogue that
has a blackthorn at the end of it, and gives Tom
one hour in which to pay or quit, and Tom hasn’t
a bean to pay with and hasn’t a quit about him,
he sings “Love’s Young Dream,” and melts the
raging blackthorn into utter oblivion of the rint.
Thomas s victories in the balladrama are too
complete. Still, it’s a handy, unconventional way
of overcoming the difficulties which beset the hero’s
path; a 2: rich more delightful way, too, than
\i somebody brought a tearful lady with a Past
and explained to the colleen that the Past was the
lordly villain’s doing ; also it’s a much more plea-
sant way of dodging the rint than via a window,
a ladder, and a neighbor’s roof,
* * * *
Truth may be stranger than fiction, but
for stage purposes, “ Tom Moore’s ” dramatist
puts his money on fiction. Beyond that he was a
poet and singer and wit, and met various people,
few things happen to A. Mack as they
happened to T. Moore. It is good melodrama
undoubtedly; but just how far it is desirable to
give absurdly distorted views of history is another
matter—the tooth previously referred to urged
a a j a very serious view ought to be taken of it.
Andrew Mack makes the situation more serious
than it might be by his natural representation of
the character. You never suspect that it isn’t
1. Moore, but only A. Mack, in front of you,
so easy and spontaneous is he. Even the
musical interruptions don’t apparently inter-
rupt. But, after Mack, the rest count for
not very much. Bessie Dyke, the colleen round
whom the love-story moves, is played with
moderate success by Miss Lovett. Mrs. Malone,
aforementioned, is a comparatively small part
made conspicuous by Miss Berlein. ' And amongst
the men, a magnificent Prince Regent remains the
most fixed memory.
** * *
‘‘Pygmalion and Galatea,” staged at
■Sydney Royal for a few nights last week,
is a piece of which the public is not yet
tired. Miss Maud Jeffries was an excellent
Galatea, and Mr. Plimmer a fine, manly Pygma-
lion. If only he would school himself out of that
melodramatic stride—though it is useful when
Marcus Enormous is throwing his mightv legs
through ancient Rome. A feature of the revival
was the excefient broad comedy acting of Mrs. G.
B. W. Lewis and Mr. Carey as the art patrons of
Athens.
** * *
Revival of “Monsieur Beaucaire,” at
Sydney Royal, on Saturday, with Mr. J. B. N.
Osborne in the name part, attracted a huge audi-
ence. Mr. Osborne is a stage-smitten youth of
some ‘ society ” standing, who has made himself
notable, to a certain extent, by marrying Miss
Maud Jeffries, the star of the company in which
he has been playing minor parts with more or less
justification. When he was given the responsi-
bilifcy of acting such a difficult character as
Beaucaire—a part, the stagey artificiality of which
is such that the slightest over-emphasis or hint of
bathos must make it ridiculous—it was clearly not
that he had of a sudden blossomed into a star
actor, but with the idea of exploiting the public
interest in a romantic marriage. And, considered
in that light, the experiment was a notable suc-
cess. On the first night the theatre was crowded
to the doors, and the following performances were
well attended.. It is equally certain that if Mr.
E. W. O’Sullivan were billed as Hamlet there
would be just as lucrative results for a few nights,
and just at present the appearance of Mr. William
Nicholas Willis as Joseph of Canaan, or Little
Eva, or almost anything, would be a profitable
spec. But, nevertheless, experiments of that sort
are not fair to the public, nor to the theatrical pro-
fession, leaving out of account Dramatic Art
(D. Art’s feelings have of late been so outraged by
musical comedy as to be quite insensible to affront
now); and certainly they are not wise policy
for a great theatrical manager, whose busi-
ness, laboriously worked up by the exercise of
judgment and skill, would be at the mercy of
any smart advertising canvasser once a system of
“notoriety presentations” became general.
Mr. Osborne, as Beaucaire, played with
consummate self-confidence, and that is really all
that can be said in his favor. Neither voice
nor figure mark him as Nature’s choice of an
actor, and he has not had the stage experi-
ence to teach him even to walk naturally on
the boards. On Saturday the elaborate French
accent on his English was dropped as
frequently as he forgot it, which was often,
and his odd bits of French were not always exactly
Parisian. But these were the natural and inevi-
table marks of what was essentially an ‘ ‘ amateur ”
performance. Mr. Osborne is not to be blamed,
rather the policy which obtrudes an amateur star
into the midst of a professional season. One
effect of Mr. Osborne’s place in the cast was to
throw up in high relief the other actors. Mr.
Harry Plimmer is always a clever actor, i.nd, as
Major Molyneux, he never showed to better advan-
tage ; and Mr. Wontner, as the Duke of Winterset,
cut likewise a fine figure. Miss Maud Jeffries is
familiar to the Sydney public in the role of Lady
Mary Carlyle; her acting on Saturday had an
unusual amount of sprightliness to add to its
charm.
** * *
To-night and to-morrow night (which
closes the season) Sydney Royal will resound to
the mellifluous romancings of Claude Melnotte,
who was the ancestor of Daniel O’Connor, of
N.S. W. Miss Maud Jeffries will carry the dainty
ear into which those sonorous nothings will be
poured; but whether Mr. Maud will be the
Claude of the play or not, is a secret which was not
disclosed when this issue went to press. A short
and most welcome Gilbert and Sullivan season
follows.
* * *
Mr. Oliver Bainbridge, who needs his
hair cut, gave his lecture on the “Black Jews”
last week. The subject is too painful to be
pursued further.
#* * *
“Yes, Barbara, you may speak privately.
This room is free from intrusion, ” says Archibald
Carlyle in “East Lynne,” revived at Sydney
Palace on Saturday night. And the damp .drama
proceeds along lines quite up to that, which
is Mrs. Henry Woods’ literary level. Curious
circumstance that every time The Bulletin
has not shirked seeing “East Lynne,” it has sat
among the same kind of exasperating damsel—the
kind that sucks peppermint-drops and violently
rustles its paper bag. That immortal scene be-
tween the runaway wife, Lady Isabel, and her
uncle, Lord Mount Severn, was almost inaudible
because the circle’s paper-bag was rustling like the
G.P.O. on English-mail day. The philosophy of
such a scene ought not to be marred by a
mere bag. The peer knows that his niece has
been “keeping house” with her betrayer for
nearly a year; he has been talking to her in
a mood of dignified sorrow and severity; then
between her sobs she lets fall an intimation
that there is an illicit infant. In hushed horror
the peer rises, his glassy eyes suggesting a corpse
looking through a window, his index finger pointing
at the Sin. Then he finds his voice and whispers,
O, Isabel, and you an earl’s daughter! ”
Mr. Stanford, who acted a lord, deserved every
echo of the burst of applause that followed, for
he swam manfully up a cataract of drivel. The
railway smash is not shown as a collision in active
business. A boomful report is heard, and the
curtain goes up and shows a fierce red glare
as of a town on fire over the pleasant land
of France, and there are engines reduced to
scrap iron, and telescoped carriages, and dead
bodies. Lady Isabel lies in the arms of a Sister of
Mercy, and the tragedy prepares to start fresh com-
plications. Miss Duggan looks picturesque in the
happy-home act, and if she would only get rid of
those unnatural acquired basement tones of
voice, and say “My husband ” instead of
“ M’hosband,” The Bulletin would love her like
a brother. Miss Ida Gresham talks the part of
Barbara Hare like a second-hand gramophone.
Miss Ivy Gorrick, as the maid, Joyce, does
a capital and natural bit of acting, and looks extra
pretty. Mr. Laurence Dunbar’s conception of the
wicked baronet, Sir Francis Leveson, is—well it is
callous as a wicked baronet ought to be, and
when he scratches his head with that freedom
from self-consciousness for which all the best
people are remarkable, and then meditatively
inspects his nails, everyone in the audience feels
sure that Laurence knows a thing or two about
baronets’ drawing-room ways. A number of other
characters keep the tragedy up to a fair level.

Mr. John Sheridan, as “King Dodo” at
Sydney Criterion, tries to renew his youth, and
continues to win a beautiful bride (without
apparent reason for such good fortune). The
songs of Miss Maud Amber and Mr. Winfield
Blake are now running as easily as oil down a
gentle slope ; the chorus has riveted itself toge-
ther ; and if the public desires to forget its gas
bills and other vexations, it may do so by watch-
ing “King Dodo.” A handsome Brisbane girl
with some voice—Miss Walden-Taylor—has joined
the co., and the beak of the Dodo does not grow
less.
* * sic- *
The Westminster glee party has been
raising its several and collective voice at Sydney
Town Hall throughout the week, except for two
days, when the boys took their white collars up
to sooty Newcastle. The collars came back sadly
soiled, but the charmingly blended voices are as
good as new; and Madame Hooten’s full con-
tralto, basso Sterndale Bennett’s humorous
encores, the piping of the large-sized alto,
Llewellyn’s vigor, and Branscombe’s own fluent
and expressive tenor continue to merit the
remarks that were poured upon them last week.
** * *
Fuller Golden continues to be the bright
star, likewise the comet with the longest tail, and
the aurora borealis, at Sydney Ti voli. There is a
considerable shortage just now of large letters on
the bills, and the man who prints them is making a
fortune out of the ink he isn’t using. If Winton
and some of the others took a sea trip to Tom
Ugly’s Point, and called themselves Morcom
Granby, and were billed as the distinguished
entertainers who had brought warmth and glad-
ness to the cold home of the Grand Llama of
Thibet, and had made seven triumphal tours
through the Courts of Europe, they would pro-
bably be reckoned very extra-special. As things
are, the local manufacturer of mirth and melody
has nearly as cold a time as the local manufacturer
of pots and pans.
** * *
At Her Majesty’s (Melb.), where she
boomed exceedingly five years ago, Miss Nance
O’Neil made her reappearance as Magda last
Saturday night. Playgoing Melbourne is mostly
familiar with the plot of Sudermann’s domestic
tragedy, and would like to have the first act cut
shorter. Magda ran away from her father’s
stuffy domicile when she was 17. The place
smelt of camphor, cough-mixture, and bourgeois
respectability. Her virtuous, tyrannical father
wouldn’t let her call her soul her own. He had
dedicated it to “The Purity of the Hearth” and
everlasting dullness. So Magda went forth upon
the loose, and a priggish young townsman, named
Von Keller, plucked the first fruits of her warm,
maternal instincts. After six months of joyous
amorosity in cheap lodgings—half a year of
sweet illusion and bread-and-dripping and kisses
—Magda lost her young man. He went back to
provincial respectability, leaving his girl to work
out her destiny. She was a girl in a million,
but the dull dog didn’t appreciate her rarity.
Magda’s destiny began with a baby. She sup-
ported her child by singing ; finally she sang her-
self into a position, where she was able to look
back at her stuffy, cheerless girlhood and thank
her stars for her escape. Now, Sudermann’s play
presents this brainy rebel prima donna as a tempo-
rary sentimentalist, roosting under the paternal
roof for no discoverable reason. It isn’t exactly
her tradition of childish love for a pig-headed
father with whom she hasn’t one thought in
common, nor her more natural affection for a
gentle, nerveless sister, nor her strange desire to
please the family pastor that induces brilliant
Magda to come and stay a week in the
atmosphere of propriety, " which had half
stifled her even before she had breathed the
air of freedom. She comes back in order to
provide plot for Sudermann’s play. Her morbid
parent, always worrying after the secrets of her
Past (it had lasted 12 years), discovers that his
learned friend, Von Keller, was the first secret,
whereupon the old tyrant commands his daughter
to marry Keller, and she positively entertains this
notion. Meanwhile her splendid career is kept
waiting outside on the mat. Keller, who is locally
a tin god, proposes that Magda, on marrying him,
shall cut the stage for the sake of his precious
respectability, and this is the last straw which
breaks the prima donna’s patience. She in-
forms her fatuous dad, in other words, that
Von Keller is merely a contemptible detail in
her Past. It has been a broad, comprehensive
Past, embracing other and more interesting men.
Then it is time for the old German tyrant to ex-
pire. He dies of apoplexy, or his daughter’s Past
flies to his stomach, like the gout. Anyhow, he
passes hence, and Magda appears grief-stricken at
the finish. Perhaps she is wondering what parti-
cular sort of Sheol can be reserved for bullying,
meddlesome, Scripture-quoting slaves of Mrs.
Grundy, such as was her father, now deceased.
* * « *
The difference between the present and
past Miss Nance O’Neil is noticeable. A some-
what quieter actress, she is less weighty and to
the same degree less forcible than of yore. Her
voice has lost a little strength whilst her elocution
has retained its main defect, for she still chops her
lines into short lengths and speaks them in a
tutored strain, the old fault being very apparent
when she dialogues with the good shepherd
in the second act. The subsequent stormy scene
with Von Keller practically compels any experi-
enced actress to let herself go naturally. Miss
O’Neil’s Magda might carry fuller conviction to the
critical mind if her support were up to the best
Australian standard of dramatic art, which it cer-
tainly is not. “Her own selected company”
includes three of the originals—M‘Kee Rankin,
Mrs. Bracy and Miss Ricca Allen. Rankin is a
weakened repetition of his previous Colonel
Schwartz, and he rather suggests a grim Uncle
Tom, with the blackness off him, than a military
person. The new arrivals are not a pronounced
success in this instance, nor is there any justifica-
tion for the buffoonery introduced by three of the
“ comic relief ” characters. “ Magda ”is only for
six nights. On Saturday, the co. will appear in
another of Sudermann’s play—“ The Fires of St.
John.”
** * *
At Melbourne Princess’s there is no wild,
unreasonable demand for “Bill Adams.” The
medley of song and dance and “wheezes” is a
brisk little show, and the public that took kindly
to, say, “Mama’s New Husband,” might have
spared a satisfactory patronage for “Bill”; but
the public is otherwise engaged. Therefore “ The
Rose of the Riviera” will take the floor on Satur-
day. A fortnight later the Brough-Flemming
Comedy Co. is to open its proceedings with
“ Beauty and the Barge.”
** * *
The announcement of Hugo Heermann as
a great master of the violin and the possessor of a
remarkably fine instrument to play upon, was
borne out by his first performance at Melbourne
Town Hall last Saturday afternoon. The bearded
stranger was heard in a Beethoven concerto, and
likewise a Mendelssohn ditto, with an admirable
orchestra of 50, and the results fetched more ap-
plause (genuine) from a fair-sized audience
than is raised on six ordinary occasions
when the hall is crowded. Heermann’s
music is not of the sensuous order. On
Tuesday night he was to play the Kreutzer
Sonata with W. A. Laver at the piano, and
although the “ Kreutzer,’’according to Tolstoi, is
a purely improper work, it goes as a matter of
course that Heermann gave it a high moral tone
at every possible opportunity. One listens to the
artist with pleasure whilst regarding the man with
reverence, not to say awe. He is so modest a
workman. He makes the finest feats of technique
appear simple. He has no tricks of pose, no
tendency to spread himself, no superfluous growth
of hair. He appears to love music better than he
loves Hugo Heermann. A calm intrepid pro-
fessor who would die, if necessary, in defence of
his famous violin.
** * *
“Sporting Life” has only another week to
flourish at Melbourne Royal before “The Flood
Tide ” sets in. Meanwhile Walter Baker is coming
up to the scratch every evening in place of the
other alleged pugilist (Albert Norman), who has
drunk of the brandy when it was drugged.
Baker’s easy defeat of the Terrible Chicken is
about the most extraordinary occurrence in the
annals of melodramatic heroism. He doesn’t need
to remove his braces for the purpose. His name
is Lord Woodstock, and his family pride will not
permit him to strip further than his dress-coat,:
vest, and shiny belltopper. One of these nights
he will probably beat the record by wearing his
belltopper throughout the battle, and jumping on
it in his hour of victory.

“0.K.”: A.N.A. competition, which
disturbs the mental balance of hundreds of
young Victorians, seems to have accounted for
the appearance of an amateur girl singer at Her
Majesty’s (Melb.) the other afternoon, when
G. B. Lewis took his benefit. The young lady
sang “ The Lost Chord ” in a small voice and
fairly well-trained style. On her singing she had
no visible pretensions to rank as a professional
contralto; also, she lacked the face and figure
for a successful public career. She was an unin-
teresting performer in every respect, but her
presence on the stage, for this occasion only, was
said to be justified by the fact that she has
taken a first prize at many of the A.N.A.

Miss Eileen Castles, the third of the
Castles sisters to have notoriety thrust upon her,
turns out to be a quite uncalled-for subject for
enthusiasm. Given the same opportunities, a
hundred other Melbourne damsels could, in a foot-
ball way of speaking, run .vocal ‘‘rings” round
Miss Eileen.
## * *
The Opera House programme is waiting
for reinforcements just at present. Alice Pierce
and Fred Millis are the largest typed performers
on the Rickards job, and their “turns” are not
unworthy of the type, but the lady’s imitations of
actresses have been there before, and the advt.
states a double truth when it claims that Millis is
a ventriloquist who “starts where all others
finish.” The matter with Millis is painfully
apparent. He ought to have been in front of the
others before they started. Next Saturday brings
Milo and Cruickshank to light. Milo is described
as a lovely living picture of marble Yenuses and
chaste Dianas who revives the glories of ancient
Greek art, what time Cruickshank reflects lightning
caricatures of modern individuals upon a sheet.
** # *
An Adelaide correspondent writes :
The Broughs, leaving Herbert Flemming behind in
Westralia to convalesce, opened at Adelaide Royal with
“ Beauty and the Barge.” The points of the piece may be
appraised in this order: (1) The stage setting. (2) The
chance given to Robert Brough. (3) Clean and idyllic
farcical comedy. The third is all right—but the other
two are better still. The bar of a country inn is realistic,
and the open river scene (from bank to bank) of the last
act beats, for spaciousness and open-air effect, anything
seen in Adelaide since “ Midsummer Night.” Also Brough
as Captain Barley navigates an enormous barge, with
sails, clean across the stage. Harry Whaite is the scenic
artist. The story is in regular Jacobs style, with the
juveniles refined a bit too much, and a peppery major
who is an excrescence. The barge-captain is a general
philanderer, with at least two “lilies of the valley” in
cultivation at once, and he hopes to make steady com-
pany of the young girl who takes passage with him. With
her lover disguised as the mate, and one of his own
‘ lilies ’ shut up under hatches, the captain makes heavy
weather of it, and at the finish he is left rather patheti-
cally alone, steering off into the ewigkeit. Brough was
welcomed back to Adelaide with a Register leader in the
morning, and with an ovation that shook the house at
night. Disguised in ear-rings, a beard, and a fearsome
provincial accent, he carries the whole play on his back.
Mrs. Brough is standing out for the present, but Miss
Temple and Miss Major play the two charmers of uncer-
tain age. What the young new-comers have to do they
skim through lightly enough. John F. Forde, as an old
sea-dog, is allowed to score heavily with a really Jacobsy
attack of pretended illness, which costs the young lover
£5. When Herbert Flemming comes to Adelaide, “ The
Walls of Jericho ” may be expected.

Thus the funds of Australasian offices have increased by
£14,737,256 during the past 10 years—an increase of
almost exactly 66 per cent., while the three American
offices doing business in Australia have increased 123 p.c.,
or at very nearly double the Australian rate. Of course
a small part of this increase is due to Australian business ;
but it is so small as to hardly count. High Protection,
and the consequent magnificent prosperity of the U.S.,
furnishes practically the whole explanation of the
dazzling figures. While the aggregate increase of
funds in Australasian offices has amounted to 66
p.c., the principal individual growths have been as
follows:—A.M.P., 61‘8 p.c.; Nat. Mutual, 81'2 (this
society was amalgamated with the Mutual of Victoria
in 1896, and an allowance is made so as to show
figures on the same basis as the others); Maoriland,
61 - 3 ; Col. Mutual, 62-8 ; Mutual Life Ass., 78-2 ; Widows’
Fund, 41'1 ; Citizens’ (ordinary), 1639-8; Citizens’(indus-
trial), 128-2 ; T. &G. (ord.), 100-8 ; City Mutual, 128. But
these figures are probably more interesting than valuable.
The young society naturally shows up best; for one thing
its outgo for claims is much smaller; also the society
which had only £lOOO to start with had to add but £lOOO
in order to show an increase of 100 per cent., while the
society which had £1,000,000 has to show a thousand
times £lOOO in order to grow 100 per cent.

Of the American offices’ holding in cash, etc., the
following is represented by stocks and shares :—Equit-
able, 55-2 p.c. ; N.Y. Life, 607 p.c. ; M.L. of New York,
52 p.c. Australian bank rates of interest having been
comparatively high during the year, many offices have
increased their bank deposits, shown above as “ Cash,
&c.” In but very few cases has there been an increase in
the holding of public securities—as a class by far the most
solid of all the securities. Freeholds and leaseholds, in-
cluding foreclosed properties, are still excessively heavy
in some offices; but in nearly every case a reduction is
shown. This class of security accounts often for low
earnings. “ Other loans ” are mostly loans on policies—
generally a very undesirable feature of life assurattce
business. Approximately 75 per cent, of Australasian
life-assurance funds are represented by public securities,
mortgages, or cash, and another 13 per cent, by loans on
policies within their surrender value.
* * * ,
Life assurance offices anxiously watched a case
that was before Justice Oohen (N.S.W.), the other day.
A man had “proposed” to the New York Life, and had
been put off on the ground of “ moral hazard.” “ Moral
hazard ” is an indefinite thing ; pretty often it means
that the proponent is financially unsound, and possibly
disposed to suicide, or, anyway, not likely to give the
office a square deal. For their own protection (that is to
say, for the protection of honest policy-holders), the
offices exchange confidential reports, so that a man who
has been declined by one office cannot lie his way into
another office. The New York Life, on the application of
the City Mutual, confidentially reported that a proposal
to the latter had been declined for “ moral hazard.” The
existence of this confidential report; somehow leaked out
into the ear of the proponent, who wanted £lOOO to wipe
out the alleged smudge on his character. Matters, how-
ever, didn’t go beyond the plaintiff’s case ; Judge at that
stage promptly non-suited, on the ground that the report
was privileged (which was admitted), and that there
wasn’t any malice to destroy the privilege (which wasn’t
admitted). Yet, how far offices can go in this matter
isn’t clearly settled even now. All depends upon the
absence or presence of malice ; and legal malice is a strange
and uncertain animal.
• * * *
Treasurer Daglish, of Wesbralia, has been try-
ing Bull-Cohen with another little lot of £1,400,000, and
uncle’s answer is calculated to make any ordinary
Treasurer feel small and sad. Apparently because he had
got some speculators to underwrite the loan for a heavy
commission, Daglish reckoned that the thing was a
success, and that Westralia could safely go ahead and
make a splash ; wherefore he filled his political pro-
gramme with bridges and harbors and railways and
batteries. But now it transpires that the public
only took £350,000 of the loan, leaving £1,050,000 on
the hands of the underwriters ; and as it is understood
that there are large sums of previous loans still in the
hands of the same go-betweens, the chances of Daglish
getting any more are Buckley’s. Any improvement that
takes place in the market will be promptly seized by the
underwriters to unload the baby they are nursing ; and
though they will naturally say nice things about Westra-
tralian stock until they get rid of the infant, once they
get out of their trouble, the Westralian market will be
billed as too dangerous to touch. So Daglish will get his
£1,400,000 ; but If he wants to stick to his gaudy works
proposals he will probably have to raise the rest of the
money from revenue. And up to now he hasn’t shown
that he has half grit enough to do it.
• * * *
The crowd that G-. H. Reid expects to get his
anti-Socialistic support from in Westralia is still busy-
reaching out for more Socialism. When Premier Daglish
was up at Kalgoorlie, the other day, the Fattest mer-
chants and property-owners of the city lined up in a
deputation, told him that anti-Socialism, as represented
by the fire insurance co.’s, was treating them shamefully,
and begged him to set a State Fire Insurance Depart-
ment going. Daglish, who represents a party which is
supposed to simply rush into Socialism bald-headed,
reminded them that they hadn’t supplied him with any
figures. But he added that if figures, when they were
supplied, were satisfactory, he would ask Parliament for
the necessary authority-.
* * * *
Premier Carruthers ought to be encouraged to
resist the attempt of the Bull co. to prevent a Govern-
ment township being “proclaimed” near the proposed
shale and oil mines and works. The Bull co.’s idea is
apparently to house the workmen in its own cottages, on
its own property ; and Australia has had several samples
of what that means. N.S.W. has one Bull-owned town-
ship where a local paper which once spoke disrespectfully
of the co. isn’t allowed to show its nose ; and another
State knows a mining township owned by the co., which
has used its ownership to break down a strike by threat-
ening wholesale evictions.
The Commercial Bank of Australia pays off, on
September 30, this year, the last block of the A series of
locked-up deposits, due in 1906. Gradually the awful
mess which was made in the days when there wasn’t any
Labor party is being- cleared up out of the profits made
in these days when the Labor party is alleged to be send-
ing the country to the dogs. And the humorous feature
of this special case is that Henry Gyles Turner, who was
manager when the mess was made, still wants to be taken
as a serious authority on how to do things.
* * * *
The Equitable Life office, of New York, seems
to be pulling itself out of its troubles. Young vice-
president Hyde, who held the stock of the founder of the
office, his father, and whose row with President Alexander
was at the bottom of the whole trouble, has sold out to
somebody (presumably the society, though this point
isn’t clear yet); and trustees have been appointed to use
the powers which this stock carries. These trustees, who
include ex.-U.S. President Grover Cleveland, will act in
the interests of policy-holders.

months ago, a large number of matured shares having
been paid out. The society still works almost exclu-
sively on its own capital, having but £9OO on deposit;
while of its securities only £lBO3 is represented by loans
to shareholders, the balance of £15,564 being out on
mortgage to non-shareholders. But S. Australia has a
State Bank, one result of which appears to be to reduce
the rate of interest on such securities as a well-managed
building orinsurance society looksafter. Several insurance
societies say frankly that they can’t afford to lend out
money in S. Australia on mortgage while they have the
other States open to them—States in which the State
Bank idea hasn’t been developed as far—and the Queen
Building Society shows gross interest-earnings of only £6
28. per cent, on its mean funds. This, and the compara-
tively small turn-over, account for the low dividend ; ex-
penses probably wouldn’t be much more if the office did
twice the work.
m * * »
“Capt.” Morish, the first manager of the
Broken Hill Central and South, died a few days back, in
Adelaide. Both the Central and South were owned by
Sydney men ; and Sydney, as usual, went down badly.
Both mines later turned up trumps ; but Sydney quitted
the game before that happened. But while Sydney had its
finger in that pie there were some magnificent market
jumps and slumps, and Morish had always the luck to get
in and get out at just the right time. He was that
comparatively rare bird—a mine manager who made a
pile before he got too old or too good to enjoy it.
* * * *
After a lull, Broken Hill shows more energy in
the matter of zinc production. B.H. Prop, has started
its acid plant again ; Sulphide Corporation (which has
been using a magnetic plant) has just added a granula-
tion process, designed to separate all the minerals in the
ore, which is the ideal process if costs can be kept down ;
and the British is about to introduce the Potter (acid)
process. This last co. has about 350,000 tons of tailings
and slimes, from which a profit of ss. per ton is expected,
though neighboring Block 14 only showed 2s. per ton,
using same process last half-year. Sulphide’s year closes
June 30; it has had a good time, and, with the heavy
carry-forward from last year, a div. on all shares is reason-
ably expected. This accounts for the move in shares.
* * * *
Broken Hill Junction shareholders held a
gloomy meeting at Adelaide last week, when it was
announced that the mill would have to shut down, and
efforts would be made to find more ore. Without a lot of
luck the co. won’t pull through on calls from present
shareholders, who are not in the mood to pay up. Mine
is not explored below 800 ft., and deeper sinking is wanted,
and that can’t be done satisfactorily on calls from people
who have largely lost confidence. Some of the profits
from former rich patches ought to have been reserved ; as
they weren’t, the fate of the mine seems to be to drift
into the hands of one of its neighbors. As anticipated,
Capt. Warren, who was so much out in his estimates, has
resigned the management.
* * * *
It looks as though a large slice of another
Broken Hill mine will drift towards Bull, South Blocks
having received an offer from a London syndicate (most
likely Lionel Robinson’s party) to put in £50,000 in ex-
change for 100,000 shares in a new 200,000 co. Present
co. is in 200,000 shares (100,000 paid and 100,000 contrib.);
paids will probably get 75,000 and contribs. 25,000 shares.
Former have stood lately at about 9s. ; contribs., 4s. 3d.,
or about £66,000 for the lot. Syndicate will get half the
mine for practically £25,000, as half the £50,000 which it
puts up will still be its own. Some sort of reconstruction
was inevitable. Mine has no mill and very few other
appliances ; it wants at least £30,000 spent on machinery,
and it is very doubtful whether the contribs. are strong
enough to put up the money. Mine was formerly part
of the B.H. South, and carries a strong body of low-grade
sulphides, which are no use to the co. without a mill.
• * * *
B. H. South Blocks contribs. will no doubt kick
against the proposed division of shares in the new co.—
25,000 to them for their 100,000 present shares ; 75,000 to
the paids for their 100,000. Is one paid worth' three
contribs. ? Market evidently didn’t think so, for it valued
paids at 95., contribs. at 4s. 3d. The old trouble over the
real meaning of the liability on contrib. shares is bound
to crop up. Contribs. will claim that they are cutting
away their interest in the mine to an eighth when, by paying
up a few calls, they might make the mine a div.-payer, and
own half of it, and get half the divs. But the B.H.
South didn’t let the contribs. escape calls, even after divs.
were coming steadily: and others have followed suit.
Speculators at one time didn't trouble over-much whether
shares in a productive mine were paids or contribs. But
some of them know better now—the contrib. is loaded.

The New Hillgrove Prop. (N.S.W.) May revenue
was £5480; working expenses, £1320 ; development and
other local charges, £847 ; profit on working account,
£3313, or just over 60 per cent, of the income. Things
are looking up again.
* * * *
Prospecting is still proceeding at Wallangalla
(Lightning Ridge, N.S.W.) opal field. Some good stone
is being won, and most of the men on the field are making
wages. The other day some stone was found which
realised £25 per oz.
* * * *
Developments continue interesting at Mouse-
trap, Wyalong (N.S.W.). In addition to an application
put in for the property by H. Naylor, a director of Golden
Fleece, A. Duncan, manager of the Fleece and share-
holder in Mousetrap, conjointly with H. Clements, also a
director of the Fleece, has pegged out the property as a
mining tenement. On the other hand, the Mines Depart-
ment is said to be disposed to review its action in can-
celling the lease: and altogether things are mixed, and
healthy-looking for the lawyers.
* * * »
Total output of W.A. mines for May was
157,6850 z. ; total for year, 810,2220 z., against 523,1690 z.
for same period of 1904. This shows a shortage of 69470 z.
To maintain same output as in 1904, returns for next
seven months must average 167,5000 z. ; and this is very
unlikely. Last year showed a deficiency of 81,5710 z.,
compared with 1903 ; this year is now likely to show about
50,0000 z. less than last year.
• * * *
South Kalgurli (W.A.), which was expected to
resume treatment of ore this month, is, for the moment,
in a bad way. Trial run of new plant failed, as power pro-
vided was found utterly inadequate. Part of the plant
was discarded at Lake View Consols some years ago.
South Kalgurli is working same lodes as L.V. Consols, so,
if furnace plant is suitable for treatment of ore, an error
was made in scrapping it 3J years ago. Developments on
South Kalgurli have recently been very satisfactory,
and it is a pity that starting of re-treatment should have
been delayed by any miscalculations. At No. 7 level
especially good ore is showing.
* * * *
North Kalgurli (W.A.), in spite of diamond-
drilling and crosscutting, has failed to locate any more
pay ore, and prospects seem pretty hopeless. Accumu-
lated slimes, which totted up 11.000 tons, have been
reduced to 4000 tons, and as every month 1000 tons more
are being treated than produced, accumulation will be
worked off by September at latest. Ore now being
treated yields Idwt. on plates, l£dwt. by cyanlding,
and 2dwt. by filter-pressing, or in all and this
certainly won’t pay, as costs work out at 30s. a ton or
thereabouts.
Golden Pole, Davy hurst (W.A.). continues to
open up splendidly. Winze below No. 8 level is down
85ft., and h£3 carried Sift, of reef, averaging 2oz. 12dwt.
Opening out at No. 4 level i's in progress, and this level
will be oonneoted with No. 3as soon as possible. A new
24-acre block adjoining on North has been pegged out,
making a total length of 36 chains on line of reef. Where
reef was cut in South block it has been followed down
40ft., where drives north and south are being put in. At
this point reef is 15in. wide, and worth 15dwt.
* * # *
Westralia Waihi, Davyhurst, is looking rather
better, and manager YVatts has hopes of making mine a
small div. payer. No. 4 reef on east is most hopeful find
made on property, and a crosscut from 282 ft. level is
being pushed ahead to test it at depth. Crosscut has to
go3ooit.,and is already in 100 ft., and is being pushed.
This new reef has been tested on surface by costeening,
and runs up to 83dwt. for 18in. wide. If values and size
are maintained when cut at depth, prospects of co. will be
materially improved.

* * * *
The electric plant of the Federal Palace Hotel,
corner Collins and King-streets, Melbourne, furnishes a
good example of a thoroughly successful electric installa-
tion, in technical as well as in commercial respects. This
plant generates all electric current required for the hotel
lighting ; it consists of a twenty horse-power gas-engine
belted to a “ Ganz ” twelve kilowatts dynamo, a “ Ganz ”
switch-board containing all the measuring and controlling
instruments, and a storage battery with 62 cells. The
generating machinery runs continually during the day
time at its full capacity and highest efficiency, the surplus
current being stored into the battery ; the latter supplies
all necessary electricity required during the night; and it
forms also a substantial reserve in case of accidents.
Compared with the previous method of working, a saving
of £4OO per annum has been effected by this plant, the
contractors for which were the Austral Otis Engineering
Oompany, Ltd., corner Hanah and Kavanagh-streets,
South Melbourne, as sole Australian agents for Ganz and
Company, Budapest, Hungary.*

The British Broken Hill Silver Mining Co.,
after careful consideration of all types of boilers, have
E laced an order with Babcock and Wilcox for three large
oilers, each of 500 h.p. Superheaters, chain grate
mechanical stokers and economiser have also been
Ordered from the same firm. This now makes 40 boilers
of the Babcock and Wilcox type supplied to the various
mines and industries at Broken Hill. Wherever economy
in fuel and maintenance of boiler has to be seriously con-
sidered by competent engineers, the Babcock boiler is
installed. Hence the recent unanimous decision of the
Expert Admiralty Committee to install Babcock boilers as
the standard for the British Navy.*

* * * *
The Wilfley Slime Table which has been in
operation on the Ivanhoe Gold Mines for some months
past has been giving extremely good results. Mr. H. T
Brett, Metallurgist of the Ivanhoe Gold Corporation, in an
article contributed to the March number of the Chamber
of Mines Journal, W.A., gives particulars of the savings
made, and states that the introduction of the Wilfley
Slime Table will be of great service in treating the low-
grade sulphide ores of the field. Since the future of
Kalgoorlie is so intimately bound up with the most
economical solution of this sulphide problem, this
assurance from Mr. Brett is a most important matter.
The Austral Otis Engineering Co., South Melbourne, are
sole manufacturers for Australasia of this table.*

The strength, lightness, durability and low cost
of spiral rivited pipe have given it a place of superiority
above any other pipe. This pipe possesses enormous
bursting streng th, and is protected, inside and out, with a
perfect coating manufactured from asphalt. Asphalt
coated steel pipes have lasted from 25 to 40 years without
any sign of decay, and may last as long again. Spiral
pipe is manufactured by Mephan Ferguson, at Wanganui,
New Zealand, and at his engineering and pipe works,
Melbourne, where the latest up-to-date machinery is also
used for making boilers, fluming, refrigerators, bolts, nuts,
and every discrintion of cast or wrought iron work.*

A REHEARSAL.
ACTOR-MANAGER: “I should not bellow those
lines, Anstruther .”
ANSTRUTHER (unmasking villainy): “Confound
it, sir, I have to make myself heard in the pit.”
ACTOR-MANAGER: “To be sieve, Anstruther, but
not necessarily in the bottomless pit.”
No less than .700 marriages were put
through in Sydney last year by the matrimonial
bureaux, as against only 92 by the State-marriage
agency—the registrar’s office. And even the 700
do not include those put through by men who
live nominally by turning people’s feet to heaven,
but really by directing them to matrimony.
S. Australia has now practically got rid of
every form of State pension. The Chief Justice
is the only remaining public servant who is
entitled to one when he retires —and at 69 he
hasn’t even mentioned retirement. Even the two
new judges, Justices Gordon and Homburg, took
up their work lately without hope or expectation
of pension ; and it will take some years before it
is known how that will work out. There isn’t
any convenient way of sacking a Judge when he
becomes deaf and partially blind, and develops
gout and a vile temper; yet it isn’t at all unlikely
that the Judge who hasn’t a pension to keep his
old age warm will hang on till these things happen
to him.
.#* * *
One Walter M‘Minnis pleaded guilty at
Sydney Water Police Court (14/G/’O5) to stealing
three small pieces of real estate enclosed in pots,
the property of ex-Lands Minister W. P. Crick.
He was sentenced to three months’ imprisonment
—a month for each jug-full of territory. Now, if
only his earth-hunger had been on a wholesale
scale, he might have borrowed £15,000 from a land
agent, who would thereupon persuade the Lands
Department to grant him a few miles of territory,
and all would have been well. But you mustn’t
covet land by the pot-full.
* * • *
The Fat party in Adelaide didn’t miss a
chance to make scornful remarks regarding the
N.S.W. and Vic. Labor M’s.P. who went across
to help local Laborites at the elections. The visi-
tors stood it all pretty well, though it was getting
on the nerves of some of them, when the last straw
arrived. It was in the shape of an anonymous
note addressed to N.S.W. leader McGowen, and
was to the effect that it was bad enough for
ignorant interlopers to come poking their tongues
into other people’s business, but anyhow they
might leave off riot and dissipation in the
hotel. It looked as though it might have
come in a disguised hand from the boss of
the establishment; wherefore McGowen, who is a
strict teetotal man and the stern father of a family,
hurriedly convened a caucus, and then shot out to
find the boss. McGowen’s indignation was at
about 190 degrees ; and, when they remembered
that McGowen had been a boiler-maker, and
hasn’t let his muscle grow flabby, and, moreover, is
a man of impulse, they waited for bits of a land-
lord to start bumping down the stairs. At
that point, the practical joker of the party
reckoned it was about time to streak after
McGowen, and implore him to be calm—the letter
had been his own little way of relieving the
monotony of life in Adelaide, where a joke doesn’t
happen naturally once a year.
* * * *
The Victorian honorary magistrate is a
humorist, in his own little way. The other day,
one of him, at Melbourne City Court, told a man
who had bitten a piece out of the arresting con-
stable, and who further owned up to 33 previous
convictions, that he was a cannibal and a savage,
and worse than a beast; also, that he wasn't fit to
be in the society of decent people. Then he fined
him £3 in all, and allowed him a week to pay.

“ Melb.” : I was fascinated as by the eyes
of a cobra di capello
in Little Collins-street
to-day. Behind plate-
glass there was a ven-
erable gentleman, look-
ing like a bishop in his
shirt-sleeves. He was
applying gold-leaf to
the new wooden mace
for the Australian
House of Representa-
tives, and I could not
help thinking of how
maces of much gold
take to themselves
wings and fly through
bluestone. They may
even find their way to
the Mint after melting.
Yes, a mace once went
out, even as sewing-
machine millionaire
Stanford’s favorite
spiritualist Bailey once
made a stone come in
through the walls of his
sumptuous drawing-room
in East Melbourne. So
the venerable gold-beater
was only engaged on a
work of art humbly emu-
lative of Raphael’s liTrans-
figuration”—transfiguring wood
to gold.
A recent escapade of “ Thistle Anderson ”
Fisher (who has caused Adelaide’s one grey hair to
stand on end with amazement because she says she
finds the hamlet slow) had ex-Mayor Cohen as
victim. She was staying at the South Australian
Hotel, and one night a steam-roller started
making unpleasant crunching noises in the street
below. “ Thistle,” or her husband, rang up the
little Mayor at 2 a.m., and asked him politely to
come and take his beastly nasty steam-roller
away. Cohen’s reply is said to have caused the
girl at the exchange great anguish. But why
should a lady who complains of a city because it
is too slow be so anxious to cultivate the slowest
of all virtues —that of being in bed and asleep ?
KNOWN TO THE FORCE.
“You seem to know all the police.”
“ I ought to I drive a motor.”
The democratic successes at the recent
S.A. elections badly upset the household of one
wealthy and aged Conservative. The day the
return of all five Labor members for the Central
District was assured, he wandered round his ex-
tensive grounds like a weeping ghost in black
clothes and pot hat, and addressed each old re-
tainer individually in this strain: “Grimes” (to
the coachman), “ Pve paid you £1233 9s. in wages,
and Giles” (to the gardener), “you’ve had £1126
11s. 6d. whilst you’ve been with me, and yet, I
suppose, you voted for those cursed Socialists.”
And his sad weeps watered the geranium patch.
Inside the house his wife, in trying to ascertain
who the cook and the housemaids voted for,
fell foul of the cook, who had once belonged to a
Young Women’s Guild and had learned public
speaking. Consequently it was a very much over-
done area of grilled steak that the Conservative
family sat down to that night.
The monumental Henty case, which has
been infesting the Victorian law courts since ’9B,
was last week amicably settled “to the satisfac-
tion of all parties.” In the beginning, Caroline
M‘Leod and Alice Hindson sued Henry Henty,
Charles King, H. P. Henty and Louisa Henty
for alleged breaches of trust in the administration
of the estate of the late Francis Henty, who died
in January, ’B9. The hearing before Chief J ustice
Madden occupied 47 days. His Honor’s judgment
took six hours to deliver ; but it was reversed by
the Full Court in much less time. Then there
were cross-appeals to the Privy Council, and the
ten barristers and six or eight firms of solicitors
were looking forward to them with an expensive
expression in their joint and several eye, when a
little friendly consultation put an end to the
squabble. Probably it could just as easily have
been settled seven years ago.
* * * *
The Law loves Precedents —old, dead Pre-
cedents, with fragments of decayed Costs clinging
to their bones. The other day in Melbourne
several learned lawyers argued lengthily, and a
learned Judge finally decided lengthily, that a
caveat lodged by a person who died before he
could take further steps still held good—“a caveat
never lapses.” So the Law decided that the order
nisi obtained against the deceased, after he was
dead, was still in force, and as deceased,
being still dead, had shown no cause why it should
not be made absolute, it was made absolute forth-
with. Costs to come out of the estate. Costs
never die.
Bent has been telling the students of the
Vic. State Continuation School, Melbourne, that
agriculturists produce something, whereas lawyers
produce nothing. Bent is always wrong. Lawyers
produce the title when Bent is selling suburban
allotments in land boom time.
“ Dico ” : The announced intention of
Vie. Education Minister Sachse to provide better
dwellings for country school teachers has sent a
wave of joy through humpy, hut, and dog-box.
The proposed new quarters are to consist of four
rooms, with bath-room,
linen - room, and
verandahs. We all
know what the
bath-room’s for
(we read a little,
you know), but
wha - wha - what’s
a linen - room '!
You will excuse
our ignorance,
perhaps, if 1
picture a typical
bush-school
dwelling for
you. Two
rooms, built
against the
school - house,
with no veran-
dah, even of
the most rudimen-
tary kind, and a
square tank and
black iron chimney
for exterior decora-
tion. Inside, un-
less the door is kept
wide open, the fire-
place smokes like
wet brimstone in
Sheol, in all winds
and weathers.
Everything light-
colored in th e
kitchen - cum - din-
ing-room-cum-par-
lor-cuni-other-
things is yellow,
and the bedroom
door has to be kept
tight-shut all day
to preserve the
bedding, etc., from
a like fate The
roof leaks near one
corner of the bed-
room, and in wet
weather small
streams run down the
wall and over the oilcloth.
The size of the rooms is not conducive to extrava-
gantly luxurious furniture. A small table, sofa, safe
and two chairs fill the kitchen-cum-etc,, and a
bed, washstand and “ portmanchew ” leave no
room for ping-pong in the bedroom. Set this
bleak house on the windy, black-mud Horsham
plains and the picture is complete. Yet men—
God reward their pluck ! —rear families in such
environment; and, if they’re extra plucky, and
flourish at the work, may get a transfer to a
three-roomed dwelling as encouragement. And
the Victorian country teacher who has a three-
roomed house doesn’t want to go to Heaven. He
is there already.

“A.G.”: Election rolls are a mine of
information. Have been doing some electioneer-
ing work (in S. Aus.), and have learned a lot as to
the occupations of various people. Pretty well
every female in some streets describes her occupa-
tion as “lady.” “Married,” “wife,” and
“widow” are the other favorite occupations.
“Gentlemen” are also pretty prevalent. Why
doesn’t some original elector say he is a man, and
be done with it ?

“Troobill ” : Sat on front seat of Coogee
car beside a brightly be-bloused and be-jewelled
matron and ditto maid, latter nursing a banjo-
box and roll of music. Pair conversed in low,
decorous tones until car reached junction with
racecourse line. Then matron, shooting out
gloved hand and stabbing atmosphere vigorously
several times, raised her voice proudly : “That’s
where the man was killed ! Just there! ” Banjo
maiden : “Were you in the first or second car ? ”
Matron: “The second.” Sweet one with the
luggage that soothes the savage breast: “What a
shame! I suppose
you couldn’t see it
all ? ” Mother per-
son, hurriedly: “Oh,
yes, dear, perfectly !
I got out, you know!”

A BALLAD OP BULLS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
John Hurley, M.L.A., who evidently know 9 little of
natural history, wasted the time of N.S.W. Assembly
lately by complaining that the School Paper described
ant-hills as reaching to a height of 20ft. or 25ft., and
being strong enough to support cattle. John laughed
greatly over his mare’s nest, and evidently regarded
himself as quite a humorous person.
In the New South Wales Assembly there was war, the
other day,
Through a speech on bulls and ant-hills by John Hurley,
M.L.A.
It appears the comic paper given school-boys as a treat
Said the ant-hills in the Transvaal stood as high as twenty
feet!
And upon these little mountains, which were thrown up
from the sand,
It was quite a common practice for the local bull to
stand.
Perched upon this elevation twenty feet above the plain,
Brigham Bull could stand surveying all the herd in his
domain !
As John Hurley laughed about it, and the House was
overjoyed,
The compilers of the paper felt exceedingly annoyed.
I don’t see much cause for anger, it appears a harmless
jest;
Maybe bulls could stand on ant-hills built as high as
Everest!
At the same time, up at Bathurst, up 2000 feet or more,
A “ bull ” by Sydney Smith made all the local people
roar.
“We can meet,” says he, “ opponents, and repel their
fierce attacks!
We can boldly face the people, for we have them at our
hacks 1 ”
All our statesmen are erratic ; seem to preach right off
the text;
And we seldom have a notion where they’re going to
break out next!
Politics are topsy-turvey ; circus-clowns have all the
pull!
And on our highest ant-hill standeth Smith’s amazing
bull.
N.S.W.
W. T. GOODGE.

The nigger-lovers of Australia love the
nigger in an impersonal fashion only. Some of
them in Queensland have been trying to establish
the right to deduct from the miserable £8 a year
which the Kanaka gets, his wages
for any period during which he
may be sick. Queensland
Fuil Court and the High
Court have both decided
against the exaction,
which therefore fails.
It’s an eloquent indica-
tion of the affection the
planters have for their
black brother Tommy.

From the West Australian (Perth), of
June 1 :
Roval Birthday Celebration.—ln honor of the birthday
of the Prince of Wales, and to celebrate Admiral Togo’s
great naval victory, a people’s entertainment will be held
on Monday evening next, the public holiday, in Queen’s
Hall. The programme will consist of a ballad concert,
elocutionary recitals, illustrated story songs, and one
hundred descriptive views, inclusive of some of Japan’s
greatest admirals and generals, and England’s notable
sons.
From the Morning Herald (Perth), of June 2
In honor of Admiral Togo’s naval victory, Queen’s Hall
will be decorated for the “People’s Entertainment” on
Monday night next with Japanese national emblems and
works of art. The pictures to be exhibited will include
portraits of Admiral Togo and his officers and their
men-of-war. The full programme of the entertainment
will appear in to-morrow’s issue.
Togo had apparently so swelled up in the interval
that he had quite obscured the Prince of Wales.
On June lit was Wales and Monkey ;on June 2
it was only Monkey.
Sydney City Council recently showed
itself up as a prize pig-headed body. In order to
widen Pitt-street, and give a decent tramway
entrance to the new railway station, the Works
Department requested the Council to forego 40ft.
of the Belmore markets site. Instead of rushing
to give what help it might towards the better-
ment of one of its principal thoroughfares, the
Council refused. Luckily the with
unusual backbone, put the matter into the Crown
Solicitor’s hands, and action is to be taken under
the Public Trust Act, whereby the existing
dedication will be revoked, and the 40ft. required
taken without compensation. Now the Council
threatens to spend the citizens’ money at law to
prevent the citizens’ own street being improved.
Melbourne City Council, instead of being
a model employer, seems to be pretty much of an
adept at sweating, at least as regards the City
Baths. Employees there, female and male, are
compelled to work, in a hot, steamy atmosphere,
eleven hours daily in summer and ten in winter
right through the week, and then have to put in
five hours every Sunday morning. This goes on
every day in the year, Christmas alone excepted.
Even the grasping Tramway Co. could never beat
this record ; and it at least gives its employees a
fortnight’s holiday a year—the clerical employees
on full pay. A council slave who needs a spell
has to do without pay and find a substitute, for
whom he or she is expected to be responsible.
Why the Council should act thus no one knows;
it treats its electrical employees decently enough.

The ghost of the old, dead war still crawls
like a blind worm on this country’s premises.
Two things happened last week to recall the
painful event. A Commission began its sittings
in Sydney to decide a claim of £70,000 tor extra
pay, made by a number of allegedly betrayed and
swindled cohentingenters ; and Nelson, the St.
Bernard dog, which used to be the pride and
glory of the Australian farm-burners, was found
in Adelaide, a mere skeleton, hungry, deserted,
and kicked out.

A squabble among the numerous Lutheran
churches of S.A. has just been settled in the
Supreme Court; after half-an-hour’s hearing a
conference was agreed to, and the parties settled
their differences in an ante-room, lhe six lawyers
engaged congratulated themselves (by Josiah
Symon as spokesman) that “lawyers are always
ready to be peacemakers.” Clause 4of the agree-
ment reads : “Plaintiffs’ costs to be paid out of
fund ; defendant to have £75 towards his costs out
of fund.” The lawyer isn’t a very cheap peace-
maker. . „
“The Mussel” writes about that ever-
lasting bore, the oldest inhabitant:
The mention of the oldest inhabitant conveys the im-
pression of an elderly person wtm has dragged a blame-
less and sober existence, and has done nobody any harm
and nobody any good. This is quite true. The secret
of longevity is selfishness. When you read m the
daily press that Ann Maria Jones still retains all
her faculties, and can read the Hop Bitters adver-
tisement without spectacles, although she was 107
years old in the middle of next week, you marvel
much, but with mixed feel-
ings. What has Ann Maria
Jones done ? Nothing—ab-
solutely nothing. Through
pure selfishness she has
lived to the boredom of
others. Did she do any-
thing for mankind? No-
thing! Some say she reared
a large family of children.
Did she rear it for altruis-
tic reasons? No ! Pure
selfishness ! They would
keep her when she grew
old. Moreover she had
drifted into a habit of hav-
ing a large family and
couldn’t get out of it. She
lived a calm emotionless
life, and did nothing but
give a journalist a half-
day’s work with her dream-
like impression of two
generations ago. Now,
take the man who dies
young. (Always remember
the good die young.) My
theory is—that there is a
certain amount of goodness
crowded into the human
machine. The man who
lets it out quickest{is the
best man. Some people
carry a larger cargo of
goodness than
others, and there-
fore it takes longer
to unload them.
That is why some
good men live to be
old. But, taken as
a general rule, a
person that lives to
a great age has only
drag ged out a long series
of colorless years. Ann
Maria Jones at 107 —Ann
Maria roosting uselessly on the
grey pinnacle of all those hoary
years, looking; back over a flat desert
of decades with no crag-or pinnacle of joy or drink or excite-
ment or adventure, to break their monotony—with no
tower or pyramid of unusualness to diversify the dead
level of the ordinary cussedness of things—what, after all,
is the good of Ann Maria Jones ? She is a toothless monu-
ment, aged 107—an atom rolling nowhere over the wastes
of chronology for 107 years, and just as remarkable n
history as the one fly that roosts on 107 leagues of wire-
fence on a hot day. The oldest inhabitant is mostly only
another name for the deadest inhabitant.

The number of subjects that a really
energetic Technical Education department could
find to teach would probably be three or four
thousand. Most of them wouldn’t have any more
real connection with technical education than the
Dry Dog has with the St. Bernard, but that
apparently doesn’t matter. In Germany and the
other places where they make the things that
Australia ought to be making, technical education
is mostly either a preface or a supplement to a
factory j in hT.S.IVi, while 25 students are learning
the principles of iron-founding, 3375 are learning
how to typewrite the beautiful letter ordering
another roll of calico from Manchester and another
ton of pig-iron from Belgium; and that isn’t
counting in the 1082 more who are learning to
typewrite the beautiful letter in the State schools.
Also, apropos the command to the young man to
go on the land, it is worth noting that the young
man to the extent of a whole nine is learning
farriery, while the elocution classes have 346
students. Presumably the 346 are learning how
to tell the other nine to go on the land, and be the
backbone of the country, and develop the great
natural resources.

If the chief business of woman is to produce emotion m

For mv own part, I hold that Nature will produce all

relief from her present crippling dress.

A story told to the Broken Hill police by
a doctor of the town might have come out of a
penny dreadful only that the p.d. has to give some
consideration to the probabilities. According to the
doctor, a man named Baker called on him asked
to see him privately, and then offered lnm foCI and
promised £5O more when the 30b was completed,
if he would kill Mrs. Baker. The lady was to
have a small dental operation, under laughing-gas,
and the doctor was asked to give her, instead of
that harmless anßesthetic, a deadly dose of chloro-
form. Baker stands his trial charged with incit-
ing to murder. A second count should charge him
with being the biggest fool m Australia, and a
jury might investigate the truth of the two charges
simultaneously.
