You have to be very careful in Melbourne
nowadays. A slightly sober individual whose
“ fan tods ” seemed to mostly run to tears, stopped
in the middle of Queen’s Bridge, the other day,
and, leaning over the parapet, wept copiously into
the Yarra. He was promptly run m for insult-
ing behavior.”
The moving spectacle of a rejected lover
lumping in his last agony doesn t always move the
tender heart of woman. A Ballarat lover, having
fallen out with his best girl, sent her a touching
letter, and received no reply. He called at her
house, and heard from her brother that the girl
was aweary of him, and there was “no hope.’
Whereupon he took from his pocket a small bottle
of “toothache cure”—carbolic jelly, to wit and
publicly swallowed the contents. Then, as the
tooth remedy began to burn the skin off his in-
terior, he did 237 astounding contortions all over
the verandah and the front garden. Interested
passers-by stopped and watched the circus.
More of the family came out and viewed
his transports for a space, then decided
that, as he didn’t seem able to keep still,
and was wrecking the garden with his feet
and his head, they had better take him to the
hospital, where he could be strapped down, and
pumped out, or cut open, or something. Next
day he explained to the magistrates that he did it
to frighten the girl, and they let him go, on paying
the cab-fare. But apparently he didn't frighten
the girl. She seems to have only taken a languid
interest in his calisthenics.

Significant of the change of Australian
sentiment in regard to the S’African war, that
Sydney City Council only agreed by a very
narrow majority to give house-room m the Town
Hall for some rifles which were offered as trophies
of that raid. Even the Gaud Mayor was against
housing the guns, and said “ Let them go to the
Museum.”
High Court C. J. Griffith has positively re-
fused to postpone a case to suit senior counsel, stat-
ing that when at the Bar he never asked to have
cases postponed. “And you know, continued his
Honor with a smile, “we must give the junior
Bar a chance.” Apropos of which, a junior once
made a similar application to Chief-Justice James
Martin of N.S.W. The Judge refused, and told
the junior to go on, adding significantly, “ Lost
opportunities are never regained,’ and that junior
went on and got on. In another instance, before
Justice Faucett, this amiable man blandly told a
junior that at the Irish Bar such applications
were never made ; if the senior were absent the
junior went on without him, and frequently did
better than the heavily paid man.
The sorry dyspeptic, submerged in his gloom,
Who feels most dejected and fit for his tomb ;
The poor hypochondriac who wanders in woe,
With no one to speak to and nowhere to go,
Whom no consolation nor comfort can please,
Who maintains he’s a martyr to every disease,
bet them aid their digestion and keep their blood pure
And recruit upon Woods’ Great Peppermint Cure.

SAID ALFRED DEAKIN
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Mr. Deakin, M.H.R., has consented to speak on “The
Citizenship of the Young Man” at the anniversary cele-
bration of the Melbourne Young Men’s Christian Associa-
tion, in the Melbourne Town Hall, on Thursday evening,
June 22.—Argus, 10/6/’O5.
“ My dear young friends, how pleased I am
To see you here to-night!
I know how much you all hate sham —
I know you know I can’t talk “ flam ”
And never utter “ skite.”
You know with virtues you are reekin’,”
Said Alfred Deakin.
“ You are the backbone of the nation,
You follow after right;
Without your good Association —
Your meekness and mild resignation—
The State would come to sheer a-hem !-ation ;
You are its Guidin’ Light—
If you understand my way of speakin’,”
Said Alfred Deakin.
“ Rum and beer, in scorn you hold them.
Ah, excellent young men !
Your manifold virtues —who has told them ?
Let all the wondering world behold them,
And to its had old bosom fold them !
The model citizen
Is you ! You’re just what we’ve been seekln’,”
Said Alfred Deakin.
Proud indeed am I to address you—
'Tis a pleasing sight!
Boast of the land that’s glad to possess you —
Joy of the parents who pat and caress you—
Typical citizens, bless you, bless you !
You never flirt or fight:
Nobody’s like you from Perth to Pekin,”
Said Alfred Deakin.
“ Shun hypocrisy, girls, and races,
Keep to the path I beg ;
Grow in complacency all your graces—
Never be seen in envious places—
Let religion shine in your faces —
Beam like a new-laid egg.
(And now I’ve done puling your leg,
I’ll go and leave you clappin’ and shriekin’,”)
Said Alfred Deakin.

THE VARNISHED BOARD
A WOMAN’S LETTER.
„ Sydney, June 20, 1905.
My Dear Arini.—
If I were sure that the weather liked to see its
name m print, I would write an atmospheric par.
which would be a swarm of golden adjectives. So
faultless are the days, so llorentine the nights,
that the very conscientious ones amongst us don’t
quite like taking all this weather for nothing.
We feel that we owe at least 2d. for it. A place
■ ?£ e you may £ et a su P er b view of the weather
is Westmead, beyond Parramatta. Long green
heaving paddocks, trees, rose-gardens, and wide-
eyed kine, and miles and miles of weather. Home
for Destitute Boys is at Westmead. Last week
a large party, which was to include Commandant
Cordon, went up there to do some inspecting.
Certain Manst Brothers have charge of the Home,
and some 95 boys are there. Those over thirteen
attend school during the mornings and work at
trades the other half day. They are trained to
grow things, which is good for the boys; and
tailoring, broom-making, boot-making and print-
ing are taught and the goods sold, which is a good
thing for Australia. Home is not subsidised by
Government, and gets along by its own efforts and
by private charity. Recently its annual ball
occurred successfully. Mrs. F. B. Freehill and the
hon. organising secs., Messrs. H. G. Quinlan and
C. C . Hepburn, are to be congratulated on the
success of the tableaux “ Song of the Trades,” the
words of the song written by Roderic Quinn.
Talking of charity balls, the Fresh Air League’s
always a wed-managed affair, realised some
C2OO, and St. Vincent’s about £360. The dance
up by the Mayoress of Mosman, in aid of the
Childrens Hospital, brought £l2O to the fund.
On Aug. Ist Mrs. Allan Taylor, Mayoress of
Sydney, will give a ball —fancy-dress and other-
wise m aid of the Children’s Hospital. The
generous giver will defray all expenses of hall,
lghting, supper, etc., and the ss. paid for each
ticket will thus go in full to prop up an institution
which calls for support at the top of its voice.
Sydney artists have sworn by all the primary
colors that their projected ball is going to be the
most sportive thing of its kind ever set rolling.
ij of jT? livin S> moving pictures will be The
Haunted Czar. Conspirators will dog the poten-
tate all over the ball-room, making sensational
attempts on his life. Finally they will succeed in
blowing him (and themselves) up, and will die
s °me immortal phrase, as for instance:
i amt on, boys—this is our last brush.” Large
tubes of paint will be represented by tall men
and half tubes by short ones. “Flake white”
will be a giant. After supper, “ when all the
St™, people have left,” and when the confetti
w ?f} * s Pod the dancing, masks and dominoes
wull be donned by the flexible ones who
remain, and the carnival will start. It
has an attractive sound. The frolic will be
given in conjunction with the Woollahra ladies in
aid of the Children’s Hospital. Mrs. Paul, one of
the artists, is hon. sec.
Literary man wants to buy or borrow old love
letters no names used.” An advt. to that effect
recently appeared in S.M. Herald. What a
ke e n desme there is to know how the other fellow
and fellowess told one another of the emotion that
once boiled at the end of the pen ! And as one
reads the faded inky trail of that emotion how
dead it all seems—like cold tea found unexpectedly
on a mantel-shelf. One of the few series of love
letters of famous persons that can stand the test
are those of Abelard and Heloise. And possibly
the hallowing effect of time has something to do
with the fact that one reads them with reverence
instead of with laughter. Again perhaps they
have been re-written and improved. The
love-letters of Juliette (I forget the lady’s
surname), the mistress of Victor Hugo, now
on the literary market, simply exude an
intense, overmastering and jealous love. But one
teels rather sorry for Victor, so insistent is the
lady that she must have every spare minute of his
time. He must have felt that he hadn’t a chance
to do his hair. But dreary as old love-letters may
,e > the novel that sells and circulates with the
greatest rapidity is the well told love-story.
Letters may tell their writers’ story with genuine
truth and passion, but, curiously enough, the
localisation and identification seem to destroy the
romance. And probably if Heloise’s letters had
been read in a breach of promise case, the Jud°-e
who came to court in an ox-wagon would have
made bad jokes about them just like the
heavy humorist on the bench to-day. A love
letter, written on clay about 2200 b.c., has
just been discovered in Chaldea—the place that
Abraham felt he had no further use for. It
very like what might be written in Sydney a.d.
r j ’> r , lover ex P r esses intense anxiety for his
"wys health (her name signifies “little ewe”)
and begs her to come soon (to Babylon) and make
h im _ happy. Lovers, ancient and modern, seem
tond of sheep in some form. In Sydney push
society a girl is often alluded to kindly as mutton.
One of the showy, beautifully-dressed girls who
act the English governesses in “The Cingalee”
fell on the stage stairs one Saturday night in that
w “d, carnivalish scene where they drive Doran
with ribbon reins. Her feet slipped from under
her and jerked upward, and she seemed to arrive
on the back of her neck. It looked an ugly con*
cussion, but the audience didn’t wait to learn if
the girl’s back or neck were broken. It just
screamed with mirth. A cascade of white lace
frills and shapely, black-hosed knees, when in-
voluntarily exhibited, is so funny that it turns a
smart assemblage into a mob.
“When in doubt write about hats ” was the
motto of a one-time acquaintance of mine who
ran a society column. She was a woman of ideas,
and lived in theoretical pursuit of the Perfect
Hat. And she pursued it, as a general rule, in an
eighteenpenny straw that had been handed down
to her by a remote ancestor—it looked as if it had
been handed down from a tree at a time when
ancestors lived up in leafy haunts. In dull
seasons she imagined hats; later on she described
the hats of her dreams as confections she had
seen walking down the street, and they filled
women with envy, and made them fly round the
town looking for the article which was only a
vision. She had a theory, especially in
elopement and divorce cases, that hats
influenced character for good or evil. For
example, in one instance the headgear of
the adventuress consisted of languorous, lovely
things, the colors tender yet bright, with a dash
of flaming red at the side and an aberration in the
shape of the brim. I tried on a hat of that kind, and
felt instantly a desire to be a harem, or to forge
the name of Bland Holt’s leading hero for two
millions. In the same ease the hat of the wronged
wife suggested a big, cinder-colored mushroom
shading a walnut. I tried on a similar toadstool,
whereupon I felt myself projected into a drying-
ground, with a spare clothes-peg in my mouth,
hanging out clothes to dry, and I felt that my
sphere was to make a four-roomed house miserable
u stead y eeltivation of all the virtues which cause
the domestic cat to run up the chimney when it
sees you coming.
This psychologist knew so much about her
favorite subject that no end of advice about com-
mencing business as a hat expert came to her.
At last a rich old gentleman who breathed ster-
torously, and who had a facade to his figure and
a beautiful plate-glass expanse of shops to be let,
came in person and offered to start her as a mil-
liner. She was wearing her most bashed and most
handed-down sailor hat, and she said “ Ugh !”, to
the fa§aded-gentleman’s proposal. “I despise
any hat that . costs over two shillings and isn’t
plain straw with a black ribbon round it. I know
about as much of the fashioning and trimming of
the fashionable hat as you know of starvation."
Ihen she went on writing intimately of the style
and intellectual significance of the new five-guinea
models.
Osborne clans have possession of Sydney these
days. They streamed in last week to see their
relative, Mr. James Osborne, play Beaucaire.
Iwo rows of the Royal dress circle were Cur-
randooleyed, and lesser relatives by marriage
had a large reserve in the stalls. As for Sydney
Society—you wouldn’t have found any Pointer or
Kose Bayleaf under 17 in her home on Saturday
night. Curiosity to see how “Jimmie” would
play the Frenchman had brought them all, in-
cluding the Northcotes, into the Royal. Whole
Osborne species have great admiration for the
beautiful American who has become “Mrs.
Jimmie.” Before starting the July season in
Adelaide she and he and brother Norman are
going up to spend a few days at their future home,
thirty miles from Currandooley. House is only a
little cottage, and to hear Maud say “I’m going
to do the cooking,” sounds like an angel threaten-
ing to boil an egg in her halo.
It is said that Lord Jersey’s proposed visit to
bydney at the end of the year is a preliminary
canter for, the . post of Governor-General. Lord
-Northcote’s private business will require him in
England early in 1906, and Jersey, it is assumed,
comes out to take the measurements of the billet and
see how it will fit him.
„rn, have received an indignant letter signed merely
Ihirty-Second Degree Mason,” in which the
sender says things about another thirty-second
de r o, r ee Mason in reference to the Masonic ball.
ihirty-becond Degree Mason” conceals his
identity behind a typewriter and gives no address,
but he asserts that he is one of the ball committee
and he violently calls the other thirty-second-degree
Mason a bagman. I am informed, through
the telephone, that there are 33 degrees of
Masonry and that there is no 33rd man in Aus-
tralia. King Edward, I have heard, is a thirty-
thirder, and lives at a Masonic height which gives
him a fearful cold when he is up, while the drop
would be fatal if he let go and came down. It has
also been explained to me, on bad authority, that
when kings gallop across the ceiling on the goat
while being initiated, the goat is chloroformed to
keep it quiet. Humbler people ride it in the wild
state.

Arthur Adams was entertained by a few of our
artists at a funny supper on Saturday night.
Really interesting part of the entertainment
was the improvised orchestra. Fred Leist
turned up with an extraordinary instrument—
a kerosene tin (all there except the oil)
in which a broom-handle had been firmly fixed,
f rom top of handle was stretched one piano wire
over a wad of paper and a cork. If Signor Hazon
heard the music Leist bowed out of that eccen-
tricity he d want it for the Amiateur Orchestral,
bid Long, too, got pretty music out of an inven-
tion of his the thigh-bone of one of the Old
Masters of Australian painting.
A recent Anglo-Australian wedding in London
was that of Colonel H. C. Money and Mrs. Cour-
tenay Thompson, of S.A. The Colonel’s two
grandfathers were Admiral Halstead and the Earl
of Exmouth, and the present Exmouth is his
cousin. _ The lady is the daughter of Mr. Francis
L. Davis, of S.A., and niece of Mr. and Mrs. J.
Whear Roberts, of Sydney.
Another recent marriage in London was that of
Mrs. Fleming, once of Sydney, to a Major
Cummings.
A gathering of city missioners in Sydney has
drawn attention to a Ballarat system of Christian
laundry work which benefits the washers, not
the institution where Christian washing is taken
in. They get paid ordinary washing wages,
and, furthermore, divide the profits pro rata,
according to the number of days they have
worked. This gives them a better chance of
feeling good than is offered to the Marthas
and Magdalenes who have to toil eternally at
the charity tub in consideration of bare board
and lodging.
The long glove is worrying its wearer. When
she sits down to knife and fork she slips the glove
hand off and rolls it up into a lump on her wrist.
An inartistic sight. So someone wants someone
else to invent a glove the hand of which will fasten
on, “ or something,” to the rest of the long article.
In other words, the ideal glove is to be in two
pieces—a long funnel and a hand.
Loungers that infest the street corners have got a
nickname —the “Dipper ’’—for the girl who dresses
in a style that she thinks is the apex of fashion—
her figure and the cut of her garments making for
a leaning-tower-of-Pisa effect. “ Here’s another
Dipper,” I heard, and along came a nice-looking
ballet girl. She wore a short grey tweed skirt,
longer in front than at the back, a white silk
blouse with a grey kid belt, its buckle pulled well
down. The skirt was padded at the back below
the waist-line, accentuating the canting forward of
the body. Her hat lurched well over her eyes,
and her hair was brushed up from the back and
shot out over her forehead. Very high heels and
(possibly) the kind of corset that makes its wearer
oblique completed a very sad phase of girl. As
each high heel struck the pavement the shock
perceptibly jarred the obliquity’s spine.

J®* I®® B6 at the Pleasances’ Juvenile hall, last
Thursday. Gorgeous decorations and illumina-

scene. The grand parade of aldermen, showing

funeral pace, lengthening the foolishness no end.
lhe tail of the procession, as a grand climax, in-
troduced the hostly pair (himself in vestment and
brass chains) and the dais contingent—the
same old contingent of elderly shoulders,
diamond stars and silken trail. In the ab-
sence of Yice-Regals, Premier Bent waddled
up. The bill of fare for excited galleryites
was quite an entertaining one. Several old and
new measures—the old making for dream-move-
ment, the new for a sort of clock-tick-quick-beat
hwistle—' were gone through under the sharp eyes
of Mrs. W. Green and her daughter. There
wasn’t a hitch or a sneeze from start to finish
of the minuet. A cotillon des Jleurs and a
Polonaise march provided a fine spectacle.

“Several select souls were bidden by Mr. Laver
to shake the hands of the Hugo Heermanns at
Menzies last Monday. Madame Heermann has
quick, discerning talk. Spanish blood runs in her
vems, and she has theßuenos Ayres taste forclothes.
She declares that the silvery green of Australian
gums was a new delight to her eyes. ‘I have
never heard that wonderful treer-tone attributed
to your gums. What a beauty to go unpro-
claimed ! Oh, I’m going to find out all the
charms by-and-bye of this great place.’ Yet
how many Australians notice the dark silver
leafage of the great old gum ?
“There is an interesting young person travel-
ling With the Heermanns—a Miss Pickersgyll.
(She s very strong on that ‘y ’ in the tail-end of
the name.) She has a highly-accomplished manner
and fine hands. The latter are said to be ex-
tremely musical.
“Society, struck by the grand manner of
Madame Hugo Hermann, rolled up to her hus-
band’s initial bow-flourishing, on Saturday, in
heavy numbers. What an asset a showy wife is
to a simple genius who wears whiskers and his
mane clipped ! Since Melba’s ovation there has
been nothing like the excitement occasioned among
the musical by this artist of the unimpressive
appearance and emotionless manner. It isn’t
that he is intensely and passionately calm. He
simply makes music without fuss. Divine music,
almost! Still, the women were not satisfied.
They were irritated at the fiddler’s German
phlegm. What is the use of a genius who doesn’t
switch his coat-tails over the music-sheets, or
angrily roll his eyes at the grey atmosphere, or
comb his mane with impatient fingers, or fling
himself on and off the platform ?
“Lady Mary Sackville, a chum of Tasmanian
Governor Strickland’s wife, descended almost un-
noticed upon Melbourne on Thursday. The ever-
vigilant dowager of Cliveden bore her off uncom-
plainingly to the Mayoral Fancy Hop that night,
which marked the visitor’s one public appearance.
Her black garb and her smoothed-back brown hair
and very still manner went unregarded in the
general fuss of the show. On Saturday Mary set
off again with her maid for Tasmania. Coogee
passengers marvelled at the small amount of tra-
velling personal effects of the thoughtful tripper.
Few feminines in the forties can manage to keep
up any sort of an appearance on less than a ton of
movable furniture. Perhaps a handle to one’s
name is a partial substitute for clothes. On the
other hand, an extra amount of common sense
may negative a variety of apparel, at once trouble-
some and costly.

“ A set of moneyed speculators about the local
Stock Exchange are in glee over the break-up of
a threatened alliance between one of their number
and a chorus chick. Just as things looked as if
they were going to drift into matrimony, one male
friend after another prevailed on the man to hesi-
tate before such folly (?). He hesitated each time,
and all these combined hesitations made a stop-
page.
“Ship-board impressions of Miss Nance O’Neil
all agree as to her sociable spurts and large silent
lapses. A man tells me that the actress reminded
him, when lounging on deck through a warmish
noon, of a large, beautiful kitten—a very large
kitten—in a quiet period of tropical doze. She
was so smooth and sleek and excessively indolent.
“The Yank methods of tear-raising differ.
Tittell Brune manifests her passionate frenzy by
directing her eyes and her language to heaven
and dragging handfuls of hair back from her
tragic brow. That spells wholesale abandon of
self. (Tossing one’s hair is a frightfully frenzied
act in woman’s eyes.) Nance O’Neil takes a
central position on the stage, turns her back to the
lights and stretches her long arms till they are as
rigid as two rails, and you almost expect to see a
train run along them. That Sandow-like opera-
tion twice moved Saturday’s audience to a des-
perate struggle with audible sobs. It was the
Magda method of expressing a despair so supreme
that she wouldn’t care if all her spare clothes were
burned to the ground.
A friend of mine lately shot at Bathurst an
eagle-hawk measuring seven feet four inches from
wing-tip to wing-tip. The bird brought itself
within close range of the enemy through coming
down to seize his toy terrier in its talons. “ A
man who would shoot so huge a bird for trying to
steal so small a dog would kill a horse for steahng
his caterpillar ” was the comment made by another
man on being told of the incident.

SKIN SORES.
DR. WILLIAMS PINK PILLS CURE AGONISING ECZEMA.
«* A BARBER shaved me too close with a
J\ dirty razor, and gave me the worst
sample of Eczema my doctor had ever
seen,” said Mr. Joseph Merrix, 43
Stanley-street, off Hyde Park, Sydney. “ The
disease spread from my face all over my body, and
it was two years before I got the poison out of my
blood. I was a terrible sight to look at, and nearly
went mad with the burning itching of it. The
best doctors failed utterly to stop it spreading. A
great skin specialist attended me, but finally it got
so bad that I had to go to the Sydney Hospital.
After six months’ treatment, the Eczema was
worse than ever. After everything else failed,
my brother made me give Dr. Williams’ Pink
Pills a fair trial. To my surprise ten boxes drove
all the festering matter out of my blood. The
sores healed up, and to-day I have a clear, smooth,
healthy skin —thanks to Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills
for Pale People.
“ The doctor told me I got the Eczema from the
barber all fight—but he said my blood must have
been in a bad state to begin with. That’s what
made the Eczema spread so quickly along my
arms, across my body, and down my legs. I wa«
one mass of itching, oozing sores. Every day I
could feel a fresh lot of small, hard pimples push-
ing their way up through the skin. Then the skin
would crack, and the sores would burn and itch
till I was almost crazy. Every inch of my body
was either covered with the dry scales of the old
sores, or there were new sores festering and itch-
ing. I could have torn my flesh to pieces. Some-
times it felt as if something were crawling under
the skin. Often I was ashamed to sit down to
meals in public. I was such a hideous sight. My
face was covered with hard, lumpy cakes of dirty
skin.
“ My legs were especially bad and swelled up
terribly. At last I couldn’t walk. I had to throw
up my billet and take to bed. No one can imagine
how weak I was. There didn’t seem to be an
atom of strength in any muscle. My nerves broke
down and my legs were always twitching. Night
after night I was kept awake. It was a treat to
get an hour’s sleep at a stretch. I got so thin that
my bones were actually coming through my skin.
To make matters worse, a kind of Rheumatism
settled in my joints—and all this time the Eczema
kept spreading.
“ I went to the best skin specialist,” Mr. Merrix
added. “He did everything in his power for me
—but could not cure me. When the treatment
at the Sydney Hospital failed, I gave up hope. I
was so down-hearted that I often prayed for
death. And there’s no doubt I would have died
out of sheer weakness and agony if my brother had
not persuaded me to give Dr. William’s Pink Pills
a fair trial.
“The first two boxes of Dr. Williams’ Pink
Pills did nothing but give me a wonderful appe-
tite. After the third box my skin didn’t burn and
itch so much, and I picked up heart. Slowly but
surely, I gained in strength and weight. The ter-
rible feeling of weakness left my legs, and the
swelling all went down. I took eight boxes before
all the sores disappeared—and then I took another
two boxes just to make sure that I would be cured
for good. By that time my skin was perfectly
clean and smooth, and I have never had a sign of
Eczema since.”
There could be no greater proof about the power
of Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills as a blood medicine.
The only possible way to cure Eczema is to sweep
out of the blood all the itching, festering poison
that causes these sores. Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills
alone can do that because they actually make new
blood. They do just that one thing, but they do
it well. That is the simple scientific reason why
they cure all blood diseases, like anasmia, indiges-
tion, headaches, backache, lumbago, rheumatism,
sciatica, neuralgia, nervousness, St. Vitus’ dance,
asthma, general weakness, and the special ailments
of growing girls and women. Remember, Dr.
Williams’ Pink Pills don’t act on the bowels.
They will cure no disease that is not caused by
bad blood. Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale
People can be had at all storekeepers, or ordered
by mail from the Dr. Williams’ Medicine Co. r
Sydney—2s. 9d. a box, six boxes 15s. 3d., post
free. Medical advice given free.
PERSONAL ITEMS.
T. H. Bath, the new Colonial Secretary of
Westralia, is now the youngest Minister in the
Commonwealth; he was born at Hill End
(N.S.W.) in 1875.
C. C. Lance, who resigns N.S.W. Com-
mercial Agency in London, has been an engineer
in South Africa, a flour-miller in Victoria, and a
butter and milk expert in N.S.W. He is still
well under 50.
The High Commissionership of Maoriland
definitely goes to W. P. Reeves, who by his ability
and his national sympathies is a notable excep-
tion to the usual run of Stinking Fish Agents-
General in London.
Besides his book on the Arab horse, stern-
faced old Justice Boucaut, of S. Aus., who when
he passed 70 became almost a Draco on the bench,
has a boys’ book in hand, and just about ready for
the printer. But he thinks he’ll stop at those two.
The N.S.W. Government has declared Mr.
T. M. Slattery’s seat in the Legislative Council
“ vacant,” and he has been wiped off the register.
Slattery is employed now in the tailors’ shop at
Darlinghurst gaol and is a model of good
behavior.
W. Uden, Semaphore signalman (S.A.),
just retired on reaching the 70 years limit, has
signalled the arrival and departure of vessels
since ’57. Said that he invariably gave the correct
name of the steamer as soon as he caught sight of
its smoke down the gulf.
Cremorne Point (Sydney Harbor), where the
foreshores titles keep Syd. speculators curious, was
first passed over to the father of one-time Premier
Jack Robertson, as a grant. From the Robert-
sons it passed to the Milsons in 1853, £4OOO being
the purchase-money.
Dr. Nathan Cobb, pathologist of N.S.W.
Agricultural Department, resigns after a sixteen
years’ tenure of office to take up work under the
Stars and Stripes. The Agricultural Bureau at
Washington has long angled for his services, the
latest offer being a post in Manilla at £825 to
begin with.
An individual who got three years at
Invercargill (M.L.) for playing Togo with a
woman’s furniture, was a versatile sort of crimi-
nal : he had convictions for stone-throwing, sacri-
lege, drunkenness, conducting a lottery, consort-
ing with thieves, and bashing a lady. A sort of
Execrable Crichton !

The member of the S.A. Legislature who
received the highest percentage of the votes
polled in his district was Labor member Archibald
(Port Adelaide), the proportion being 65'43 per
cent. The member who polled the highest number
of votes was Labor member Coneybeer (Torrens),
vl o received 12,543 votes. Labor leader Price
was a good second with 12,318 in the same district.
These figures show exactly how influential was
the Tory crusade of George Reid in S.A.

The hurried departure of N.S.W. Governor
Rawson threw the Governor’s “work” on Chief -
Justice Darley again. Mr. Darley has been too
ill to attend to his Supreme Court duties, and his
appointment as Governor, under the circum-
stances, advertises the State Governor’s job as a
useless affair which an invalid can attend to in
between his medicine.
“ B.” : Apropos Lorimer Fison’s lift
on to the British Pension List (£l5O a year).
Civil List pensions in England are some-
times deserved, sometimes not. I re-
member a poet who had spent 40 years
writing desolate bosh, and then
Prime Minister Gladstone placed
him on the Civil List. Cham-
berlain and Disraeli both
denouuced the appoint-
ment, and Gladstone’s
explanation gave away
the whole fraud of
the system. He said
he had never read a
line of the poet’s
manufacture, but
that the memorial in
favor of the pension
was signed by the
Deputy - Lieutenant
of the County, by
the Protestant
Bishop, the Catholic
Bishop, and the Pres-
byterian Moderator,
and “although I have
since learned that
none of these ever
read a line of his
verses either, yet I
considered the me-
morial good enough to
go upon.” If Australia
is ever to do anything
in the literary subven-
tion line, it should take
the British Civil List as
a bad example to avoid.
Died recently, at the
age of 84 years, Mrs. Char-
lotte Foot, relict of John
Foot, tanner and currier, who
in the late forties carried on busi-
ness in Castlereagh-street, Sydney,
below Liverpool-street. Mrs. Foot for many years
did business in Market-street as a straw bonnet
cleaner, but as straw bonnets —excepting in the
Salvarmy—have long since gone out of fashion,
the industry, like the old worker, is dead. Sixty
years ago there were 29 straw bonnet makers in
Sydney. There is not one now. The “Army”
headgear is imported.
J. Zimri Sellar, of Adelaide, who had been
trying for S. Aus. Assembly regularly for about
30 years, during all which time he was the State’s
most active politician, had never been inside Par-
liament House until after he was elected the other
day. He announced long back that he would never
go till he went as a member, and he kept his word,
though he had to wait till he was 74 before he
could walk up the marble steps.
A NATURAL FINANCIER.
HUSBAND: "I’m glad to see you have
kept the household expenses down this time.”
H ER: “ Yes, you said you were a bit short, so I
asked the tradesmen not to send their bills in
till next month.”
Gilbert Probyn Smith, reported dead in
Melbourne at the age of 52, was in the Indian
Mutiny as an infant. He was in a state of
mutiny against things in general most of his time
after, but between his alarms and incursions he
picked up great quantities of general information,
and he absorbed languages till he held so many
that there wasn’t space in him for even one more
dialect. His father was a major in the Engineers.
“G.P.5.,” as he used to sign himself, did much
work, mostly of the sensational kind, on the Aus-
tralian daily press.

The Royal Commission which opened New
South Wales Parliament was composed of two
Australians and one nearly so. Frank Suttor is
the son of a Bathurst landowner ; John Hughes,
of a George-street (Sydney) grocer; and W. J.
Trickett, who was born at Gibraltar, son of an
official in the Sydney Mint.
A. A. P. Tighe, who died recently at
Glebe Point, Sydney, aged 7b, was for 75 years an
Australian. For a time he was in political life,
and for 27 days in 1868 was Postmaster-General of
James Martin’s Ministry. Docker had been
P.M.G., but on Parlies’ resignation as Chief
Secretary, Docker was promoted, and Tighe came
in.
About a pioneer :
An interesting identity of the Richmond River has just
gone under —pioneer George Edward Cole. In 1859 (when
myalls dogged the traveller) he took 1000 head of bul-
locks from the Richmond to the Murrumbidgee, bis fee
being £1 per head. The trip occupied about four months.
In 1861, while managing Bonalbo station (R.R.), he
married Christina Duncan, of Woodenbung (().), under
unique circumstances. Preparations had been made for
the wedding at Unumgar, but a difficulty cropped up
when the clergyman (Rev. J. VV. Wilson) arrived from
Ipswich, his license not being valid in N.S.W. So the
whole party set out on the 15-mile ride to the border, the
last three miles along the mailman’s track, up the steep,
scrubby side of Mount Lindsay. Under the lofty plateau
that crowns the mount, the cavalcade dismounted, and,
hitching their horses to the bushes, assembled on a little
clear spot by the scrub, and the ceremony began. When
it was about half through, the party found a horde of
leeches swarming up its collective leg. The rest of the
service was rushed through ; then the party dispersed
round the corner in two sections, one of which swore and
the other screamed. Cole afterwards became manager
of Bermunda station, near Gladstone, and later (1869)
took up (carrying on the Grafton-Casino line, carriage
being then 20s. and 30s. per cwt.
The death of Ratu Lala, chief of the
Thakaundrove district, in Fiji, recalls that his
ancestor of 35 years ago, Tui Thakau (often called
“The Cow”), carried on his governmental func-
tions under conditions very different from those
of to-day. Thakau professed the Roman Catholic
religion, but drew the line at abandoning his 50
wives. It was his custom to remove all objection-
able people by the club. At the end of the debate in
the Parliament of the Lau Confederation, in ’7O,
he asked Maafu (the President), in a private con-
versation, to promise that, in the event of his
being assassinated, he (Maafu) would “exterminate
them all.” A large green mound in the centre of
Thakau’s town marked the burial place of 300
women, who were killed that they might accom-
pany a great chief to the spirit world. The ghost
of Ratu Lala will have to make the journey alone.
Crown Solicitor J. M. Stuart, of S. Aus-
tralia, retires at the end of June. He
was 41 when he gained admission to
the Bar, and had previously tried the
banking and school-teaching profes-
sions as a means of livelihood. Though
personally popular with law clerks, he
was nick-named “Necessity,” because “Ne-
cessity knows no law.” Once he was part-
ner with ex-Justice Boucaut. 0. J. Dash-
wood, who succeeds him, was a partner with
ex-Justice Bundey.
Frank VVyburn, the Melbourne solicitor
who suicided in the scrub near Warrnambool,
might have been one of the leading caricaturists
of Australia had he followed the game up.
Wyburn was the son of one of the oldest Mel-
bourne lawyers, and was educated at Heidelberg
University, Germany, with artist Rupert Bunny.
He seconded the latter in several of his duels, in
which he sustained numerous honorable slits about
the head. Five years ago, Wyburn’s oldest friend
and schoolboy mate shot himself in the scrub at
Cheltenham, and there is little doubt that the
example had its effect.
Dead, in April, at Bournemouth (Eng.),
aged 80, Charles E. Broadhurst, pioneer squatter
and pearl-sheller. He was a native of England, and
in 1840 came to Victoria, where lie shared a station
with liis elder brother. In the early 60’s he led a
party which took a quantity of stock to the North-
West of Australia—then practically an unknown
land—with a view to sheep-farming and cattle-
raising. He w.as the pioneer of the pearlshell in-
dustry, and is said to have introduced the first
steamer on the Westralian coast. Later on he
leased the Abrolhos Islands, and started the guano
business, which, after he dropped out, was con-
tinued by his son until the prohibition of guano
exports by the Westralian Government.
“ Melb.” writes :
Furnished with a gilt-edged card of entree, I went to
General Booth’s evening reception, in the Town Hall.
At the portal my costly pasteboard had to run the
gauntlet of various Army lackeys. No rouseabout,
bushwhacker, or sundowner would have had the
ghost of a show there. Well, getting upstairs, I was
shown to a luxurious room filled with Melbourne’s
elite, of ladies and gentlemen and the third
sex, for seemingly all the clergy were there, from
Archdeacon Hindley, Presbyterian Reverends Marshall
and Rentoul, Wesleyan Rev. Edgar, Baptist Rev. Carey,
and Muggletonian Rev. Muffinworry downwards.
With journalistic instinct, I wanted to press inside,
expecting that Booth was engaged there in personal con-
flict w'ith the devil, and that I might be too late to see
the third round, but I couldn’t squeeze in. A heavy Army
dignitary relieved my anxiety, for, with a wave of his
hand, he pointed to a room where Mayor Pleasance enter-
tains worldlings with lemonade. “ The General is there.”
Yes, he was there, with Pleasance, and only one or two
other gold-tops. What a luxury to have the clergy at
his feet, humbly fawning till he should deign to beam.
How this conversion? Alone he did it —I refer to King
Edward. So the General sauntered easily along, giving
directions to his clerical under-strappers. 1 could not
help noticing the gallant courtesy of the grand old chap
to the ladies. He can make them happy for life with one
gleam of his cockatoo eye.

“ Dico ” : Old Murphy was driving his
load of milk up the hill to the creamery the other
morning, when suddenly the harness gave way,
the shafts shot sky-ward, and Murphy landed on
the road. Before I reached him he was up,
hauling frantically at the shafts to right the cart,
together we restored it to a horizontal position,
arid then looked to Murphy’s injuries. There was
bad gravel-rash, and one knee was so hurt that he
could hardly stand. Leaving the horse and cart
on the road, I helped Murphy, leaning heavily on
my shoulder, to his house. Sighting Murphy
junior, a hulking yokel of 18 or so, I shouted for
him to come and assist. He lumbered heavily up,
and blurted at dad, “ Didjer spill any milk?”
We went on to the house and met the “missus ”
Murphy looked so bad, with his bleeding face and
lame leg, that I started to reassure her ; but there
was no need on the old man’s account. Her first
scared wad was, “Where’s the milk?” Then
I left, an 1 Murphy was escorted inside with
excited queries re the milk sounding in each ear
“ Old Chum ” :
Apropos the bottles or tanks on Cockatoo Island (not
Garden Island, as “ Alee Zander ” has it in B. 8/6/’O5).
In 1867 a notorious scoundrel, calling’ himself Owen
Suffolk, who scribbled poetry, and who had been in
most of the chief gaols of Australia, sold his reminis-
cences to a Melbourne journal. He had spent some time
on “ Cockatoo ” in the late 40’s or early 50’s, and for
some breach of regulations, he says, he was “conveyed
to the underground cells (damp, dark places) known on
the island as ‘The Bottles.’” He was kept in the
“bottles” three weeks awaiting trial, etc. In reply to
Suffolk, J. F. Peers, late medical officer, Cockatoo,
wrote under date Aug., 1867, to explain that the term
“bottles” was used by the prisoners at Cockatoo in
reference to the cells hewn out of the solid rock. “No
prisoner,” said the doctor, “to my certain knowledge
has, since ISSO, been ‘ locked up ’ in the siloes, and
1 am equally certain that none were ‘ locked down ’
before that period. Some years ago several men lost
their lives cleaning out the siloes, from the poisonous
gas at the bottom, so it is not likely men would be put in
such a place.” A person who was living on the island at
the time wrote thus, some years ago: “Cut out of the
solid sandstone, on the highest part of Cockatoo, are a
number of large siloes, shaped like a huge bottle. These
"’ere made after a great drought, when the colors had
suffered very severely from lack of grain. These were
t > prevent such a famine occurring again, and they were
filled with immense quantities of grain, only to be used in
time of necessity. After remaining sealed for some years
one of them was opened, when, to every one’s great sur-
prise, the contents were found to be mouldy, the damp
having soaked through the porous sandstone. The others
were immediately examined, and found to be in the same
state, and useless for human consumption. During
the process of getting the mouldy grjjn out, one silo had
been partially emptied and temporarily covered. It was
a good depth down, and when the gang came, one of the
men, without waiting for a ladder, jumped down, only to
fall back overpowered by foul air. Another man, without
reckoning the risk, jumped down to help him, only to be
overcome in a similar fashion. There were at one time no
less than 13 men lying insensible in the silo. When they
were brought out, three were found to be dead. The
man who was the primary cause of the disaster recovered
though he had been in longer than anyone else. One of
those who died in the attempt at rescue was to have left
the island a free man in two days.” “ Captain Cuttle,”
writing in 1867, gave the version I gave in B. (l/6/’O5),
also the statement that Governor Gipps got rapped over
the knuckles. Gipps created the siloes, and hoped that
the farmers would follow his example. Only recently a
huge silo at the rear of the “ Rangers ” —the old home of
the Bloxsomes at Mosman—was filled in. The “ Rangers ”
house was built about the time that Gipps was construct-
ing his siloes on Cockatoo Island.

“Harry S.” : In the S.E. of S. Australia,
years ago, the natives were much given to killing
shepherds and carrying off sheep, and when they
had got more sheep than they could manage to eat in
a few days, they used to “hobble” the unfortunate
animals by either twisting the limbs out of joint
or breaking them outright. Numbers so maimed
used to be found by the pursuing whites.

a small brown snake, coiled

mouth. Hadn’t time to in-
vestigate —one barrel going
to the ducks and one to the
snake —but it struck me after-
wards that the snake was
crushing the frog prepara-
tory to gorging him. By-che-
way, a 17ft. carpet snake
ought to squeeze a man a

but the “carpet” is such an

with a waddy. I’ve read,

Wouldn’t be a bad idea to
import some boas to Aus-
tralia. It’s too beastlv safe

which accounts largely for
our tired feeling. We haven’t
enough to keep us on the
jump. A few lions, tigers,
and boas judiciously dis-
tributed over the continent
would make the population
sit up. And if a boa
struck George Keid on tour
—well, he wouldn’t be missed

The Bog-a-Duck Debating Society held a
meeting recently; subject for debate, “Does
philanthropy pay?” Members grew hot upon
the subject; but all conducted themselves peace-
ably until a land-agent got up to argue for the
noes. Then a bankrupt selector arose and wrung
the speaker’s nose violently, and inquired : “ You
dashed philanthropist ! If you charge people
who are bankrupt and starving £OOO for getting
the Land Boards to do things that should have
been done, that ought to be done, and that will
have to be done, what do you charge Fatman for
putting through things that ought not to be done ? ”
Then a heavy boot struck the land-agent upon bis
terminus, and the meeting broke up into a dis-
orderly heap, from the bottom of which the
agent s muffled voice was heard threatening to
drag Ins assailant through all the courts in the
country, and knock him out with an ingeniously-
constructed bill of costs.

“Elmore” : Be horse-endurance (“ H.Gr.,”
B. 11/5/’O5). The horse has been known to live
25 days with no food, but plenty of water ; with-
out food or water, about 15 days ; and with plenty
of food but no water, four to six days, according
to weather.

“B.”: Up in Central Queensland I
recently struck the final tail-end of the dugong-
fifiling industry. An ancient man on a lonely
peninsula represented the last of the fishers. His
equipment was a couj>le of nets of thin rope, Bin.
mesh, and 130 yards long. These are sunk to a
depth of about 20 feet, and in due course a female
dugong comes along with her calf, browsing
upon the seaweed on the ocean floor.
She blunders into the net, her head going
into the mesh and, half frightened, she
turns to warn the calf, but is too late. She
is caught in another loop of the net, and be-
comes angry, but is now so entangled that
her immense weight and strength are of no
avail. This queer creature —weighing
12001 b. to 14001 b.—is now helpless. The
ancient fisherman hauls up his prey, and
on getting her to the surface he puts a
bullet into her. The calf pathetically
refuses to leave its mother’s body, and
finally falls a victim to the nearest shark.
The shark is a deadly enemy of the dugong,
and the bane of the poor old man’s exist-
ence. “Just as I gets ’em up, a shark
will, with one snap, bite the head clean
off of’em.” The dugong and the manatee
are the only surviving representatives of the
order Sirenia, the allusion in the name to
the classic Sirens being due to the fact that,
as the females of these creatures have two
breasts in the human position, it is sup-
posed that the occasional sight of them,
partly raised out of the water, was re-
sponsible for the mermaid idea. The dugong
rises to the surface to breathe with a
peculiar, graceful sweep, holding its young
under its flipper. The hide, which is half
an inch thick, accounts for about 2cwt. of
the creature’s weight; it is covered with a
scanty growth of bristles, and reeks with
oil, taking six months to dry, even in the
sun. It is worth 30s. in the open market.
The flesh is boiled down for the oil, which is worth
another 3Js. to the old man—or else it is corned.
It is not unlike beef in flavor ; indeed the animal,
which is a purely vegetable feeder, has stroi g
general affinities with the terrestrial herbivora.

“San Remo”: “Falder” (B. l/6/’O5)
writes a good sketch about bullock-driving. But
has “Falder” ever witnessed that queer and un-
common sight- a bolting team of bullocks, waggon
and all ? In Rockhampton, some years ago, a
team of 12 bullocks, drawing an empty waggon,
lumbered in a frenzy down William-street. Turn-
ing a cprner, the outfit capsized, and bullocky,
overhauling the show, soothed the menagerie. A
more grotesque sight 1 have never w.tnessed than
Bally and his brother Poley cantering along and
trying to look furious. What scared them I nevei
found out. Dynamite would fail to scare the
average bullock team.

“ Gnib ” : The small brown hawk men-
tioned by “0.W.” (B. l/G/’O5) is the dollar-bird,
not the doll-bird. Name comes from a circular
white patch on the under side of the wing, which
patch looks like cash. Said bird is fond of
chickens as well as of gill-birds.
DOWN THE BOGAN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Down the dusty Nyngan road,
Stumbling' ’neath his heavy load,
Came he to that house accurst,
Where the swagsman slakes his thirst.
Seven loafers smiled in glee ;
But, alas, this stranger, he
Did not wear the mien exalted
Of a man whose thoughts are malted.
“ Have you seen a small, fat, sandy
Man, whose legs are rather bandy ? ”
So he spake, while sad and cheerless
Grew those men, who waited beerless.
“ Seven weeks I’ve followed Ifogan
Down the reaches of the Bogan ;
Holy smokes ! O won’t I grind him
Inter dust, when once I find him 1
“ Went to Bourke in pleasant weather,
There to bust our cheques together.
I had twenty pounds, and he
Handed in a stinkin’ three !
“ Clothes an’ tucker first we bought ;
Then M'Garron’s pub. we sought.
Though ’tis hard to get my maw full,
Hogan’s thirst, I thought, was awful !
“ Eight days later, when T woke,
Dizzy-headed, sore, and broke,
Vanished, scooted, gone was Hogan
Down the reaches of the Bogan !
“ Then I found he’d pawned my swag—
Mortgaged every blooming rag !
With his cheque uncashed, unspent,
Left me there without a cent !
“ When I chance on Patrick Hogan
Somewhere down along the Bogan ”
Here he laughed, tho’ aught but gay
Seemed his mirth—then strolled away.
Seven drunks, with necks a-crane,
Watched him, speck-like, cross the plain
Till he vanished in the glimmer
Of the spectral lakes a-shimmer,
Seeking still with quaint persistence
Ear out, in the hazy distance,
Tidings of a man named Ilogan,
Somewhere down along the Bogan.
X.S.W.
A. P. ROGERS.

temper, and, when his horse jibbed one day about
20 yards from his front gate, he bolted into
the house for a gun, and gave the horse the
benefit of the right barrel. Turk’s magpie
was sitting on the fence, and as Turk
passed through the gate it greeted
him with “ Who are you ?” “By
——, I’ll shoiv you who lam !’’
Slid Turk, and he emptied the
left barrel into the bird. “Y’
ought ta been shot yerself, .
for shooting the neddy,”
said Turk’s cousin when
Turk told him. “See
this moke o’ mine ? He
was a reg’lar cow to jib
till one day I took
’im out fer a load
uv box, an’, cornin’
home, be jibbed with
me right in front,of
the pub. I waited
20 minutes and the
cuss never moved
an inch, so I j am med
a log between the
spokes an’ went an’
had a drink an’ a
smoke, an’ then an-
other drink, an’ so
on, until blowed if I
didn’t ferget all
about the ’orse till
seven nex’ mornin’.
Crawled out an’ tuk
the log outer the
wheels, an’ the old
’un went off the
mark like a race-
’orse, an’ I never ’ad
no trouble with ’im
since. When yer
’orse finds out that
you can jib longer
than ’e can, ’e gets
a kinder respeck f’r ye.”

“Dave R.” : Be mouse running into
goanna’s mouth (“Blue Tongue,” B. l/6/’O5).
There was no fascination in the matter. It was
simply a case of knowledge on the “go.’s”part
that, when disturbed, a mouse always runs for the
nearest visible opening. The “go.” also knows
instinctively that almost every rubbish-heap about
a house or camp is inhabited by mice. So he gets
close to a heap and waits, with his mouth open,
like a hollow log or a parliamentary recess, till
something disturbs a mouse ; then he scores.

“Chip”; “ N.E.” (B. 95/5/’O5) is all
abroad about the habits of the tree ant. The ant
doesn’t cement leaves together, and the natives
don’t “ pound the whole into a mash.” The nests
are made with leaves drawn together with webs
by the workers, to protect the much larger drones.
It is these that the blacks eat, but not in the
manner he states. A handful of half-dried grass
is set fire to. Before it is burnt the flame is
smothered, and while the embers are red the big
ants are shaken out of the leaf-receptacle on to
them. There is just enough heat to cripple the
ants ; they ar.e then picked up and eaten, and are
really quite equal to sherry and bitters as an
appetiser.
A petition is being signed
in the Beaufort district (Vic.),
urging the Government to
resume, for closer settlement,
48,000 acres of Admiral
Bridges’ Trawalla Estate.
“Haw! those wretched So-
cialists ! ” quoth the doughty
Admiral, “ I’m not afraid of
the beggahs.” The common
reply of the business people,
when urged to sign, is:
“Well, you know, so far as
hoping the estate will be cut
up goes, I’m with you ; but
—er—you know—a man in
business —er —mustn’t quarrel
with his bread and butter,
you know.” Still, a good
many signatures are being
obtained to a petition that will probably be quite
futile. It is said that Bridges’ wool clip was
worth about £25,000 last season.

“Periwinkle”: Your friend “ Thurla ”
hums a lot about discovering wild-bees’ nests (B.
25/5/ 05), and buz-z-z-z-z-z-zes more or less eock-
surely as to a black-gin’s find in the cleft of a tree !
Any bushman, not suffering from hay-fever or the
snuffles, can locate a wild-bees’ nest by sense of
smell only—a mile off. Any sane aborigine can
smell the robbers’ store, three miles off, on a warm
day. The smell of honey is the sweetest the bush
can boast. The down business is very picturesque,
but it’s a bit of doodleflap that’s played out. Any
lad around here will take his axe and locate his
treasure-trove, in the timber along the river,
entirely by nose. The wild-bee is an innocent
chap, but not so innocent as those who would
believe “Thurla’s” fairies.

“ Sugar Cane ” : With regard to “ N.E.’s ”
statement that the blacks in North Queensland
pound up green ants into a mash and eat it with
relish—that is not the practice of the niggers on
Sully River and thereabouts. They pound up the
green ant all right—they pound him till he is
quite dead—and then they strain off the juice
and drink it, sometimes neat and sometimes with
water. After a heavy bout of Chinese opium-
charcoal the niggers may be seen for days hunting
through the scrubs for green ants. Ant juice
seems to have a soothing effect after a heavy dose
of the Chow’s mixture. It might be worth try-
ing as a pick-me-up after rum. Apart from the
chance of finding a cure for the grog-thirst, there
is the prospect of giving the blessed “primary
producer ” a fresh start, and “ settling him on the
land,” and “ developing the great natural re-
sources,” and so on.

the Echuca and Moama districts is 100 per cent,,
and though “Bung” makes a poor mouth, a few
years ago a local bullocky sold up, started a pub
in Moama (a stone’s throw over the Murray from
Echuca) and did so well that he liquidated a large
debt in a few months, has a big banking account
now, and holds mortgages over several properties.
The bulk of the Echuca beer trade is done by four
hotels, and the balance is split up amongst a
dozen or so no-class shanties which just manage to
hang on. As for that greasy man worth £5, now
sleeping in “Bung’s” bridal chamber, I hope he
took off his boots before going to bed.
“The Prooshan ” : Was engaged
on Jeralie in 75. “ Scotty the Wrinkler ”
was cooking at the home station, Nap
Nap, and drove all hands, including
some niggers, into Maude for a Christ-
mas spree. Sandy Bell, manager of
Nap Nap, invited me to take Christmas
dinner with him. Toothache, however,
got me down in one of the huts. At 3
p.m. on Boxing Day four black revellers
returned, Gilbert, Yarry, and Micky,
and Caroline, his gin. They were all
tight, and Caroline, seated out in the dust
with the others, was urged to sing. On
refusing, Mick pounded her with a great
waddy on the head. Each blow would
have settled a Hackenschmidt. I rushed
out and hunted Micky off, and returned
to the hut to entertain my guest of the
evening, the toothache. In five minutes,
turning on the bunk, I noticed Caroline,
the belabored, Sneak out with my half-
bottle of rum, sent by the manager for
the toothache, and I sprinted a quarter of
a mile into the lignum before I caught her
and recovered the medicine.
“ W. 8.” : To “ Ray ” (B. l/6/’O5).
Mohair goat-breeding has been carried
on for the last four years at Knox’s No.
2 station, between Wyalong and Barrned-
man, and the industry is now in a thriv-
ing condition. Pure angora rams are im-
ported. and mated with common nannies.
Young bucks are selling at good prices,
and the imported animals have become
thoroughly acclimatised, showing that this
distinct is eminently suitable for the
industry.
“ Blue Shamrock ” : Last summer, in the
Rutherglen district (Vic.), snakes were so
numerous that their skins were used for tying up
vines. For this purpose they answered better
than the usual flax or rushes, as they did not
become loose or sag in a high wind. A Scotch-
man made some £5 a week catching them. His
method was to dig a circular hole about 12ft. deep,
and then stand a gum pole about 12in. thick in
the centre. On the top of the pole he fastened a
music box, which he wound up at sundown. It
played Scotch tunes, and the snakes were attracted
for miles around. They gathered at the pit
mouth and became infatuated with the melody, and
finally they rushed at it and fell in. One morning
he found 2000 snakes, and—most astonishing of
all —half of them were already dead. Presumably
they had been trodden to death in the struggle for
front seats close to the orchestra.
* * * *
“ G. 8.” : Concerning mead, they brew a
drink in the Highlands of Scotland thus com-
posed : One part of honey to three of whisky.
It is named Athol Brose, and is as potent as it is
palatable. It used to be yin o’ the sichts o’ the
Highlan’s and the most popular indoor Caledonian
sport to induce the unsuspicious Southron to hae
a glass or twa of this seductive and searching
brew. After two drinks, the stranger in a far
land who lived a thousand yards off might be con-
fidently expected to walk five miles before he got
home—on account of the width of the King’s high-
way—unless, indeed, he was wheeled home. It is
said that the duke who invented this beverage
laid-out Louis Napoleon by its aid when the Naps
visited Scotland—sometime prior to the birth of
the Prince Imperial.
THE COLLISION.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
His clothes were sombre and his aspect sad,
He faced the filthy place where could be had
Beer of a deadly quality and whisky bad.
Ruthless, in dreary coat and visage grim,
So, filled with awe, I stopped and gazed at him
And saw a drunk emerge with bloodshot eyes and dim.
He hiccupped twice within the portal w'de,
And with a wolibie and a lurch and slide
Approached the holy man who stood there in his pride
(As fixed and grim as any weathered rock),
And came against him with a beery shook,
And in thick, husky accents hailed him as “ Old Cock.”
O fool, to rush where angels fear to tread !
Grasping the sacred cloth (which tore) he said
“ (Hie) come ’n’ ’ave a rumsh wish me ! ” He lurched ! I
fled !
vic.
C. M. FLYNN.

No notice (except of a hostile character) will be taken of
communications insufficiently stamped. Name and
address must appear on MS. No liability is under-
taken in any case re contributions voluntarily sub-
mitted, ivhether sent by post or handed in; and in
no case will MS. be returned unless stamps (of any
State) accompany.

H. Coates : Rather too amateurish.
Oh ! Thou husky, asthmatic old fellow,
Whom coughing has bent like a bow ;
Thou child with the colic, whose, bellow
Disturbs the whole neighborhood so.
O ! Lad with the appetite hearty,
Whom sweetmeats too greatly allure;
Now, mark what I say, nor depart ye
From Wood’s Great Peppermint Cure.*
But now ’bis like Eternity,
To think of those days gone by—
To think of my own diversity—
Uttering only a prolonged sigh.

THE UNEMPLOYED AND THE STATE.
Broadly speaking, the idle may be grouped in
three divisions :
(1) Those who want but car.not obtain work.
(2) Those who consistently evade work.
(3) Those who can live without work.
The third section, not only by evil example, but
also by the transmission of lifelong habits of
luxurious laziness to its posterity, is to some
extent responsible for those of the second class.
The rest of the responsibility must fall on the
shoulders of the people’s representatives, because
of their failure to grapple seriously with this great
problem. Nor is it permissible that any grade of
society should be permitted to evade its responsi-
bilities with regard to the unemployed on the
score of “class legislation.” The unemployed is
of no class in particular, is recruited from all
classes. In addition to skilled and unskilled
workers, it contains disrated officers—naval and
military, once prc sperous merchants and traders,
drought-reduced squatters, defalcating lawyers,
unfrocked priests and parsons, warned-off book-
makers, doctors for whom drink had more attrac-
tion than disease, and so forth. Even were it
not so—were the unemployed drawn either all
from the professions, or all from the
laborers—it would still be the unshirk-
able duty of the community, as a whole,
for its own safety, to succor the unfortu-
nate, the helpless, the ineapa jle, and the ill-
disposed, from the degradation which awaits them
if left to their own devices. The community
should shape its policy to secure remunerative
work for the willing and capable; it should
compel the unwilling but capable to work ;
it should support, and reform where possible,
the physically, mentally, and morally incap-
able. If any or all of these things be neglected,
then society will eventually be dragged down
by its burden of human vermin. As for the
loungers, the social butterflies, these must be left
to the less tender mercies of a more practical
posterity.
• * * *
Civilisation’s greatest problem has always been
the lack of continuous employment for the willing.
Many civilised countries are now making it
a leading political and municipal question. In
Holland they have Beggar Colonies. Frederik-
soord, a private philanthropy, was founded by
General Van den Bosch, in 1818, at the conclusion
of the French war', when the Netherlands was
plunged in pauperism. In the midst of a desert
are 5000 intensely cultivated acres, which support
ir plenty 90 families of partially-dependent
laborers, who in time will be self-supporting;
224 families of self-supporting farmers ; and 120
aged and orphaned, who board with the farmers.
Altogether, nigh 2000 people are supported here
at the minimum of cost. The State’s Beggar
Colonies were founded in 1870 on a wild waste.
Here 3000 men and women—most of them arc too
old or too ill to work—are supported by the work
of the sturdy at an annual cost of little more than
£8 per head, a sum likely to be reduced as the
land becomes more easily worked. But as large
numbers of idlers and incapables are still at large,
preying on the community and free to reproduce
their kind, the scheme is by no means complete.
Yet if Holland can support its aged and feeble at
a cost of £8 per head largely through the labor of
those who would otherwise be outcasts, Australia
might well give the Dutch method attention.
* * * *
Various cities of the United Kingdom deal
more or less tentatively with the unemployed
question, but Berlin is notable for a bold munici-
pal handling of the matter. Shelters are there
provided where the homeless men, women,
and children —are suppered, bathed, bedded, and
their clothes cleaned and fumigated while they
sleep. But no one may go oftener than five times
in each quarter to the municipal shelter, or five
times in each month to the shelter managed by
the Social Democrats. Those who go oftener,
like those found begging or idling in the streets,
are sent to the workhouse, where they earn board
and lodging by very hard work, and are taught the
trades for which they seem best fitted. This may
seem harsh treatment, but extensive diseases re-
quire drastic remedies, and discipline is what the
morally weak need above all things. Berlin nurses
its sick poor back to health ; finds work for the
willing who cannot otherwise obtain it ; compels
the lazy and criminal to maintain themselves;
and, aided by the State insurance system, supports
the aged and incapable. Of course, the scheme is
far from perfect—for instance, we have not yet
heard that any loafer has there been plucked from
the bosom of his motor-car and committed to the
workhouse—but, nevertheless, the system is worthy
of earnest consideration.
* * * *
In Australia this question should be grap-
pled with by the municipalities, shire councils
and State Governments, with the Federal
Ministry as the central authority. Each sec-
tion should provide, as far as possible, for
its own unemployed, and should regularly
furnish the central body with information
and statistics, so that a labor shortage in one
quarter might be balanced by the surplus of
another district. It should also be part of the
domestic economy of each State and municipality
to establish industrial and agricultural colleges for
the support and instruction of the willing but unfor-
tunate —these to be worked in conjunction with the
farm colonies and factories, whence the instructed
may be drafted. For the convalescent poor and
temporarily out of work, shelters should be estab-
lished on waste lands within easy distance of
cities—resembling that, for instance, on the
Bunnerong Road, Sydney—where the deserving
may re-establish health and prosecute the search
for employment while earning supper, bed, and
breakfast daily by a few hours of open-air toil.
Lastly, each large centre should have its peni-
tentiary where confirmed cadgers, beggars, loafers
and petty thieves should be compelled to earn
bread ere permitted to eat it. Of course, Tub
Bulletin is not blind to the fact that all these
stimulants to effort will not avail without the
greater stimulus caused by easy access to land and
the development of native industry, but these
matters are not now under discussion. It is
the habitual unemployed with which this article
deals, the man who doesn’t want to work and the
man who, whilst he will work if a job is thrust at
him, has no energy or initiative to search out an
opportunity—the lazy men and the feckless men.
* * * *
The individualist, the private enterprise crank,
will, of course, at once, ask: “ Why should the
State do any of these things ? ” Well, this country
already expends something like 7s. per head on its
police and prison system; it possibly spends
4s. or more per head on its judicial machinery;
while its expenditures in charity, public and
private, much exceed both these sums. At the
same time the annual expenditure is increasing on
prisons, hospitals, benevolent institutions, soup-
kitchens, lunatic asylums, reformatories, children’s
homes, and other punitive and charitable places.
These at best are only palliatives—State
recognitions of social evils which it seem-
ingly considers irremediable; evils which
grow annually with what they feed upon.
On the other hand, the scheme just detailed, if
only moderately successful, would have the effect
of lessening considerably our charitable and puni-
tive expenditure, and would at least make some
attempt to deal systematically and logically with
a great national difficulty. At present every
loafer, every vagrant —hungry, dirty, vermin-
infested —is a seed-plot of bodily disease and of
mental and moral contamination. The community
which permits this process of self-infection to go on
and to grow perceptibly is more criminal than the
evilly-constituted who impair its health and en-
danger its existence. Thelonger it abstainsfrom sus-
tained and scientific interference, the more quickly
approaches the moment of its own disintegration.
It is a crying shame that thousands should ask in
vain for work; it is a national disgrace that
thousands should be permitted to evade all honest
toil. The idler, the beggar, the sharper, in due
time will evolve the thief, the prostitute, the
degenerate, the lunatic, and the murderer. And
the numbers of these assassins of national life are
rapidly increasing.
A woman’s thoughts don’t always fly first
to her frock. When a fire broke out in a Maori-
land house lately, a young woman rushed to the
front room to grab a Tatt.’s sweep ticket that had
arrived that afternoon. She saved that, but she
lost most of her wardrobe.
