POLITICAL
POINTS
Judging by the statements made to the
Lands Commission, the object of a good deal of
Cornstalk legislation was not so much to settle
Mr. Willis on the land as to settle the land on Mr.
Willis.
Pumpkin Smith, orating in the anti-
Socialist interest at Bathurst (N.S.W.), told his
audience that his party wasn’t afraid to face the
people and it was quite sure it had the people at
its back.
B. R. Wise, who seems to have dropped
altogether the Freetrade foolishness, is pushing
home in England the facts as to the Yankee Har-
vester Trust’s attempt to knock out the Australian
agricultural machinery firms.
A “bull” by Edden, M.L.A. (N.S.W.).
During his speech on the censure debate he
observed several Ministerialists leaving the
Chamber, and remarked : “They stalk out one
by one without a leg to stand on.”
Sydney Fatmen are talking of promoting
a comic paper to help their cause in politics.
“ We need the help of ridicule,” says John Stin-
son, solemnly. They want ridicule ? Well,
they’ve only to get on a platform to excite it.
The Westralian press reports of Justice
Burnside’s decision in the recent Chinese exclu-
sion case, show that the summaries telegraphed to
the Eastern papers were grossly incorrect. Instead
of saying that the “ test ” should have been put to
the Asiatic in a language he could understand,
what the Judge actually did say was that the
request to undergo the test should have been
interpreted to the man in his own language—a
vastly different thing. The Bulletin apologises
to Mr. Burnside.
BRITAIN'S CLOSING DOOR.
The second reading of the Aliens Exclusion Bill has passed
the House of Commons.
So far Sweden seems unable to decide
what to do about that Norwegian revolution.
The King, who received notice to quit, has gone
out and banged the door hard behind him to indi-
cate that he is annoyed, and for the time being
the matter rests there.
Half-Premier Reid at Rockhampton the
other day:—“He had faced the wealthy classes
and taken them down. Now he was facing a
much more powerful combination, and was
endeavoring to take them down." Reid was
always a political “take-down.”
When the financiers of the world—as it
was alleged—were turning their faces resolutely
way from this country, and the Commonwealth
was rocking to its base, Mr. Reid was found
pinning his faith to an amendment of the standing
orders.— W. M. Hughes in a recent speech.
Holman, M.L.A., stated lately at Gun-
dagai (N.S.W.) that the time was near when the
Labor party would dominate Australia. But if
the party has any sense it will postpone dominat-
ing till it has High Protection on its programme.
Otherwise industries will continue to languish,
people will still drift away, immigration will still
be practically a blank, and for all this the demo-
cratic policy will bear the blame till the party
goes out of office with discredit, having set the
cause back 20 years.
“Adelaide” : And is it really possible
that you haven’t a Width of Tyres Act in N.S.W.
either (B. 25/5/’O5) ? S. A. passed hers close on 40
years ago—9cwt. allowed for each inch of wheel-
width. On the whole, N.S.W. might do well to
borrow our statute-books and acquire some useful
hints.
The Carruthers Government in N.S.W. at
last ends the Arbitration Court deadlock by agree-
ing to appoint a Deputy-President. So the
attempt to strangle the Act fails; its most
definite result has been to unite the labor unions
in a defence of the Arbitration Court, in support
of which they were not too cordial before.
The Tory London Morning Post now
urges that it would be better for Australia to have
its own navy than to subsidise the British fleet: —
Her own navy would provide Australia with the only
means of producing a school of seamen able to grasp
problems of naval strategy'.
Which was one of the reasons pressed by The
Bulletin when arguing against the naval subsidy.
Premier Bent has violently informed a
deputation that he will have nothing to do
with a Woman Suffrage Bill. He is against it to
tlje utmost of his bellow, and “he’d sooner lose
his seat than grant women a vote.” And, to do
the female voter justice, there’ll probably be a
perspiring market gardener earnestly searching
for a place whereon to rest his weary bulk so soon
as she gets the suffrage in spite of him.
The further G. H. Reid wanders into
denunciations of Socialism the more hopelessly
muddled he shows himself :
If Socialism would put into the hearts of the people a
spirit of Christianity, if it put into the breasts of the
proud a spirit of humanity, then he would rush to sup-
port it. But what a monstrous folly to say that a code of
words could do by compulsion what the Divinity could
not do.
Will the Church ask Reid what he means by say-
ing that the Omnipotent “ could not do ” this or
that ?
Manager Thornley, of W. Sandford, Ltd.,
the firm which has been struggling for 20 years to
set the iron industry on its legs in Australia,
announces that they are not going to be wiped
out; if they cannot manufacture, they will
import. But what sort of policy is it that shuts
up workshops and opens an office alongside a
wharf —that takes a job away from a couple of
hundred skilled workmen, and gives a job to a 30s.
a week clerk and an odd shift to half-a-dozen
coal lumpers ? The manufacturer probably won’t
care, for he will make more money; but the 200
workmen
Launceston (Tas.) Ex-
aminer, always a rather low-
spirited publication, expresses
the opinion that the island may
this year have another deficit of
about £25,000—0f course after
charging, as usual, a large
amount of ordinary expendi-
ture to loans. And it solemnly
warns the Evans Ministry that,
whatever the deficit may be,
“there must be no further
schemes at present for imposing
fresh burdens on the people.”
Tasmania is a very lightly-taxed
State compared to such a place
as England, but it has a won-
derful number of papers which
assure its people that they are
a crashed and down-trodden
assemblage, and that if they
are expected to pay their way
they will burst. And unless
Tasmania gets a little more
grit and honesty it might al-
most as well burst and be done
with it.
Mr. Briner moves in the
N.S.W. State Parliament for
elective Ministries—a wise and
ultimately inevitable step. The
theory of Party Government,
which makes it necessary to
eject a good Cabinet because
one of its members is impos-
sible, or to tolerate a bad Cabi-
net because of the reluctance to
lose a couple of good adminis-
trators, is obviously opposed
to common sense. It is an
inheritance from England
(where the nation seems to
have made its Parliamentary
system purpo-ely unpractical
and “viewy” with a view to
casting off the reproach of
being too business-like), and
doesn’t work in Australia.
N.S.W. Premier Carruthers takes every
opportunity (the High Court expenses discussion
was the last) to wail over the lost rights of his
State through the delay in setting up the Federal
capital. And Carruthers himself is the only ob-
stacle to a settlement. The Federal Parliament
has decided upon a site and Carruthers will not
yield it, and Reid is not courageous enough to
take it. Hence the deadlock.
Earl Beauchamp, at an English meeting,
the other day :
He could not understand how it was that the political
party which was so anxious to exclude aliens from this
country were so anxious to dump Chinese into S’Africa.
He knew of mines in Australia, as deep or deeper than
mines in the Transvaal, which were worked by white
labor at trade-union rates of wages. If this was possible
in Australia, why was it impossible in S’Africa? The
introduction of Chinese under the British flag was a slur
and a tarnish upon the good name of our country.
Though they have clipped the lawyer’s
wings a good bit in S. Aus., he can still spread
them pretty wide. The law allows municipal
corporations to sell lands for overdue rates.
Recently the Thebarton Corporation decided to
sell eight allotments. The sale brought in £74;
the lawyer’s bill was for £79. It was taxed
down to £6l; but even then, when all the other
bills were paid, all that was left for the coun-
cil was £1 Bs. 7d.
The quality of horses shipped to India
from Australia for army remounts has fallen off
seriously of late, and the same old remedy is pro-
posed—Socialism. It is suggested that the State
should inspect stallions, should provide good
stallions to breeders at a nominal fee, and should
prevent brood mares leaving the country. When-
ever the business man gets the power to see an
evil and the intelligence 'to think out a remedy, he
always seems to pick upon a further instalment of
State Socialism as the only thing to meet the case.
Then he writes a letter to the papers pointing out
how the Socialistic Labor party is ruining the
country.
A departmental return
shows that during 1904, 117
immigrants were excluded
from the Commonwealth
under the Alien Immi-
gration Restriction Act.
Of these, 76 were
Chinese. On the other
hand, 603 Chinese, 2
negroes, 4 Cingalese,
9 Filipinos, 13 Hin-
dus, 12 Japs, and 14
Syrians were admitted
as exempt on account
of former domicile ; 163
Chinese came in under
State permits, and 461
Malays, and 419 Japs
arrived as pearlers. Query:
How many of the Chows
listed as being admitted on
account of former domicile and
under State permits, were.
helped along by that “forged-certificate” factory
unearthed at Hongkong recently ? Another query :
Is an Act which keeps out 76 Chinese, and
admits 1280 colored people of all sorts, the sort of
White Australia Act we want? This country
begins to have a doubtful feeling in the stomach.
One of the motions on the business paper
of tho Labor Congress which meets on July 8
reads: —
That Protection be made a plank in the Federal Labor
party’s platform.
By way of amendment, The Bulletin moves that
the word “high” be inserted between “that” and
“Protection.” And this paper holies it will be so
high that the importer will sprain his neck trying
to see to the top of it.
The logic of the cleric. The other day a
parson of Southern Victoria attacked the Labor
Party and its legislative proposals with true
Kerristian bitterness. Almost in the same breath
he said there was no chance for a man to get along
in Victoria, and solemnly announced his intention
of going to Maoriland (which has been run on
Labor principles for 14 years) “where things are
booming.” The same gentleman a while ago said
that he wasn’t going to bother about a vote
because so many people didn’t trouble to record
their votes now-a-days. He was too tired even to
vote against the Labor Party.
As against Cardinal Moran, who has
dropped to G. H. Reid’s bogey campaign, Reid
quotes Bishop Dunne as delivering “a warning
and protest against the Socialistic tendencies of
the Labor organisations.” Which is just about
as accurate as might have been expected. Dr.
Dunne delivered his warning and protest against
the secularism of a debating club (which held its
meetings on Sundays), and not against the
Socialism of the Labor party. Is George Reid, by
the way, game to have his policy judged by the
things he babbled in his young, slim days at the
School of Arts talking-mili ?
The Heid party has decided, and perhaps
rightly, that its best chance lies in making the
next Federal election a fight between Socialism
and anti-Socialism. At all events, if it
can fight the election on that issue alone,
it has a chance to keep the present coali-
tion together. And the Labor party
plays its game by meeting it on the
ground it has selected. If the Labor
party would only get a patriotic fiscal
policy, and would insist on answering
Reid’s Tory howl with a howl for demo-
cratic progress, High Protection, and
no borrowing, it would burst the coali-
tion asunder. The fact that Reid wants
to sink the tariff question at the elec-
tion is just the reason why his oppo-
nents should keep it high up before
the people—as high as Hainan's gal-
lows. The commander who only attacks
his enemy where the enemy asks him
to attack is generally a failure. At least,
that wasn’t the way Togo won his battles.
Some of those Transvaal war con-
tractors must have picked up a wrinkle or
two from certain old-time N.S.W. Lands
Department experts who were pervading
S’Africa during and since the war.
Premier Daglish, of Westralia, when he
took office, said that lie was going to mark time.
Since then, in 10 months, he has spent £533,719 of
loan money, or at the rate of £2 4s. lOd. per head of
the average population, or nearly twice as much
as the entire debt of Japan, or about an eighth of
what Great Britain has managed to pile up
through a couple of hundred years of wars and
preparations for wars. And Daglish did it in 10
months, and piled up a deficit on revenue account
into the bargain. Which only shows that if you
choose a bog to mark time on, and mark time with
enough vigor, you can get deeper in the mess than
if you didn’t mark time at all but simply walked
straight ahead.
The voice of Senator Dobson has been but
little heard of late, but it was uplifted in a recent
Argus, and it was the old voice and the old
Dobson. The re-awakening of the Tassy Senator
is due to the Kanaka. After declaring with un-
necessary vehemence, that—
The Kanakas cannot and will not be deported—
he refers to them as “harmless and useful men,”
of whom, if they are sent back—-
Some will go to starvation, others to violence and pro-
bably death, and all to barbarism, except, of course, the
few who may be fortunate enough to fall into the hands of
missionaries.
The quaint, contradictory Dobson sort of person
never seems to see anything strange about his
description of Tommy Tanna, temporarily in
Queensland, as a “ harmless and useful man,”
and his brother, Jimmy Tanna, of Malekula, who
has probably been in Queeensland, as a violent,
murderous, fratricidal scoundrel.
“T.S.”: Apropos of the ramifications of
the Australian Stinking Fish party. I travelled
a few months ago from Rangoon to Calcutta on
board an unkempt steamer —the Ethiopia—which
was infested with ants to a degree which made
sleep impossible. The captain learned that I was
from Australia, and asked what on earth was going
to become of that dreadful country, seeing that
the railways in Victoria alone had a deficit of three
millions a year. Heaven only knows where he
got his figures from. This captain, out of every
22 days, spent nine ashore at Calcutta and six
ashore at Rangoon. He had a pilot to take him
up and down the Hooghly and the Rangoon river,
so he had six days of actual work (he never stood
a watch, however, so far as I could find out) in
every 22. He told me, Australians were lazy.
And when the soda-water ran out, half-way
between Rangoon and Calcutta, because somebody
had forgotten to put the usual supply on board,
and when it was discovered that the liquor depart-
ment hadn’t laid in a single cigar-well, the cap-
tain was just explaining to me how inefficient was
the management of everything in Australia when
those discoveries were made, and he didn’t seem
in the least put out.
SOME AUSTRALIAN MUMMIES.
N.S.W. State Parliament—the 20th—has
met for its second session, and the first business
paper simply reeks with petty parochial
motions and questions. There is a request for
Parliament to interfere in the matter of a King-
street fruit merchant’s fines ; in the matter of the
discharge of a man from the railway yards; in the
matter of the employment of four stonemasons by
the Works Department. Select Committees are
asked for, to report on some claim or demand
against the Railway Commissioners, on a re-
appraisement application, and on the suspension
of a bookbinder from the State employ. It is
natural that a Parliament which devotes its atten-
tion to such matters should have no time to estab-
lish a workable Local Government system, or to
create an iron industry, or to straighten the
finances, or to do anything really important. It
is natural, too, that it should be a Parliament of
men with parish souls controlled by a Premier
with a parish-pump soul.
At last N.S.W. State election Premier
Carruthers talked at first of the total abolition of
borrowing, and though he tapered off a good deal,
he was solid even at the end on keeping loan
expenditure down to £1,500,000 a year. He has
got through a trifle of £1,833,700 in eleven
months, and he puts this in the Governor’s speech
at the opening of Parliament: —
The necessity for the completion of the many works
and undertakings that, under the authority of Parlia-
ment, have been entered upon, has prevented, and will
for some time prevent, the volume of State borrowing-
being- reduced to the limited dimensions considered
desirable by my Advisers ; but it has been, and, is being,
greatly curtailed. It is hoped that the day is close at
hand when borrowing outside the Commonwealth, except
for renewal purposes, will be entirely discontinued.
It isn't being greatly curtailed—in fact, during
the last three months (March, April and May)
the loan expenditure was £130,505 more than in
the corresponding three months of last year under
Waddell. The rest of the clause is simply a bald
announcement that Carruthers isn’t going to keep
his promise, and that he got votes under false
pretences.
GEORGE'S NIGHTMARE.
“Mr. Reid has done a lot of train-travelling during his
political career, but on his present visit to Queensland
the Prime Minister is beating all previous records. The
initial portion of his trip was a journey of nearly 1000
miles, from Sydney to Bundaberg, accomplished in two
big jumps with an half-hour interval between the jumps.
He had barely time to dust his clothes and dine in Bunda-
berg before he climbed into another train, and sped to-
Bingera plantation. That train has been at his disposal
ever since—a special engine, a special car, a special officer,
and the three combined are forming into a spectre, a
spectre that startles Mr. Reid in his sleep. He feels that
his bed is being drawn by the engine, and that at the foot
stands a railway official.”
“ Faster! Faster!—we mustn't let Watson
catch up!"
Apparently there may be a High Court
case before Victoria loses one of its representa-
tives. The Constitution says that representation
shall go according to population as shown by “ the
latest statistics of the Commonwealth.” Victoria
claims that there are no statistics except at census-
time—only estimates; also, that there are no-
Commonwealth estimates, but only State esti-
mates by State officials, the Commonwealth having-
set up no statistician as yet.

DIRECTORS’ REPORT.
To the Shareholders O.K. Copper Mines Development Syndicate, N.L.
Ladies and Gentlemen, —The Directors have the honor to present herewith for vour consideration the Half-Yearly
Report and Balance-Sheet of the transactions of your Company from the 6th November, 1904, to 31st March, 1905,
together with the report of the General Manager, Mr. A. Gibb, the Mining Engineer, Mr. Peter Brander, and the Auditors.
The period Under review is a most Important one in the history of your Company. Your financial position on
the 24th May may be taken as follows :
Net value of matte on hand after allowing for cost of transit to Liverpool and all realisation charges.. £13,317
Surplus on shipments in transit 5,362
Cash in hand at Bank ’6go
, 19,359
Less outstanding liabilities, including wages 4,359
Balance available ■. .. £15,000
You will observe by the General Manager’s Report that he is confident of producing 300 tons of copper monthly.
At the present price of copper, this should yield a monthly net profit of £lO,OOO. Viewing the financial position, and
with the prospects for the future Apparently assured, as shown by the General Manager’s and the Consulting Engi-
neer’s reports, your Directors feel they are justified in declaring a first dividend of ss. per share, pavable on the 14th
June.
The total expenditure on plant to date has been about £6OOO, whilst the plant is in every way adequate to con-
tinue the present output, your Directors are of opinion that this could be greatly improved by the addition of a
Converter plant, which they have decided to purchase. This will permit of the percentage of the matte being raised
from, say, 45 per cent, to 50 per cent, as at present, to Blister Copper, thereby effecting a very considerable saving in
the transit charges, which, owing to the isolation of your property, is the heaviest expense.
The mine, as you are aware, is situated by present routes 46 miles from Mungana, the railway terminus, 150 miles
from Cairns. Considerable difficulty was experienced in obtaining carriers from the mine to Mungana. Advertise-
ments were inserted in the leading newspapers throughout the State, calling tenders for any quantity from 50 to 400
tons per month, and, although unsuccessful in getting horse teams, finally arrangements were made with the Burke
Carrying Company for the conveyance of 400 tons per month at £2 10s. per ton from the mine to Mungana, with all
back loading at the same rate.
Mr. Peter Brander was appointed Consulting Mining Engineer, to report to the Board periodically the progress
made, and attached herewith is his last report. The figures therein may be accepted as an independent confirmation
of the General Manager’s estimates. The limestone required in smelting is procurable in unlimited quantities within
a distance of 30 or 11 miles of the works, and contracts lately entered into will ensure a constant supply at a con-
siderably reduced rate. Provision is being made for wet season stocks of both limestone and coke. In order to mini-
mise the delay in transit during the wet season, it has been decided to construct a bridge over the Walsh River, at an
estimated cost of £l5OO, of which the Government have promised £5OO.
Messrs. MacArthur and Co., of Glasgow, have been appointed your selling agents, and the whole of yourship-
™ en ,, are ,’ 3ei .ng disposed of in the open market. The quantity realised upon to date of the books being balanced
(31st March) is small, as the matte shipments are of but recent despatch.
In response to a, request from a large number of shareholders that the shares should be subdivided, your Directors
have called a special general meeting of shareholders, to be held at the conclusion of this meeting to consider the
question.
The directors visited the mine during the past week, and after consultation with the General Manager, a vigorous
policy of systematic development was decided upon. The drives north and south at the 80ft. level are being pushed
forward continuously, and the two winzes sunk to the 160 ft. level. '
Smelting operations are now being conducted continuously, which will ensure you regular monthly dividends.
» h7 OU j , ectors confidently look forward to a considerable enhancement in the value of the mine, arising from
further development work ; and are satisfied that, in addition to maintaining the present output for the smelters, the
ore reserves will be considerably increased. ’
anticipations S^°n ’ '° Ur ® ir ** tors congratulate you upon owning a property that has fully realised the most sanguine
I am, Yours Obediently, JOHN NEWELL, Chairman.
Premier Daglish, in Westralia, is drifting
into trouble. Lately, without warning, he called
on all his colleagues to resign ; some of them were
out of town, and to them he simply wired that
their resignations were wanted at once. When
they arrived he threw out “Mulga” Taylor and
Holman, and took in Bath and Lynch. Works
Minister Johnson, the strongest man in the crowd,
is said to have been behind the shuffle; there was,
anyway, an old feud between him and the two
who were displaced. The change is generally
reckoned to have strengthened the Ministry from
a personal point of view, but the way in which it
was made left a sore behind it—several sores, in
fact.
The revival in Queensland cotton-growing has got
so far as to necessitate the re-starting of the old
ginning mill and factory at East Ipswich to deal
with the present crop. Apropos , H. P. Wynne,
representative of an Oldham (Eng.) firm of textile
machinery makers, who has been visiting the
plantations, told a Melbourne Age interviewer :
From what I have seen of the production of cotton this
season in Queensland I am convinced that, at any rate for
miles around Brisbane, it can be grown to perfection by
white labor. This season the farmers have been able to
get from 1500 to 18001 b. of cotton in seed per acre. It
takes 31b.. in the seed to make lib. of lint. That is a pay-
able yield to the farmer. If you take it at only 12001 b.
to the acre and only allow Id. per lb.—-the farmer is likely
to get ljd. for uplands, and 2d. or 3d. for the long-staple
Sea Islands variety—as the price of cotton in seed, the
farmer gets £5 per acre for his trouble, and that should
be a payable yield.
It certainly should be a payable yield. The
average value of crops in N.S.W. (mostly a white
labor State) is about £2 Bs. per acre, and if £2 Bs.
per acre can pay white men’s wages, £5 should be
able to do it without bursting itself with the strain.
For the eleven months to May 31, 1904,
the N.S.W. loan expenditure on public works
(apart from the Sydney land resumptions) was
£1,614,167. That was under the frantic Owe’
Sullivan regime. For the eleven months to
May, 1905, the amount (apart from resump-
tions) is £1,502,169. That is under the great
Reform party. The difference hardly seems
worth all the perspiration which dropped off the
State in its struggles to get Joe into office. There
was quite a pool of moisture round it by the time
it had got its grand and beneficial and economical
Joe in.
Yes-No Reid didn’t advance his cause
much in Queensland. One of the biggest business
men in Brisbane after the Reid meeting put it
this way :
I went on the platform feeling proud of our champion,
and indignant at the way that the scurrilous Bulletin
attacks him. I came away feeling that he deserved it
all. A meeting ? It wasn’t a meeting: it was a show. I
felt as if I were a chair-warmer at one of Rickards’ variety
shows, looking on while a nigger comedian performed.
Socialism? He didn’t say anything sensible about it.
Here’s a cranky lot of Labor men threatening to ruin the
country, and we’re asked to follow this song and dance
man’s lead in opposing them. Let’s send for George
Lauri.

In the Commonwealth Gazette (20/5/’O5)
it takes 150 words and three solid inches of space
to announce the appointment of two little polling-
places in Victoria and Tasmania, and the whole
thing begins with the following tremendous cry of
a strong Ex. in his agony :
Commonwealth of Australia, to Northcote,
Governor-General. By his Excellency the Right Honor-
able Henry Stafford, Baron Northcote, Knight Grand
Cross of the Most Distinguished Order of Saint Michael
and Saint George, Knight Grand Commander of the Most
Eminent Order of the Indian Empire, Companion of the
Most Honorable Order of the Bath, Governor-General and
Commander-in-Chief of the Commonwealth of Australia.
Why should not the Commonwealth make brief
but necessary announcements in a businesslike
way ,and why this loud buzzing of the Ply on
the Wheel?
Canvassing by the agents of the Yankee
harvester combine runs something on this line :
“Oh 1 you’ve ordered a ‘ Sunshine ’ (for instance).
Yes; it’s a good machine. We don’t want to run it
down, but we say we have a better one. I’ll just show you
why. When our people thought of sending harvesters to
Australia, they had one of every Australian make sent to
America, and' after studying the construction of all of
them, picked out the best points of each, which are now
embodied in this machine.”
Which is a brazen admission of the piracy of
Australian brains and energy. Not only in
harvesters, but in ploughs, has the same exploiting
been practised. One Yankee firm has representa-
tives travelling about whose boast is that their
plough is the result of an exploiting of every
Australian plough yet invented.
Australian detectives complain bitterly
over the gradually increasing difficulties of their
work. “It takes twice the trouble to get a job
that it did 10 years ago, ” said the leading ‘ ‘ demon
in one State recently. “The young criminals
growing up now won’t open their mouths. At
one time, when a job occurred, I could go round a
few places and get 20 men who’d be only too glad
to earn half-a-sovereign by putting me on. ; But
since, they’ve gradually shut up, and its as
hard to get men who know about a job to talk as
it is to get the man himself.” ‘‘Then theres
honor among Australian thieves?” “Urn, yes,
he replied, sadly, “I suppose you can put it that
way.”
White workers of S’Africa, according to
the cables, are now finding that, as a consequence
of the importation of Chinese labor,
the wages of the white workers have diminished, and the
ratio of whites to colored laborers has been reduced.
The S’African white man of British race seems
to be a very dull sort of person. He killed oft
Kruger to get himself a vote and a better job.
When Kruger was demolished, the vote and the
improved job didn’t turn up, but when the people
who had deceived him once assured him that these
blessings would come when the Chinese arrived,
he believed them again. Now most of him has no
job at all, and no prospect of ever having a vote.
There may be some kind of property franchise
hanging about, but the shoeless unemployed won t
get any of it.
The anti-Socialists in Westralia are seeing
who can yell the loudest for more Socialism.
Mine-owners want cheaper State water, coal
companies want a State subsidy, timber com-
panies want their jarrah carried by the State at a
loss, and the farmers want the whole box of tricks.
They all want to lower wages too. Timber em-
ployers met the men in conference, the other day,
and gave them the choice of taking less wages or
joining in the demand for lower freights. Em-
ployees, against the advice of their road-and-
culvert representatives, said they would neither
consent to a reduction nor take part in a buc-
caneering expedition against the State, which was
a sturdy and notable maintenance of their duty
as citizens against their interests as employees.
“O’Fipp ” ; My latest tailor is a fervent
loyalist, mainly because King Ned once spoke to
him. Working as cutter for a London firm, he
had the peerless privilege of cutting a coat for the
then Prince of Wales, and “the Prince was most
affable —such a gracious manner.” The tailor has
a photo of himself, with the royal coat hanging
over his arm, in a prominent position in his shop.
He never tires of telling people all about it. Of
such is the kingdom of crawl.

SPORTING NOTIONS
The second test-match started at Lords’ Ground
on Thursday last in weather described as lovely. The
English were at the wicket. Hayward was the first to
leave (l.b.w. for 16), but the start was an excellent one
and the day closed with eight English wickets
down for 258, of which Maclaren had made 56 off
his own. Rain fell through the night, but Friday
was a beautiful day. Before noon, the English innings
had closed for 282, Fry’s 73 being top score. Australia
started fairly well, running up 110 for four wickets ; then
the wicket began to dry, which favored the bowlers. A
slump set in, five wickets fell for 50 runs, and the
side was out for 181. The English second innings
started 101 to the good, and found Maclaren again in
excellent form, and he knocked up 79 before Armstrong
bowled him. Fry came out strongly, too, and at the end
of the day they had five wickets down for 151 runs.
Saturday brought heavy rain to the rescue of the kan-
garoos, and by reducing the wicket to a slough, made a
drawn game of what would otherwise almost certainly
have been a decisive defeat.
• * « »
The continued failure of “demon”
trundler Cotter has brought about its own reward—
exclusion from the second test match. Cotter was
drafted into the team as the last hope, but thus far he
has failed dismally, having taken (up to the second test)
only 23 wickets at an average of 29'34. With the excep-
tion of maiming a couple of players, Cotter hasn’t even
created a sensation, and all the praise of the London
sporting papers is wasted.
* * * *
How these cricketers do run to journalism !
Frank Iredale is “doing’’the Australian XI. for London
Daily Mail ; L. O. S. Poidevin writes it up for Sydney
Referee ; and now the London correspondent of Sydney
Telegraph announces that Trumper will exercise his
injured back as representative for London Daily
Chronicle. When the team left, manager Laver declared
with emphasis, that none of the members would be
allowed to act as newspaper correspondents. Does this
mean that Trumper has definitely retired from the team,
or that Laver’s back is injured also ?
• # # *
Ex-Australian captain Murdoch called the
Surrey match “ Weary Willie cricket.”
• * * *
Understood that the Maoriland Government is
aiding and abetting a proposal to import an English
cricket team next year as a sort of side show in connec-
tion with the International Exhibition to be held at
Christchurch.
• • • *
The first match against the Maoriland “Soccer ”
f ootballers found both teams lacking somewhat in com-
bination. Still, play was interesting, and sometimes very
pretty ; and the kicking, though occasionally ill-judged,
was in itself very clean. Although the Sydney Metro-
politan team won by three goals to two, it was anybody’s
game almost to the end, and the even balance of things
was indicated by the fact that all the scoring was done in
the first half. By the time the second spell was begun
the teams had dropped down to one another’s little ways,
and although play was fast and strenuous, but somewhat
too individualistic, neither side managed to score. The
Maoriland men seems to be suffering from want of com-
bined practice, and, by the time they have had it, they
will be certainly a very formidable lot. The Cornstalks
have seriously jeopardised their chances by leaving the
picking of their teams till the last minute.
*r W m #
Saturday’s round of Rugby in Sydney saw the
still undefeated Glebe roll up the struggling Eastern
Suburbs by 10 to nil; and at the Sports Ground South
Sydney, which is only one point behind the victorious
Glebe, demolished Balmain by 12 to 3. Next Saturday
Glebe and South Sydney meet. The South Sydney backs
impress this writer as being better than those of Glebe,
but the Glebe pack is just as superior to that of South
Sydney, and it ought to be a game worth going far to see.
Sydney District came to utter smash at the feet of New-
town, which trampled it in the mud at Rushcutters’ Bay
by 10 to nil. At the Uni. Oval the Varsity went down
before North Sydney by 7 to 3, and but for the lack of
ioncerted play between the Northmen’s back and for-
ward divisions the downfall would have been much worse.
The result of the day’s battling was to put Glebe and
South Sydney further in front than ever ; Glebe now
heads the list with 13 points, and South Sydney is running
close with 12. Then come North Sydney, Newtown and
Western Suburbs with nine each. If Glebe wins the
match next week against South Sydney, the chances are
that some of the club will take to drink—if it loses, they
may suicide.
* * * *
At last, and at the boots of Redfern, the vic-
torious career of the Y.M.O.A. came to a stop on Satur-
day. Their high kicking hampered them against the
wind, and Redfern, which was playing in its own back-
yard, won by two points, the final scores being 54 to 52.
The Redferns scored 7 goals 12 behinds and the Good
Young Men 8 goals 4 behinds, which, as the cat naturally
remarked when it felt the dog’s breath on its tail as it
fled over the fence, was mighty close. Two other winners
under Australian rules were Newtown, which beat Syd-
ney by 41 to 20 ; and East Sydney, which just managed to
finish 6 points ahead of Paddington, the beaten side
making 24 to the victors’ 30. But it was reserved
for Balmain to supply the slaughter, the untidy suburb
with the questionable smell wrecking West Sydney at
the rate of 99 to 4. This leaves Y.M.O.A. still at the head
of the competition list with one win more than Sydney,
the next best on the roll. There will be no club matches
under Australian rules in Sydney next Saturday. That
day has been given up to the struggle between Fitzroy
and South Melbourne teams, which will be played at Syd-
ney Cricket Ground for educational purposes. After
watching the Australian game for a reasonable time, this
writer has come to the conclusion that it is rougher and
more dangerous than Rugby. On the other hand, the
referees under Australian rules are much quicker to
detect foul play, and more prompt to exercise their
authority than the present Rugby men. If the Rugby
fellows did their work half as well there would be few
charges of rough play. It would be too costly.
* * * *
N.S.W. Rugbyite Stan. Wickham will be
taking a jubilee, or something, shortly, having kept his
place for 10 consecutive years—since 1895. He is a three-
quarter-back of credit and renown, and has captained the
State team on more than one occasion.
♦ * # *
Wellington (M.L.) Rugby Union intends dis-
couraging cannibalism as far as possible—so it says. A
case which is alleged to have happened during a recent
match is under investigation at time of writing. The
general presumption is that the gentleman hadn’t lunched
before the game started.
* * « *
Maoriland is saved ! The great army of Rugby
exponents in that country won’t need to worry in their
cots at night over their rep. team in England, for it has
been definitely decided to send “ Jimmy ” Duncan as
coach. As a 'man has been knocked off the 26 billed to
go, in order to allow the bald-headed veteran to join,
“Jimmy” may don a jersey in many a match ere he re-
turns. He has been showing all his old-time dash at
Dunedin this season, and is better than the vast majority
in the team. The only thing urged against Duncan is that
he’s a working man, and this element has hitherto been
strictly excluded from the high-toned combination.
* * * *
Southland (M.L.) Rugby Union likes to hear
its own voice holding forth to offending pursuers of the
muddy sphere. Culprits have hitherto taken the dis-
qualification without attending, but it is now decreed
that a suspended player must turn up at the hearing* of
his case, or at least before his term of disqualification
expires, in order that he may be spoken to in a deep
voice.
“P.” : Watching two boys’ teams playing foot
ball in a Sydney suburb, the other day, I noticed that,
generally, players were booted as to the right foot, naked
as to the left. The football is very hard on the bare toe,
and only about half of each team possessed boots; so
those with boots divided with the bootless for the game—
any shortage being made up by the barrackers. Fit was
amicably arranged between teams, blokes with large feet
lending their lefts to coves who took ’em several sizes
smaller. The arrangement looked awkward, but seemed
to act well enough. I saw one little cove with a large
left boot kick a goal in brilliant fashion.
* * * *
The V.R.C. takes very little risk of making a
bad handicap for the Grand National Hurdle Race. The
race is due on July Ist, and the weights were not pub-
lished until June 12th.
• • • •
In the action for damages brought by book-
maker Sluice against Adelaide Racing Club officials for
forcible removal from the course whilst laying the odds,
Justice Homburg found for the defendants. He
held that the ejectment was within the club’s rights; the
fact of plaintiff purchasing an entry ticket no more
entitling him to use the club premises for his betting busi-
ness than it gave a rabbito or bottlo the right to use
them for his vociferous rabbit or bottle occupation.
• » * »
Merrion, by winning the Toolambool Hurdle at
Caulfield last Saturday, followed in the footsteps of stable
companion Marmont, who took the same race in 1903
before winning the Grand National. Marmont then
carried 41b. more than his weight for the bigger event,
and Merrion did likewise the other day. Which doesn’t
alter the fact that Marmont’s burden was 211 b. less than
Merrion’s. A stone and a half is a big difference over the
Grand National course, and even supposing that Merrion
is as good to-day as the “ crack ” hurdler was two years
ago, the race looks pretty open as far as the mare is con-
cerned. All the same, she ought to beat Orlando again
on recent “form.”
» * * *
Was Merrion born a male or a female ? Yol. 17
of Miller’s Racing Chronicle describes Merrion as a mare,
but in Vol. 19 the animal appears as a gelding.
• * * *
David James, of Adelaide, who won the Mel-
bourne Cup with the mare Auraria, years ago, is sending
two of her progeny to Flemington to be trained by Dick
Bradfield. Auraria thusfar has been a disappointment as
a stud matron, although she comes of a brilliant family,
and her own performances were fine examples of game-
ness combined with speed. The Auraria and Wallace
“dead heat,” after they had respectively won the Cup
and Derby, is remembered as a wonderful struggle.
* * # *
Lord Rosebery’s third Derby winner (Cicero)
had a backer who took £30,000 to £lO,OOO about the colt
in one wager previous to his winning the Newmarket
Stakes, a few days before the classic race. Punters of
that sort may get along prosperously if they are backing
an honest jockey as well as a good horse, owned by a
swell owner. Scandalous cases of jostling or blocking are
seldom or never heard of in connection with favorites
from powerful stables in the Cold Country. But the indi-
vidual rider, of course, is likely to be approached by a
bookmaker who has accepted a wager of £30,000 to £lO,OOO
on Lord Muck’s terracotta colt Coughdrop. And sooner
or later the plunging punter risks his thousand “quids”
upon an animal that isnlt owned by a Rosebery sort of
lord, and then the swindle is fixed up quite easily. Thirty
or forty years ago the father of the present Lord Dudley
amused himself—he was a tired person who grew his hair
long to conceal the loss of his ears—by backing “dead-
birds” for as much as twenty and thirty thousand pounds
at a time. At first it seemed like picking up money, but
pretty soon the “good things” began to suffer from
“extraordinary reversals of form.” Then the tired Earl
turned away from horseracing, and took to spiritualism
or something.
* * * *
The great scarcity of lightweight jockeys in
Maoriland was touched upon by G. G. Stead at the annual
meeting of the Canterbury J.O. Stead puts it down to
the fact that during the past generation Maoriland youths
had been well and properly fed and reared under im-
proved sanitary conditions, with the result that they
were relatively far heavier than their badly-fed compeers
in England of two or three generations back. Stead advo-
cates raising the minimum weight in handicaps to 7st.,
in preference to lowering the dwarf boy to the present
minimum.
* * * *
The Melbourne police have of late gone to
enormous trouble and expense to suppress the betting
shops that have recently sprung up, like Jonah’s vege-
table, all over city and suburbs. Nearly all are run in
tobacconists’, hairdressers’ or lollie shops, and the Ikey
responsible pays either starting prices (as per daily paper)
or tote prices (the div. returned by Wren’s Collingwood
tote). Wren is supposed to finance many of these gam-
bling places, they being, it is suspected, only branches of
the big barricaded establishment that has so long defied
the law. To get over the judicial decision that a solitary
wager, or even two or three, do not prove a place to have
been “ habitually ” used for betting, the police have for
months past been accumulating evidence, themselves
making as many as a dozen wagers in each place, and be-
tween June 14th and 28th over 100 occupiers and their
assistants will interview the city or suburban courts
about the matter. Three “places,” so far, defy the Law
and that decrepit entity has as yet discovered no way
of getting at them. The conductors of these have
formed limited liability companies, and registered
themselves under the Companies Act. Learned and
expensive lawyers were employed to draw up the
articles of association, and so far their position has
proved impregnable. Each co. has a share register of
very many shares at 2s. each—to be fully paid up on
allotment. Directors are regularly elected, a manager
appointed, and everything is in strict commercial order.
The “ objects ” of the co. include “ the assistance of horse-
racing," “ the purchase of racecourses and other proper-
ties,” and “ anything else calculated to advance the in-
terests of the members of the company”—which latter is
wide and large enough to cover almost any object under
the sun. These three are nominally genuine public com-
panies, formed for a legal, useful purpose; but, in effect,
anyone may go in, buy one share at 25., and so become a
member of the company, then pass into the co.’s back
office and “ assist horse-racing.”
* * # #
N.S.W. is rich in young racehorses this season
—4O of 124 entered for Maribyrnong Plate have been
nominated by that State.
* * * •
The tout and the other animal who goes to
Randwick in the' small hours of the morning, when the
burglar is just turning in ; , are much impressed with
Maoriland horse Machine Gun. He has a fine appearance
and a fair record, having won 12 times in 19 starts, and
accumulated £4165 prize-money in three seasons.
* * * *
If this country ever replaces the horse with the
motor it will give the “ poor farmer ” a considerable lift.
European motor builders have discovered that, so long as
one doesn’t want to break records, alcohol is as good as
gasoline or petrol for fuel, and. it has the advantage of
being cheaper, easier to handle, and obtainable any where.
Alcohol for industrial and locomotive purposes means
vast crops of potatoes, and potatoes are what the Free-
trade papers call one of “ our great primary industries.”
- * * T *
Beach beat Gaudaur, Gaudaur beat Stanbury,
Towns beat Gaudaur, and now Beach trains Stanbury to
beat Towns.
* * * *
Premier Bent has announced that the “bloomin’
cyclists” who injure the roads of Victoria should be taxed.
Before the cyclists can injure the roads, Bent or his
Kyabramite shire councils will have to make some roads
to be injured. Anyhow, no road that is properly made
can possibly be cut up by a bicycle. It’s the “poor
farmer” that Bent will have to come at with a Width of
Tyres Act. A good idea would be to levy a wheel tax,
and spend the whole of the money on making a few real
roads.
.** * *
The automobile racing track at Aspendale
Park (Vic.) is now completed, and must have cost J. R_
Orooke a power of money. It is a mile in circumference,
nicely banked at the corners, and wide enough for several
cars to ride abreast- Although a motor-bike negotiated
it in lmin. 37sec. recently, after the heavy rain, the pro-
prietor does not intend to let anyone officially risk his
neck on it till Melbourne Cup week, when it will be
formally opened by a big automobile race meeting.
An inventive genius at Dunedin (M.L.) put
together a steam carriage about 24 years ago. It was a
rather cumbrous affair, but could achieve 33 miles an
hour. The vehicle was sent to Christchurch Exhib.tion
before the rubber tyres arrived from England, and the
iron rims gave out, the show collapsing on the Port
Hills. The boiler is now in use by a Dunedin sausage
merchant.
* * * *
N.S.W. Cyclists Union inter-club road-race
premiership event should be a good one. The prelimi-
nary heats will be over 20 miles, semi-finals over 30 miles,
and final over 40 miles. The winning club will thus have
to keep its nose on the front tyre for 90 miles.
* * * *
Just at present negro cyclist Major Taylor is in
the soup. This pious man used to say that when he rode
he could feel another foot beside his own on the pedal,
but apparently the other party has taken his foot off
lately, for quite recently Major started explaining that
it was just possible that he might not fulfil his engage-
ment to race Kramer at the World’s Championship meet-
ing. Kramer, who is anxious to meet Taylor, appealed to
the Yankee governing body, and it promptly called upon
Taylor to give an assurance that he would fulfil his
written engagement. Taylor told the Council to mind its
own interference, and the Council retorted with a
suspension that blocks his racing anywhere in the world,
till he makes up his mind what he is going to do. Taylor
will now climb down if he wants to make any more money
on the track—if he doesn’t, he will stick his chest out,
and pose in the limelight as a martyr driven out of the
game in his prime. He has a very comfortable home at
Worcester, and is quite affluent enough to spend the rest
of his days driving round in his motor car to collect his
rents. His wife, who was greatly scared when Lawson
upset him in Melbourne, has been very anxious for him to
retire from the track before he gets broken, and this
latest development may incline him to grant her wish.
“Doping and firing” of dogs was much in evi-
dence during the running of the Waterloo Cup at Rooty
Hill. Watchful stewards and an experienced vet. were
quite unable to compete with the “ smart ” men who ran
the dogs, so they just delayed the slipping till the effects
of the drug had fizzled out, and left the loaded animals
limp and much below their regular form. Then the
runners changed their tactics and doped the dogs they
wanted to lose ; and again fell in with a plunk, as the
dog they wanted to lose more than any other was strong
enough to win in spite of the fact that he was “ crooked.”
* * * *
The City-Redfern hurling match on Saturday
last, at Moore Park (Sydney), was a great game. Last
time they met the City won after a very strenuous
struggle. Having won the toss, Redfern played with a
wind that blew straight down the field into the City goal.
What with the powerful breeze and the ferocity of the
attack the City was blown right out, and in less than
three minutes the men from McGowen’s electorate had
secured a goal and a point. The City pulled itself
together, and belted the bung to some purpose, but Red-
fern came again and lifted the score to eight points
(2 goals, 2 points). City got frantic, and, having tight-
ened its belt, managed to keep the ball in the middle of
the field for a while, but the wind blew harder and half-
time found Redfern 14 points and the City with a blank
sheet. With the change of end the City improved, but
only a little, and the game ended in a victory for Redfern
by 22 to 5 points.
* * *
Paddington and Woollahra, the two undefeated
baseball teams which are running neck and neck with 14
points each in the competition, meet next Saturday at
Rushcutter’s Bay, and people who are interested in this
curious form of amusement will get some excitement out
of it. It is always interesting when two unbeaten teams
meet to decide which is Jonah.
* * » *
The defeat of North Sydney lacrosse team by
Marrickville was not unexpected, for the Marrickvillers,
who have developed speed escaping from floods, were
both fast and facile with their sticks. In fact, it was the
best exhibition the Marrickvillers have yet put up. On
the other hand, the Shoremen were trying out some colts
who, although they played up well, were not at home,
and naturally thereby destroyed the combination of the
team, and Marrickville won by five goals to one. Burwood
went across to Balmain and got defeated by exactly the
same score. The Water-rats played a first-class offensive
game, one of those sustained attacks against which the
only defence is to secure the ball and become the attack-
ing party in turn, and the big capitalists from Burwood
were not able. Burwood goal-keeper Henriques, a full-
back on whom there are no winged insects, played the
game, but the fast passing of the Loutit brothers for
Balmain beat him.
Clarence Weber has undertaken to put the
wrestling championship on a white basis. He is to meet
Buttan Singh at Fitzgerald’s Circus building, Melbourne,
on July Ist, and the black man has signified his confidence
in his Hindu self by putting up £3O against Weber’s £25.
with the proviso that the winner is to draw a clear £lOO
of “gate” (if it’s in the house) before the loser gets any
percentage whatever. Which suggests that Buttan
doesn’t quite understand what sort of an expert Weber
has become since Hackenschmidt educated him.
* * * *
Hackenschmidt is a rare combination of muscle-
man, real champion wrestler, true “sport” and level-
headed show-worker. His recent match with Tom
Jenkins in New York revealed the full strengthof Hackey
as a tactician. Last year he met Jenkins in London and
beat him after “ playing light ” with the Yank, champion
for 17 minutes. When he got to America a while ago he
allowed his old antagonist to chase him with challenges
until they had arrived at New York where the big gate-
money comes from, and there the nuggetty “Russian
Lion” consented to a match. Result a couple of pro-
tracted bouts—3l minutes and 22 minutes respectively—
both of them ending in the foreigner’s favor, but neither
of them calculated to break the heart of the local idol.
Probably the utter discomfiture of Jenkins will come
when his friends back him for a big sum to last out 10 or
15 minutes against Hackey, who always plays to win, but
is seldom tempted to chuck his opponent rightaway as he
did Gunga Brahm at Melbourne Exhibition building.
The lightheartedness of the Russian helps to make him
the clever general he is. He has a twinkling eye
for business and a lively sense of humor. For instance,
there’s the story of the inflexible small boy whom
he met at Frankston one Sunday, when he was the
guest of Syd Day, the Melbourne printer. They had no
milk in the house, and when tea-time drew near Day
(with Hackey) journeyed forth on his motor-car to get
milk. A very small urchin came along bearing a jug-full
of the liquor they were looking for, and Day, dismounting
from his car, tried to do a deal. “Sonny ” couldn’t be in-
duced to trade. The milk was for a customer, and he
daren’t sell it at any price. At last, when the nipper had
refused an offer of half-a-crown, Hackenschmidt crept up
to Day on tip-toe and said in a bloodthirsty whisper:
“ Let us both togeder knock der feller down und take it.
Ve can escape on der motor-car.”
* * • *
“Uppercut” : Supporters of the rival systems
of German and English-speaking races in physical
development overlook the fact that the respective ends
of the two systems differ. Germany, with its heavy,
laborious weight-lifting and gymnastic training, aims at
strength—plain, muscle-bound strength' and a race of
blocks, who require a crane and tackle to turn them
round. The British system of athletics' makes for
activity. I have no doubt whatever that the gymnasium-
made man is much stronger than the average boxer,
footballer or oarsman, but he would be a hopeless man in
any one of those sports. In wrestling, a game slow' in
its movements, and in which strength is a great
factor, the bloeky foreign person is perhaps supreme, but,
in games of speed, the Germanic and Slavonic races have
yet done nothing, and never will under their present
policy. Whatever advertisers of various systems of phy-
sloal oulfcure say, every man who has studied the game
knows that slow strength-making exercises make a man
muscle-bound. Hackenschmidt was a freak in activity,
but he is the only one of all the strong men who can
move without hurting himself in the job. Further, in
spite of the big, lumbering strength-machines that the
German gymnasiums turn out, their effects are not uni-
form. The German sailors on visiting warships, for
instance, are not in it as regards physique with the
shapely, active, little British tars. My own opinion is
that the two systems should be brought together. Weedy
children, not able to hold their own at athletics, now
drop out and get no exercise at all. These might well be
built into strong, if slow, healthy men and women, while
the German system might well be relaxed in favor of the
Britishers’ games. In a properly-governed community, a
Government medical officer should have power to seize
any stooping, anaemic boy or girl—especially girl—and
send him or her for six months to a State physical culture
school, where nothing would be taught but bodily deve-
lopment.
* * * *
The George Johns-Al. Neil fight for the welter-
weight championship was a good fight with an unsatis-
factory' decision. Johns’ footwork was excellent, and
saved the local man much punishment; on the other
hand, Neil’s smothering tactics were just as effective, and
the American was the favorite for the greater part of the
battle, which went the full length. At the end referee
Ford said, “ I must give it to Johns on the punishment
meted out.” Which was utterly ridiculous. There was
practically no punishment. Neil was bleeding slightly
from the bridge of t,he nos? and the upper lip, through
the opening of only partially-healed cuts received while
training. Punishment there was none, and, anyhow, it
is points, not punishment, that count while both men are
on their feet and able to continue. The decision is the
most absurd ever delivered in a Sydney bruising hall.
* * * *
Sydney Gaiety Athletic Hall will not require
much cleaning up after the brief and mild Frost-Lenton
disturbance on Saturday night, for the affair left no traces
behind it save a loud odor of fish. Lenton started as
though anxious to impress the house with the deadliness
of his intentions, whirling his arms, stamping his feet, and
making most amazing faces. Mr. Frost did not seem to
be much impressed, but poked the man from the bone-
mill district familiarly in the ribs. In the second round,
Mr. Lenton was apparently tired; and Mr. Frost, who
was politeness itself, refrained from bustling him in any
way. So they just revolved round one another till the
gong sent them to their corners guiltless of nose-blood.
Requests from the audience that they wouldn’t die of old
age on the premises cheered them up somewhat in the
third campaign ; and Mr. Lenton let his right glove fall
on Mr. Frost’s neck with amazing results. The hardy
battler from Newtown fell as though trodden on by
elephants. When he got up and staggered about
the ring, Mr. Lenton made at him, and whirled
his fists in a terrifying manner; but Frost grabbed
his enemy and embraced him till the gong went. At
the sound of the armistice Frost fell off Lenton as a dead
fish drops off a rock. While they were in their corners
anxious sports begged to be taken up at any price on
Frost, who had gone to his chair apparently almost
unable to stand, but nobody took any notice. The fourth
round saw Lenton tap Frost on the side of his nose, and
again the man from Newtown heeled over like a sinking
battleship. Lenton whirled his fists and rushed at him,
and the sinking Frost gave a grunt and an uppercut.
Lenton turned up his eyes just as Csesar did when Brutus
assassinated him, and sank on the floor. Frost was de-
clared winner. It was a fearful and wonderful exhibition.
* * * *
Announced in the United States that the gentle
ex-boiler-maker will retire from the heavyweight cham-
pionship of the world, the ring, and the stage simultane-
ously. This is in deference to the wishes of Mrs. Jeffries,
who has always had an aversion to the prize-ring, and
who now finds that James is in far more danger in the
green-room than on the athletic club premises. Ever
since Jeffries appeared as Davy Crockett he has been
rushed by affectionate women and overwhelmed with
baskets of letters, and the maids have been unable to get
the housework done through rushing to the door to take
in flowers. And Mrs. Jeffries has got tired —so Jimmy
will go into the corn business, or something equally prosy.
» * * *
“Jab.”: The blow with which McCall doubled
up Dan Oreedon was similar to that with which Bob Fitz-
simmons sent Corbett to rest, in the match for the cham-
pionship of the world. If it is fairly delivered it is impos-
sible to recover from it within the allotted 10 seconds.
Through fear of injuring that game shoulder of his,
McCall has developed a right half-arm drive that comes
from the side and is about as nice a thing to meet as the
rush of a locomotive.
Ted Nelson, the Australian pug., beat Jack
Lalor at Jo’burg last month, and such is human nature,
the Britishers are adopting a slightly more reverential
tone towards “ those Australians.” Nelson, adopting a
sort of Yankee crouch, open style, rushed things as if he
were on a contract to level Lalor’s face down. Lalor was
the S’African middleweight champion.
A Yankee parson, one Dr. Watson, has
been apologising in Maoriland for Noah’s drink-
ing habits. The gentleman with the twang says
that from the time of Adam down to that of Noah
there was no “ actin ” in the atmosphere, and
hence no corruption or fermentation. Alcohol
was, therefore, an unknown quantity, and the
juice of the grape before the Flood was harmless.
Noah, not being acquainted with the altered con-
dition of things, got deplorably drunk. This
theory isn’t supported by a bottle of the atmo-
sphere previous to Noah, nor does it explain how
anybody died previous to Noah. And it involves
the theory that all the men and animals who died
up to then only began to decay in Noah’s day,
which must have been rough on Noah.
“Hamer” : In the mildew of evening the
man who pulls the rubbish out of city dust-bins
and piles it on the pavement begins his search for
an existence. He has some queer finds. One of
the fraternity, the other day, told me that about
the best find he ever had was a double set of false
teeth embedded in a very bad apple ; but others
had chanced upon various pieces of jewellery.
Whole cigars are often discovered, and a complete
bottle of brandy is a recorded find. My informant
hadn’t come on any brandy that day, but he had
just had an exhilarating drink of methylated
spirits.

information whether he is living or dead please
communicate with

The Fatman and His Charity.
An Adelaide correspondent writes :
At the last election for the Torrens the Labor party ran
five direct Labor men for the five seats, and captured the
lot, thereby throwing' out Darling, our leader, a most
useful man, and one of the few who honestly don’t care to
grab for office. Now one hears in various quarters the
suggestion : “ Well, button up your pockets for a bit.
We who have money subscribe heavily to keep on foot a
Children’s Hospital, local-trained nursing and clothing
guilds, and other charities which are almost entirely for the
benefit of the Labor people. They think they can do
without us. Let them try it.” Well, this seems, if not
mean, at any rate, an unsportsmanlike way of taking a
licking. But is there any satisfactory reply to it ? The
privilege of ruling has always carried with it the obliga-
tion to be charitable. With the privilege rapidly vanish-
ing, does the obligation continue? Can The Bulletin
give a satisfactory answer ; not a sneering or a fighting
answer, but one that would tend to convince anyone with
an open mind on the question ?
Emphatically The Bulletin cannot give an answer
which is not a “sneering” or a “fighting” one.
When it is suggested that because the Adelaide
citizens elect to Parliament the men whose views
they agree with, the rich men of Adelaide should
button up their pockets and leave sick children to
perish in the streets, it is hardly possible to be
cool and polite. By what strange sarcasm was the
Tory party dubbed “the gentlemen’s party ”? In
our correspondent’s question there is one valuable
hint to the Democratic party abolish the
“ charity ” system in regard to hospitals and
asylums ! Make them the direct care and charge
of the State and tax the rich man for their support.
His voluntary charity is, but too often, the dole he
pays to proselytise you, the sop with which he
expects to buy your independence and debase you
to the level of the serfs. Do away with the
necessity of private charity. You may still leave
it plenty of avenues for doing good, but don’t run
the risk of Patman shutting up the Children’s
Hospital because you vote Labor.

The S.M. Herald had a reminiscence last week
in the course of an article on “ ship-building ” :
The percentage of locally-built craft trading on the
coast, however, is very small, and no vessel of 500 tons
has yet been built in Australia. Through a variety of
causes, but principally on account of the high rates of
wages existing in this part of the world, the shipbuilding
industry has not made that progress which might have
been expected.
Upwards of 30 years ago an iron screw steamer of 487
tons was built and engined here (in Sydney) for the
Queensland mail and passenger service, but no vessel
approaching her size has since been constructed in any
of the Australian ports. The steamer in question is the
Governor Blackall, built at Mort’s Dock, and now success-
fully running in the coal trade, while numbers of other
vessels built at home (in England) at the same time have
long since been consigned to the scrap heap. The Gover-
nor Blackall was a speedy vessel of 13 knots, and in her
prime was one of the most popular vessels on the Austra-
lian coast.
It is therefore evident from this fact that we can build
vessels here equally as well as the great yards at home (in
England), and the only question is one of cost. The plant
at Mort’s Dock to-day is capable of turning' out any iron or
steel steamship up to 1000, or perhaps, 1500 tons, and
there is an ample supply of skilled workmen here. Still,
with wages 80 per cent, above those ruling at home (in
England), and other severe handicaps, the local works are
unable to compete for the construction of vessels to be
employed in our own trade.
The Federal Labor men might do a think —a
large, deep, brown think —over this. Will they
reduce wages to the European level, or will they
go in for High Protection, or will they kill off
Australian industry ? There is no fourth alterna-
tive. “Sinking the fiscal issue ” isn’t an alterna-
tive. It is only a ridiculous burying of the head
in the sand and cocking of the tail up in the air.
Dr. Murphy, R.C. Archbishop of Hobart,
was born on the day of Waterloo, June 18, 1815,
and is therefore in his 91st year. Half a century
ago it was said that he could not live another 12
months. His is the oldest mitre in Christendom,
he having been consecrated in 1846. Dr. Murray,
of Maitland, was consecrated in 1866.
The sex has arrived at the conclusion that fine
clothes do not a figure make. The Corset is the thing—
not any old thing of bone and canvas, but the one mould
of 1905 form branded P.D. on the off-side. It must have
those particular letters to ensure a shape something like
the sculptured Venus Verticordia. Cast in such a mould,
almost any she-person may claim some grace and some
seductive lines. Let lovely woman have the sense to
walk abroad in a P.D. corset, and manifest her most
lasting beauty.*
Deakin gave an interview to Melbourne
Herald the other day, and the same issue of that
paper contained this explanation
Mr. Deakin, M.P., telephones to us from Parliament
House this afternoon as follows : “I have just seen for
the first time last week’s Bulletin, and am impressed by
the likeness of observations used in an article it contains
to some remarks I made to the Herald interviewer on
the defence of Australia. While I am gratified at the
coincidence, let me say that, if I had been indebted to
The Bulletin, I should have been pleased to say so.”
A coincidence which can be matched. Some
weeks ago The Bulletin suggested a scheme for
working in naval defence with the mail subsidies.
The same week B. R. Wise independently
proposed almost the same plan in London. The
coincidences show how general is the agreement
among those who give the subject a thought as to
the steps necessary for the defence of this con-
tinent.
The presence of death in the house was
accepted with perfect cheerfulness and composure
by old Tim Cullinan, of Buninyong (Vic.). Tim
and his sister (aged 85) lived in a one-roomed hut
until the other day, Friday, when the old lady
happened to die. Three days later the survivor (a
mere boy of 70) found time to report Bridget’s
death to the police, after which he returned to his
domicile to get his tea. When Constable Kelly
arrived at the house of mourning he found Tim
enjoying his tea with the corpse lying at his ,feet.
The fact that Bridget’s funeral was considerably
overdue had not made itself evident to Timothy.
On perceiving the constable, he shifted some bags
from his sister’s remains, lifted one of her hands,
and said in an admiring voice, “ There you are,
fresh as the day she died ! ” One. naturally sup-
poses that the tender-hearted old fellow must have
been a great comfort to Bridget during her last
illness.
The Commonwealth has just begun to recognise
the dim outlines of another fraud which is per-
petrated upon its industries—a fraud which was
made possible through the anxiety of the Free-
traders to help along every manufacturer, save the
Australian manufacturer. Other countries, as a
rule, levy duties on the value of goods as landed
at their own ports — i. e. , on original cost, with cost
of conveyance, insurance, and all other charges
added. Australia at first took the value at port of
shipment with 10 per cent, added. Then it was
persuaded to take the alleged value at the place of
manufacture—that is to say, to make an allowance
for alleged railway charges to port of shipment. In
most cases it has to take the other fellow’s wordl
for the amount of railway charges, and when it.
takes the American fellow’s word it finds that the-
original cost of the article is about nothing, while-
the railway oharges are something awful. In fact,,
the American article which looks as if it should
pay duty here on £lO often only pays on about
£3 or £5, when the amount which the importer
swears was railway charge is deducted. This one
American dodge is said to swindle Australia out
of £35,000 a year, which isn’t calculated to create
good feeling.

At every distribution of prizes at King’s
School, Parramatta, the fact that Justices Win-
deyer, Innes and G. B. Simpson were educated
there is trotted out. At the recent distribu-
tion another Justice was added, Justice Bring,
and the gentleman who distributed the prizes and
made flattering allusions was Justice A. H.
Simpson. Why do these Justices all talk shop ?
South Aus. Register, which is ordinarily
a rabid anti-Socialist, became a shrieking Socialist
the other day. It looks with a kindly eye on the
butter-export business; but Adelaide is a small
spot, with no particular natural advantages,
wherefore of the Lund, Aberdeen, and White Star
lines only one looks in there, and that only once a
month, whereas the whole three look in on the
butter-stores in the other more favored cities.
Therefore, the cow-squeezer who sends his stuff to
Melbourne starts off three laps or so ahead of the
cow-chaser who buys the Register. Therefore,,
the Register cried for “ equality of opportunity
for S. Australia, and when the co.’s made their new
agreement, giving “equality of opportunity,” it.
rejoiced in quite a frisky fashion. Yet even now
it isn’t clear that the dull old lady sees that it was:
barracking for the very thing that the Socialists:
barrack for, and was, in fact, using the very
phrase which is at the bottom of every Socialistic:
argument.

From Douglas Story’s “The Campaign
with Kuropatkin” :
Japan acclaims to the world her civilisation and her
progress. They are but the lacquer on her barbarism.
Liberty of thought, of opinion, of action, is unknown in
Nippon. A free press were as much a marvel in Japan as
a mastodon in Hyde Park. Comment is crushed,
criticism killed, in Japan. The man who would speak his
mind fairly and freely must place the Yellow Sea between
him and the Mikado ere he writes a word in opposition.
Japan is certainly progressing in civilisa-
tion. Recent head-lines in the Japan Chronicle :
IRREGULARITIES IN ARMY EXPENDITURE.
ALLEGATIONS AGAINST OFFICIALS AND
CONTRACTORS.
THE MINISTER FOR WAR UNDER
EXAMINATION.
LARGE SUMS INVOLVED.
From the Contemporary Review (Lon-
don) :
The sturdy English race of former times is becoming
almost extinct, and is being replaced by a puny, stunted,
sickly, sterile, narrow-chested, wealc-boned, short-sighted
and rotten-toothed race. What Great Britain requires
for the salvation of her agriculture is, in the first place,
the gradual creation of a substantial peasant class, who
work with their own hands on freehold agricultural pro-
perties of moderate size.
It is estimated that the rush of immigrants
to the United States this year will top all previous
records, in spite of the rigid enforcement of a set
of exclusion laws that Bruce Smith and the other
enemies of the white race would call “inhumanly
harsh,” &c. Uncle Sam’s high-wage, high-Protec-
tion policy makes his country so attractive to the
work-people of Europe that those of them who
can raise enough cash and are able to qualify for
citizenship are going there at the rate of thou-
sands a day (on some clays lately over 10,000 went).
The official guess for this year is over 1,000,000
immigrants.
An English-educated Japanese officer is
credited with the following candid observations
about the Anglo-Jap alliance :
It would have suited us better to have effected the
alliance with Russia which Ito failed to negotiate. This
alliance would have been offensive against you. Having
with Russia’s aid destroyed your power in the Far East,
we could have dealt with Russia in our own time. We
do not fear Russia, and we have cause for our confidence.
This latter will soon be brought home to you as the out-
come of this new alliance, in spite of the fact that it lias
been heralded by you as a guarantee for the peace of the
East in the immediate future. Are you so blind as not
to see that our aspirations to blot you out, our main
menace in the Far East, failed through Russia’s rapacity V
Well, her blood be upon her own head ; but we all wish
it had been the other way.
“ Do we learn nothing at all from history 1
Do men still adhere to the belief that the white
man and the yellow or black man can dwell
together on the same soil in peace and amity ?
Are there those who lack the imagination to see
how the whole face of the world is changed since
the yellow man shattered into irrecoverable frag-
ments the prestige of the white man in Asia, and
made his name a byword and a hissing among all
her teeming millions? Are there those who fail
utterly to apprehend that instead of Japanese
immigration being a trifling parochial issue, to be
decided by debate about comparative ‘morals,’
‘cleanliness’, ‘efficiency,’ ‘reliability,’ and other
microscopic considerations, it is in fact a phase of
one of the world’s great problems—the problem of
earth-domination, of the preservation of our
civilization, of our racial integrity ? ” — Argonaut
U.S.A.
Stuart Russell, in his “ Genesis of Queens-
land,” quotes a very old Sydney Gazette leading
article, in which the district about Brisbane had
its fortune told : “To make Moreton Bay one of
the most valuable adjuncts of the colony (N.S. W.),
nothing is wanting but a large and regular supply
of Indian and Chinese laborers—men who thor-
oughly understand the culture of tropical plants
and fruit trees, and to whose constitution the
climate is well suited. Should this supply be
afforded us, we should not find men of capital and
experience diffident of availing themselves of the
advantages offered by this district. ” It was Bris-
bane then; it was Bendigo a little later; it is
North Queensland now. The advocates of colored
labor are always finding some place that only wants
a dirty, ignorant, incompetent nigger to make
people rise up and call it blessed.
“ Dareel Remarked in a recent Bulletin
that nothing came out of the Mercadool (N.S.W.)
land inquiry. I had a trip since then to a town-
ship in that district, and was struck by the absence
of old hands of the station type—safe men, who
never squeaked. They’re all gone. They got a
sudden fit of roving, and have moved on to billets,
always in some other State, where the subpoena
troubleth not. All this because an obscure
Progress Committee sought a widening of the
scope of the Lands inquiry. In those pre-
Mercadool days, a Jew, a student of the Govern-
ment Gazette, used to haunt the Supreme Court
whenever a homestead lessee was being sold up by
the sheriff. He was a believing. Jew, for, how-
ever keen he was on securing. A lease, he could
be persuaded by someone who knew the land that
it was not a good investment. Apparently, he
always took that advice—for what it was worth—
as to-day his hat is glossier than in the days when
he hustled to go on the land.
The Bent-Gaunson alliance is one of the
most shameless of the many jobs which will
make the Irvine-Bent Government regarded
by future Victorians as Tammany is by decent
New Yorkers. When Bent decided on separate
representation for public servants, Gaunson came
forth as a Labor candidate, signed the pledge, and
went in flying. He sat on the Opposition side of
the House, but, from the first day, he was hand-in-
glove with the unspeakable Bent, whom he was
elected to fight, and has voted with him on
almost every division. He is in Bent’s office
almost as frequently as Bent himself, and some-
times sits beside the Premier when deputa-
tions call, like a veritable member of the
Ministry. That he may yet be one in fact,
is indicated by a remark he made at a recent
smoke night to the effect that the public servant’s
member must be a supporter of the Government
if he is to do good for his constituents. Of course,
a number of those who voted for Gaunson are
raging over his somersault; but he has been
clever enough to show to some small extent that
he can work more points for his constituents by
treachery to the Labor party than by hostility to
Bent. Briefly put, Gaunson says that they can apply
the very political influence, which separate repre-
sentation was introduced to prevent, more strongly
under separate representation. But as there
is still a big section which will have nothing to do
with Gaunson, Bent has announced that it will be
necessary to legislate so that the casual public
servants, as well as the permanent ones, shall-be
brought into the separate net. This will mean
two members instead of one for the public service,
and make Gaunsoh’s seat safer: Gaunson 1 is be-
coming so necessary to Bent that the safety of his
seat is a matter of importance,
