WEDNESDAYS.
Hainault, Kalgoorlie, is still being pushed for
all it is worth, profits for last three months reading £l3OO,
£l9OO, and £2,000. For a long time ore treated averaged
7dwt., but, last two months, it has been nearer Bdwt.
Reserves were only valued at 7dwt. ; so, apparently, ore
over average is now being treated. Lionel Robinson, of
London, is said to hold the bulk of shares, and the im-
provement in returns dates from his recent visit to
Kalgoorlie. On developments and prospects of mine,
market value seems to be fairly high, though it must be
admitted Manager Moss has been very successful both
with Hainault and Kalgurli.
* * * •
Ivanhoe Junction, Kalgoorlie, is driving south
at 340 ft. level of main shaft to test rich ore alleged
to have been met at 200 ft. in No. 2 shaft. Mine hitherto
has proved so patchy and disappointing that few have
any confidence in it. Again and again shareholders have
been told that pay-ore was in sight, but battery tests have
upset predictions. Mine appears to be a wild-cat, and
will probably never he anything else. 00. is £36,502 in
debt, but this is squared-up by writing a similar amount
to development, &c., as an asset. And a barren hole in
the ground is a cold asset.
* * # *
Mikado, Burtville (W. A.), a Perth flotation in
3000 shares of £5 each, of which 2000 are issued, has
joined div.-list. Two divs., Is. each, for May and June
have been declared ; but distribution amounts to only
£lOO a time. Co. owns no mill, but gets stone treated at
State battery.
» * * *
Devon Consols South Extended and Rising
Sun, on north end of Kalgoorlie belt, are under offer to
westralia Waihi crowd, Adelaide. Area is 19 acres, and
proposed co. is to be in 200,000 shares. Of these, vendors
are to get 120,000, while promoters collar 30,000, leaving
50,000 shares to provide working capital. Lode runs up
to 20ft. wide, and crushings have varied between loz. and
2oz. per ton. Proposition looks highly promising if
properly tackled, but only £5OOO working capital is
guaranteed, which is ridiculous for opening and equipping
a gold mine. g
* * * •
Sons of Gwalia, Leonora (W.A.), had a most
successful time last year, and mine is in a better position
than ever before. During year co. treated 108,800 tons
for 62,6260 z. of gold, worth £266,610. Total cost is put
at 225. l-49d. per ton, which pans out £120,360 only;
but, including depreciation, &c., costs amount to £150,480,
or 275. Bd. a ton. Estimated ore reserves are given as
362,900 tons, worth 475. 4d., so that at gross costs of last
year there should be a profit of £1 a ton. As 6£dwt.
now covers all expenses, probably a lot of ore passed by
y early days as too poor to pay, will be extracted, and
added to reserves. Developments are being steadily pushed
ahead, so co. is in a sound position.
* * * *
Mt. Jackson, Southern Cross (W.A.), whose
mill has been closed for nearly a year, chiefly for want of
water, has again started ; and during May treated 1060
tons for 5600 z. fine gold. While mill was stopped a deal
of development was done. Still, mine is small, and is
never likely to improve much on present position.
Number of W.A. mining co.’s working at a
loss appears to be increasing. During May half-a-dozen
were on the black list: —Westralia Mt. Morgans, £9OO ;
Hannan’s Star, £500; Princess Royal, £3OO ; W.F. Main
Reefs, £3OO ; North Kalgurli, £lOO ; and Waihi £lOO.
* * * *
Management of Golden Horseshoe (W.A.)
announced that for past six months no more ore would
be shipped 10 smelters ; yet May return reads—7332oz.
won by smelters and only 58790 z. won by mill. This
means that, out of 20,500 tons treated, some 1800 were
smelted for an average of 40z., and 18,700 milled for an aver-
age of about 6dwt. Unless this can be remedied soon there
must be a collapse, as supply of rich ore can’t last for
ever. May’s costs panned out at 31s. 2d., lowest on record,
and great improvement on Boulder’s 425.
* * * *
Kulamadu (Woodlark) has arrived with a rush.
For the month 798 tons were treated for 9920 z., and 20
tons of concentrates remain unsold. This is the best that
has been turned out. A div. of 6d. was promptly de-
clared, equal to £2500. Half-yearly meeting is at the end
of this month. Strange that the mine should have had
some reputedly good men as managers, yet it was left to
Nymagee Bob Heaslop, a tributor, to squeeze dividends
out of it—9d. in two months. But ore-reserves are still
very slight, and don’t amount to more than four months’
supply. Shares six months ago were unsaleable ; to-day,
buyers 4s.

<■ AM? tha * 1S slowly leaking out is that the
hydro-thermal has shown up in the ground let bv the
Berry Extended, Cresivick (Vic.), to the
Leads. This means that the rim of the ancient lake
which it was thought was within the boundary of the
Leads lease, extends west into the Extended 'northern
ground. A rather serious contingency this, and it may
account for the disappointing returns being got as latter
co. drives north towards where the tributing co. is work-
ing. Ip the Leads they seem still to be in a fog about the
course of the gutter, and now there is talk that the folk
who said that it was away to the east are not such idiots
as they were thought to be. When co. gets out of its
maze and finds settled ground, there may not be much
left to pay divs. from.
* * * *
The passing of old Dick Noble from the Long
Tunnel Extended, Walhalla (Vic.), did not surprise those
who knew how the tide had turned against him. Still,
he has left like a man, with not a whimper, and with a
host of friends in Gippsland mining towns. Starting as a
mine-boy, he went from one post to another till he was
put in control of the Extended. Then he paid over
£600,000 in divs. with scarcely a break. It remains to be
seen if anyone will be so good a Sir Oracle and as popular
with the general shareholder.
* * * *
‘‘Chuck! chuck!” says that old hen, the com-
mittea of the Stock Exchange of Melbourne, and there
comes a great cheeping from the little unfeathered chick
THE LATE GILBERT PROBYN SMITH.
in the brokers’ ring. The whole of this Umpty-Dumpty
trouble is that someone has hatched a little duckling, in
which the bulk of the shares are held in trust by the legal
manager. This system, the committee says, is fraught
with danger, because (1) a market may be created
in which Juggins may be victimised; and
(2) because a market may be created in which;
those dear little innocents, the members of the Stock
Exchange, may be cornered. But people don’t get cor-
nered when they don’t sell shares that don’t belong to
them or to their clients. If the genial spec, seller gets
caught and bowled out, he deserves punishment and not
coddling. As for No. 1 reason, why shouldn’t two or three
people gather together to work a mine and limit their lia-
bility by forming a co. if they wish to do so? If they
don’t work, and try Stock Exchange dodgery, Stock Ex-
change cuteness will soon find the fraud out and shun it.
And it is so easy to jump over the stone wall the com-
mittee would put up, by having shares put into a dozen or
twenty names. No, this row is a china egg, and it won’t
hatch anything, for brokers want stocks listed as long as
they are counters to carrying on dealing with.
* * * *
Dredging on the Loddon River (Vic.) threatens
badly, if the Loddon co. is barred from hydraulic sluicing.
River is a dirty trickle in summer and a dirty torrent in
winter, because alluvium from ploughed-up fields and
old workings gets washed into it. Pretty well 600 men
are engaged in the dredging industry in Castlemaine, and
only a few farmers will have their imaginations hurt by
the little additional sludge that gets away from the dams
of the dredge-paddocks. Last year in Victoria 59 dredge
and sluieing-plants worked 346 acres for an average return
of 143£0z. per acre, and gave employment to 1409 men.
In all, about 50,0000 z., valued at £200,000, came from pre-
viously worthless ground. Wages and maintenance aggre-
gated £104,000, and divs. amounting to £41,000 were paid.
A big slab of this was earned in the Castlemaine district,
yet a few cookies, who won’t see beyond their sun-tipped
noses, want to shut the dredges down. Mining is not in
such a healthy state in Victoria that it can afford to
hustle out of work a lot of dredges on such a cackle.
* * * *
It has taken a long time for the public to size up
the alleged “ profits ” from the first clean-up of the
South Mt. Cameron tin mine (Tas.). Chairman Tuxen
was made to say that the profit from about 16J tons of
stream tin was £1433. Stream tin is worth three-
quarters the price of the metal, as the proportion
of metal in the ore is roughly 75 per cent. Take
tin at £l3O per ton, and it leaves tlje co.’s ore worth a
shade under £lOO per ton. Deduct smelting charges and
cost of realisation, and another £lO per ton will probably
be consumed, leaving under £9O per ton as the value of
the stream tin. This brings up a total of about £l5OO as
the gross value of the 16£ tons. Yet the Argus blunderer
never saw the “ bloomer,” and gravely puffed the £1433
as profit. Such a thing was impossible. But ought not
the directors to have corrected the mis-statement ? If
the profit on nine weeks’ work runs to £5OO the co. will
be lucky.
* • * •
South Australia’s famous old Burra copper mine
has a kick left. A new ore shoot found lately adds some-
thing to the 10,000 tons of ore available, and the directors
are now hunting up a cheap old mill to dress the stuff.
* * * *
South Australia is still looking for a gold mine.
Its most consistent show for some time past, the Tarcoola
Blocks, made a loss of £BOB past half-year ; and prospects
are rather dismal.
Says Queensland Under-Sec. for Mines M‘Don-
ald in his annual report“ Experience hitherto has led
us to expect that the gradual exhaustion of our produc-
tive mines will be compensated by new discoveries ; but
the year can be credited with no development of first-
rate importance. It is in the extension of facilities for
transport, and in the more general attention to economi-
cal treatment, that the hope of expansion lies... Within
the last ten years the cost of recovering gold(atMt. Morgan)
has been brought down from £3 to a little over £1 per ton.”
Exactly. And Mt. Morgan, which is one of the hottest
shops in Queensland, hasn’t had the Kanaka or other
cheap nigger. In fact, these remarkable economies have
evolved because they haven’t had the Kanaka. Cheap
labor is the deadly enemy of progress.
* * * *
Tally of Vic. gold-mining dividends for 1904,
made early in the year at £650,434, has been cut down by
Vic. Mines Department to £623,397 ; Westralian divs. were
£2,055,959; Queensland, £831,966; N.S. W., £41,350. Of Vic.
co.’s, S. New Moon paid £120,000 (or 430 p.c. on its paid
capital), and New Moon £48,000 (or 1140 p.c.). These two
eo.’s have paid respectively in all £322,400 and £242,728.
One can get an idea of the size of Westralian mines’and
the speed with which the gold is being ripped out of
them by noting that last year alone Fingall paid
£337,500, Oroya-Brown Hill £281,000, Horseshoe £270,000,
Perseverance £227,500, and Ivanhoe £225,000—a1l more,
by the way, than Charters Towers’ brilliant rocket of last
year, Queen Cross Reef, which gave £220,000.
* * * *
Somebody writes to The Bulletin to suggest
that even the Australian life assurance office isn’t the
thing it is cracked up to be—which is carrying the
Stinking Fish business pretty far, and almost serves some
Australian insurance people right for hitherto patting the
cry on the back. The Scottish Widows, in England, it is
pointed out, works on £9 16s. lid. per cent, of premiums
whereas the A.M.P.’s expense-rate of £l3 6s. 9d. is the
best in Australia. But in comparing expenses the pro-
portion of new business has to be taken into account;
for it obviously costs far more to whip up new premiums
than to sit in an office and collect renewal premiums.
And _ whereas last year the Scottish Widows’ new
premium income was only £62,136 out of a total of
£1,097,837 (equal to 5'6 per cent.), the A.M.P.’s
was £138,426 out of a total of £1,747,599 (or 7’9 per cent).
That is to say, for its £l3 6s. 9d. the A.M.P. got nearly
50 per cent, more new business than the Scottish Widows
got for its £9 16s. lid. And the Scottish Widows is an
exceptional case, anyhow ; for the average British rate is
£l3 2s. Even that, certainly, is lower than the Austra-
lian office’s £l3 6s. 9d. But the interest earnings have
also to be taken into account. The Scottish Widows earns
only £3 17s. 4d. p.c., while the average Australian return
is about £4 Bs. Od. ; and the higher interest earnings are
a natural set-off against a lower expense rate ; for in the
country where interest and rents are high, salaries,
wages, and other expenses of running a concern will
pretty certainly be high too.

, R - s - Smythe has departed in the Geelong for London
via South Africa. He toured South Africa long before
gold and diamonds were thought of, and was the first
manager to cross the Orange River. By the same ship
went another theatrical manager, Mr. H. H. Vincent and
his wife. ’
An actor in a recent imported co. bears the comprehen-
sive nickname of “ God’s Understudy.”
Oyri! Keightley is doing steady work with the Benson
Shakspeare co. in England, in such parts as Laertes and
the Banished Duke. During the bard’s birthday week
eight plays were given at Stratford-on-Avon.
Someone described as “Nellie Stewart, fascinating
serio-comic and danseuse,” is appearing at Melbourne
Temperance Hall.
Maxine Elliott made her re-appearance at London
Lyric lately in “ Her Own Way.” Turns out to be Clyde
bitch’s piece, "Blind Man’s Buff,” played in Australia
this year by Nellie Stewart. The critics have few good
words to say for it.
Violinist Kubelik has acquired by his fiddling more
money than he can readily spend. He is now getting
together a string of racehorses to help him to go through
it, and a horse can get through an awful lot of money
when he starts, especially if he starts slowly. Also he
can get through a great deal of money when he doesn’t
start.
Bill Anderson’s proverbial good luck didn’t attend a
recent yellerdrama season at Broken Hill. The funny girl
took ill, and a serious girl had to take her place. Some
minor villains were also laid aside, and when finally the
villain-in-chief was ordered to bed by the doctor things
looked brighter than usual for the hero. However the
chief villain (Herbert Linden) pluckily kept on his legs
and did the persecuting one night with his temperature
nearly up to 104 degrees, because the management didn’t
have a monster sufficiently nefarious to take his place.
But Linden was a very limp villain by the time he was
seized by the strong arm of the Law in the last act, and if
his arrest had been delayed another half-hour he would
probably have collapsed.
Maggie Stirling, the tall mezzo-soprano, who might have
passed for a twin-sister of Lady Hopetoun of yore, is
coming back to Melbourne for good. A few years ago Miss
Stirling went to Europe for voice-culture, and put in some
work with Marchesi. Her singing is said to have
improved, but if it is only as good as when she made her
farewell appearance at Melbourne Town Hall it will be
much better than the best of the younger local vocal
talent.
Somebody protects in a loud voice that Miss Una
Bourne—the most accomplished piariiste in Australia,
without question—“ought to be sent to England to mature
and try her fortunes in the art centres of the old world.”
Apostles of foreign “ culchaw ” are unconsciously playing
the Foreign-traders’ game. This country suffers from
too much prejudice against local musical manufactures.
The ancient saying that two is company hasn’t acted in
the case of the two Adelaide skating rinks, and they are
at open war. The ice rink allows sweaters to be worn ;
the roller rink doesn’t, and advertises that no hoodlums
nor hoodlumesses will be admitted. Also it holds special
“society nights,” on which occasions every gentleman
must wear perfect creases in his trousers. All creases are
examined at the doors, and those failing to pass inspec-
tion are passed out into the night.
“Mullewa”: The “positively no encore” rule was the
cause of a peculiar incident at the Austral Society’s final
demonstration at Bendigo (Vic.) lately. A clever kiddy
from Echuca took the house by storm with a coon song,
and was demanded back vociferously. The committee sat
hard and held tight, and for three-quarters of an hour the
audience took charge and yelled. The rule held good,
nevertheless, and the programme eventually drifted on to
a tardy ending. The encore business, fostered by the
habit of getting something for nothing, is so overdone,
that managers have a choice ,of two things—to bill half
the items they intend to give, and let the rest go as
recalls, or to absolutely refuse encores. If the latter,
why grumble ? When a workman tips five loads of gravel
in my yard, I don’t expect a sixth free because I give him
three cheers for the able manner in which he tipped the
first five.
The model of Mackenhal’s “ Dancer,” representing a
nude girl playing the castanets, is a well-known Austra-
lian ballet nymph, who,' like many other Australians,
went to London to make her fortune, and found that
theatrical success there lay through the pocket of some
influential Fatman rather than through meritorious high
kicking. The girl, who is more talented than many
receiving immense salaries, in London ballets, was unable
to obtain a job even among the rank ahd file, without a
recommendation from some well-gilt patron who had the
ear of a management, and was in a bad way financially
when she heard of Mackennal’s projected statue. Then
she struck at least a moderate quantity of “ ile.” The
professional models were poseuses pure and simple, know-
ing nothing of dancing, and were incapable of entering
into the spirit of the composition. Haying the necessary
knowledge, together with the necessary shapeliness, the
Australian girl was able to do something for the honor
of her country by helping Maekennal to turn out what
seems to be regarded as his best work up to now.
Haddon Chambers’ brother, Harry, who is said to have
scored gorgeous press-notices rather than big business
with his comedy, “ Abigail ” (lately staged in New York),'
is following it up with a play called “ Frenzied Finance.’’
“Holdar M.”: In London lately one Oswald Stoll, a
“ variety ” magnate, selected from several thousand
applications some 60 stage-aspirants, and let them loose
at a special matinee at Holloway Empire Music-hall,
before a la ge audience, including many managers and
agents. Some “turns” went well, .but many gave dis-
pleasure to all—except the turners ; and out of the several
thousand original applicants about ten might be finally
set down as having a hope of success. The same day a
long-out-of-work actress, aged 25, committed suicide. It
was the only chance she saw of a permanent engagement.
Theatrical business in Britain is said to be at a
remarkably low ebb, and the managers to a man have
lost their old respect for Freetrade. A while ago they
looked for all sorts of reasons why the public stayed away
from the theatres. The lack of good plays, the surfeit
of musical shows, the general tiredness of the people,
etc., etc., were theories to account for the falling off.
But now the trouble is attributed to the country’s
growing impecuniosity. Play-going is a luxury, and the
multitude are knocking it off. They are even knocking
off beer.
“ Mac.” : The city youngster has a natural antagonism
to the police. At a carnival at Melbourne Exhibition the
other night a bushranging scene was performed. As the
troopers, unseen by the ’rangers, rode stealthily into the
arena, there was a wild yell of “ Yow ! ” from all the kids
in the sixpenny enclosure. They “yowed” from pure
instinct.
How remarkable is the vitality of Boucicault ! Four of
his pieces have been acted in Melbourne within the last
few weeks: “Arrah-na-P6gue,” the “Octoroon,” the
“ Shaughraun,” and “ After Dark.” The “ Octoroon ”
was first acted in Melbourne by Madame Duret and J. H.
Leroy. The next production was thus cast: Scudder,
Jefferson ; Pete, Stewart; Wahnotee, Clarance Holt;
M'Oloskev, Fitzgerald ; Peyton, Heir ; Sunnysides, Lam-
bert ; Ratts, Dunn ; Poindexter, Ireland; Paul, Julia
Edouin ; Zoe, Mrs. Heir ; Dora, Rosa Dunn ; Mrs. Peyton,
Mrs. Phillips ; Grace, Dolly Green. E. A. Jager, one of
our most esteemed musical professors, was the original
Melbourne “ Weeney Paul.”
Charles Brookfield, London comedian, tells of the com-
pliment that gave him most pleasure in his stage career.
He once gave a pass to a burglar who wanted to take his
wife to the theatre. Brookfield was appearing in his own
play, “ The Burglar and the Judge.” The scene in which
Brookfield broke open a safe delighted the expert in the
dress circle. After the final curtain the house-breaker
came to congratulate him, and paid him the
highest compliment he had ever received —“My word,
Mr. 8., you must have been a tea-leaf in your time.”
“ Tea-leaf ” being rhyming slang for “ thief.”
Thomas Bent shows by his awful revellings
in comic circus business that he has not yet struck
the Right Brand. When he quits his goatish
antics, the world will know he is obtaining that
inspiration to dignified conduct and wise deeds
which D.C.L. Whisky bestows on its thousands of
reasonable votaries. Thomas should lay this
advice to heart, and promptly dedicate a nip of
D.C.L. to his Little Mary.

Can anyone define the difference between
a man imported to manage a shop or factory and
a minister imported to manage a, church, do
concerns are run on the purely business lines or
nothing for nothing and not much for threepence.
Both men are paid salaries, though one calls it a

“Hving.” If we bar out the first man because ot
cuntract, it is time we finished with sentiment,

“ J.Y.” : I recently had an. interesting
conversation with the secretary of the local branch
of the Maoriland Farmers’ Union, _T he chief
reason for said union’s existence is its alleged
endeavor to secure for the State tenants the
“right to purchase” their sections. This gentle-
man mentioned casually that he owned a fine
bush-farm, which, many years ago, he had let on
an immensely long lease, with the right to purchase
at a fixed sum. Ever since that time land values
have been going up. The original tenant sold out
his goodwill and his right of purchase for a tidy
sum, and two later tenants have also sold out to
considerable advantage. “So,” said the secretary,
“three men have already made a little fortune out
of me, all by reason of the most ridiculous clause
that ever went into a lease.” And thus their own
officer knocked endways the chief plank in the
union’s platform.

At the annual meeting of the Van Diemen’s
Land Co., in London, the chairman “mentioned
that the co.’s rent-roll had increased during the
last seven years by about 120 per cent.’’ In other
words, the foreign octopus has managed to so
squeeze Tasmania that it yields 120 per cent,
more blood-money than it did seven years ago!
For the right to use the land the Tas. farmers pay
the absentee £2 4s. where they paid £1 in 1898.
And the poor creatures who govern Tasmania
haven’t the courage to hit back with anything
harder than a jelly-fish land-tax, and when the
fattest octopus of all dodges even this they remain
the same poor creatures as before.
How much does ifc cost Australia to hump
dirt backwards and forwards over the earth ? Last
year this country imported £287,024 worth of
manure. The same year it sent away 339,219,7991 b.
of wool with the dirt on it, and 111,769 tons of
silver ore, which was about half-dirt, and vast
quantities of other sorts of dirt. This dragging of
dirt all over the globe is a weary business.

My Melbourne correspondence continues: —
“New rules are enlivening the course of the
Alexandra She-Club. Men visitors are now rele-
fated to one part of the premises for tea with their
ostesses, and another portion is kept clear of all
male encumbrances. There are some members
who do not encourage man, in tweed or the
abstract, and they have secured a feeding reserve
for themselves. They there surround the sand-
wich in exclusively petticoat company, and leave
the more skittish to nibble at their scones with
Johnnie-guests.
“The bow-wow of the Tooralc poodles and
pocket terriers is heard all over the land. The
dames take their pet barkers driving and on their
call-rounds. Sometimes Jehu looks after the
beast. Other times the owner takes the yelperin
to the house and demands for it biscuits and a
milk-shake. One inveterate spinster takes her
three pets out for a walk, and blows on a chate-
laine whistle when they would fain get frisking
with the common pedestrian dog. A charming
grass-widow has been photographed with a pug
snuggling against her pink cheek. It isn't so
much a matter of Beauty and the Beast as an
elaborate token of safe company and affection
while her husband is away.
“Up in Bananaland there is a flourishing
Governesses’ Home. I don’t think there’s another
in Australia. Every dancing season a ball is held
for this Home. Considering that governesses are
the worst paid and often the worst treated of
all the she-toiling classes, the wonder is that the
Christian Endeavorers in other States, who provide
beads and handkerchiefs and socks for the dusky
heathen in far-off climes, do not turn their surplus
care to the needs of a Governesses’ Retreat.
“The new evening frocks are admitting larger
strips of bare shoulder and backbone than for-
merly. Once it didn’t matter how little of one
was revealed at the back, providing the chest was
shown to advantage. But the vagaries of Eashion
have no end. The bravest are going minus shoul-
der supports. By keeping the bodice boned up to
the top tier there is no fear of losing that portion
of the toilette in the theatre or ballroom, so they
say. But it wouldn’t be nice to try it and to sud-
denly find yourself about to swing with your
partner in a jolly set of lancers after your bodice
had slipped clean off, leaving you like an inoppor-
tune corset ‘ad.’ in the centre of the room.
“A trouser-designer lets off a wild whoop in
the lucubration termed the Sartorial Art
Journal This is some of it: —
If the tailor’s thoughts are poetic, he can issue pastorals
in colors that are charmingly suggestive of hillsides or
meadow, foliage, or waving grain ; he can make his over-
coats speak of December, his dress suits of frolic and
festivities, his frock coats of dignity and wealth, his cut-
aways of self-content, and his sacks of strenuousness, and
he can make his waistcoats jingle like laverocks, his
trousers sing- of sunshine or of rain and mud, his spring
suits chortle of hope and joy, and his summer suits
prattle of flowers. ,
In a further burst of blank hyperbole the man of
Shears trolls of imparting musical expression to
the poetically-cut clothes, suggesting a whole
orchestral range in the hue of tweed, the set of a
vest, or the hang of a coat. And yet, strangely
enough, the way the real poet bags at the knees is
often dreadful. And one of the finest poems
about a sunset that ever appeared in Australia
was penned, I am told, when the writer had no
collar on.
“Justice Madden’s youngest daughter, Beatrice,
who still wears long hair and shortish skirts,
grows more like her comely sisters, and be-
gins to appear here and there at social junkets,
m preparation for her emergence from the chry-
salis stage. Mrs. Yallange, formerly Miss Sylvia
Madden, looks older since her return from Eng-
land this time, and reveals more and more diver-
gence from the physical type supposed to be sui'*-
able to a lady whose first name is Sylvia.
“It is characteristic of the public ingratitude
that Miss Sutherland, who is a mother and a bene-
factor to the neglected children of Melbourne,
should be so low down on the list of the Herald s
Ten Best Citizens, while rich and garish self-
advertisers rushed the front seats, ibis large-
hearted lady makes the youngest infants among
her charges her own special care. Eleven babies
always sleep in her room o’ nights, and this
number is added to freely, if necessary.
“ Is elopement hereditary, like the habit of not
having any children ? Two families in this State
are asking the question. In both cases the
parents had gone to the registrar, or a ready
ecclesiastical joiner and fastener, for the marriage-
lines. Now a daughter of each couple has followed
suit. Two angry papas want to know the mean-
ing of such depravity. Two tearful mammas
retort with: ‘Have you forgotten, dear?’ Of
course they had forgotten. The brutes always do
forget little things of that kind. Henceforth,
Paterfamilias will taboo the tale of his romantic
wooing and unceremonious wedding, and forbid
his missus to repeat the story to the children.
One charming little girl, in her teens, when con-
fessing the story to her tearful but highly-
interested mommer, made the appeal go right
home. ‘lf Ernest and I are only as happy as you
and papa, I shall be content.’ After that, what
can a mommer, who is not yet grey or hardened,
do but cry and kiss her darling, and ask a blessing ?
“An auctioneer has been talking to me.
‘ Women are, above all things, covetous and emula-
tive. A knowing party sets a bid rising, and all
the others follow suit till I blush at the figure they
have run a rather cheap bit of bric-a-brac to. At
a recent sale in Malvern a record price was struck
for all the big items in the catalogue. I assure
you the bidding was never brisker at a house and
furniture sale in my recollection. The figures ex-
ceeded our wildest expectations. A few men in
the trade closed up their lists and cleared early in
the sale—seeing, as I did, that the wealthy women
had lost their heads, and were out-bidding each
other regardless of price. Carpets, curtains, two
pianos, and all the heavy furniture brought more
than was originally paid for them. And this after
two years’ use ! ’
“ There is sadly wanted a convent-school series
of picture post-cards. Last week 1 had an im-
pulse to add to a cloistered kid’s collection. I
expected to easily seize on a suitable one at a
hazard, scrawl the address, and drop it in
the postal pillar. But the first was a wicked
thing. The second was a nerve-shocker. The
third was a violent atrocity. The fourth was all
bibulous buffoonery. The fifth was an insult to
the sex that can’t help sometimes remaining single.
The sixth was gross. . The most decorous were
the least artistic. I fingered hundreds in a fruit-
less hunt for one in any way suitable., The comic
selection (the saleswoman told me, in a tired tone,
they were sold as comic) were chiefly dog and
policeman. I left the shop with a pussy-cat series
rather than hurt the feelings of the saleswoman.
Some idle photographer or etcher should give his
attention to a series of post-cards for the very
young and innocent.
Yours Affectionately,
AKENEHI

Mr. W. G. Hearne.
Dear Sir, —In 1898, I was for four years previously in
Queensland travelling: representative for a mercantile
house. Having- contracted a heavy cold, I placed myself
under a medical man, and used all kinds of chemists’
prescriptions without avail. I was then staying at Hayes’
Terminus Hotel, Brisbane. The manager did all possible
for me, for which I shall always feel grateful. Mr. Duncan
Currie, at that time acting manager for the New York
Life Insurance Co., came to see me daily, and he advised
me to try your Bronchitis Cure. I was about done for,
could not eat, or scarcely swallow ; in fact, used to nearly
suffocate a dozen times a day, suffering intensely. Guess
of my thankfulness, when Mr. Currie brought me a bottle
of your Bronchitis Cure. A few doses actually cured me.
I took another bottle away from the city with me, and
gave part of it to a young girl at Southport, and it also
saved her life. The balance I gave to Mr. T. McMillan,
Tweed River—three persons cured by your valuable
medicine. Although I have camped out and travelled
about 10,000 miles since my illness, I have not contracted
a cold since.—l am, Sir, your best well-wisher,
THOS. ROSS,
Labasa, via Suva, Fiji.

dition of the hands becomes a positive torture. First the skin
loses its pleasant “feel,’’ and there is pronounced roughness and redness,

Down the Gorge.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I HEARD a bit-bar rattle
And horse-hoofs pacing near.
In the starlight and the silence—
How they broke the starlight silence
In the gorge !
Then I heard the tramp of cattle—
Heard a leader bellow clear,
As he led the mob, a-staring—
Breathing hard with big eyes staring,
Down the gorge.
Down the gorge a drover jingled,
In the starlight pictured clear,
Oh ! his horse stepped out so gaily,
Going homeward, blithe and gaily,
Down the gorge.
And the tramp of cattle mingled
With the jangling saddle-gear,
As they shouldered down the roadway—
Fence to cliff they filled the roadway —
Down the gorge.
Flash of horns and scent of meadows,
. Round the bend the cattle came—
Just a mass beneath the star-light,
Shapeless bodies ’neath the star-light—
In the gorge.
Staring wild-eyed at the shadows,
Partly wild and partly tame ;
And the drover started singing—
Light of heart he led them, singing,
Down the gorge.
Down the gorge came noisy laughter
And the cracking of a whip,
And the mass of cattle shuddered —
Snorted loud and plunged and shuddered
Down the gorge.
Came a string of wild oaths after
From a ribald, mocking lip,
As the pack-horse brought the camp-gear.
Trotting to the clank of camp-gear,
Down the gorge.
Down the gorge the leaders bellowed,
Challenging the drover’s song.
But the cliff is on the near side.
And a fence is on the sheer side, j
Down the gorge.
And the drovers’ grim hearts mellowed
As they rushed the mob along ;
For the cattle could not scatter—
There is little room to scatter
Down the gorge.
Came at last the stragglers running
From the stinging, cracking lash
Of the sturdy, silent drovers,
All abreast they rode—three drovers —
Down the gorge.
And the whips were lithe and cunning ;
With their horns and eyes a-flash,
Came the tired and footsore stragglers,
Running from the whips—poor stragglers !
Down the gorge.
Down the gorge I heard the mellow
Voice of someone singing still,
And the dust hung in the roadway—
Just a film above the roadway—
In the gorge.
And an old cow’s startling bellow
Echoed loud and very shrill,
As she went with soft eyes staring,
All alert and great eyes staring,
Down the gorge.
Then the dust sank in the shadows,
The last hoof-beat died away ;
And once more a sleepy silence—
Such a starlit, radiant silence—
Filled the gorge.
Came a whiff of scented meadows,
Ripening corn and drying hay.
Then I heard a weka calling—
Bike a lost soul, calling, calling—
Up the gorge.
ii. 1..
WILL DAWSON.
The Prescription.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
f INHERE was an indefinable suggestion of
familiarity about the figure which emerged
from the dark archway. This suggestion
resolved itself as the man’s face took the light.
Of course; it was Ellars. Years ago, somewhat
to my relief, I had lost touch with him. Rumor
had spoken, in uncertain voice, of a position a 3
chemist's assistant, in Melbourne. At all events,
his accustomed place knew him no more. And
now, his face, springing suddenly upon me out of
the dark, woke old memories.
“Well, dear old chap!” he said. His same
infernal manner. Then, “ I’m stumped ; cleaned
out; stony,” he informed me in dramatic cres-
cendo. In the face of this, there could remain no
possible doubt. It was Ellars, right enough.
And I had that day put my money on the right
horse. Five shillings, perhaps
He took the money with extravagant promises
of repayment.
“You’re just the same as ever,” he murmured,
gratefully; “a friend in need.” He counted the
spoils, and his face lit up. “ Why, this is gene-
rous. ” And, amongst the silver, behold the yellow
gleam of gold. One pound four ! Confound it!
I resolved to check in future my vicious habit of
surrendering to my generous impulses. It was no
use explaining my mistake to him. I knew him.
With unexampled audacity he invited me to
drink. A refusal was upon the tip of my tongue.
And then, quite casually, he spoke of a dead
friend. I am, by nature, somewhat sentimental.
I changed my mind ; after all
This drink, for which, by the way, he changed
my sovereign, led to others. Then came Harry
Ford and young Pegrum.
“ Hullo, , Ellars ! Still alive? Hoped you
were dead,” said the uncompromising Ford.
Ellars laughed. “The same as ever,” he said.
“ The same old rough diamond.”
“ I heard,” answered Ford, “ some tale of
poisoned women in Melbourne seven years ago.
Thought you might have ” He encircled his
throat with both hands and stuck his tongue out.
“By Jove! That was a funny affair. But
you’ve got it wrong—nobody died. It might
have worked out that way, though. If you’d like
to hear ” —and then he started.
“ I came out to this blistered country because my
father was such a Oh, I never told you about
him, did I? Well, he was an unctuous and holy
sea-captain, and had other drawbacks. I ran
away when I was 16, and had a rough time. I
got a job at a chemist’s in London; I’d nearly
finished my course in chemistry at the Polytechnic.
Fifteen shillings a week—and I earned it. But I
learned a good deal about dispensing there.
After two years’ misery and semi-starvation I
found a berth as under-steward on the old Cuzco.
A steward sees human nature with its pretty
clothes off. He gets low ideas, and —well, I sup-
pose I was not sufficiently servile.
“ I landed here with £1 4s. Bd. in my pocket. I
remember thinking, as I counted it, of the pleasure
it would give me to look back from the lofty emi-
nence of £4OOO a year, upon the poor lad of
eighteen who had landed—Bah ! The one-four-
and-eightpenny stage has been, with sundry
fluctuations, my financial footing ever since, said
fluctuations usually upon the wrong side. Five
years after that I had risen on stepping-stones of
my dead patients to higher things. I was a duly
unqualified doctor in a very small way. It was
about that time that I met you. You took me up
enthusiastically as a new addition to your artistic
menagerie. I appeared as the picturesque black-
guard —oh yes, I recognised my distorted features
in your very indifferent illustrations. Recollect
that one about the villainous seducer, of gentle-
manly manners? But, Lord bless you, I’m an
easy-going chap. And you had to live, same as
me.
“So I sold electric belts and aqua pumpa and
cascara, and tincture of valerian, and sulphate of
magnesia to timid souls whose insides were out of
order. Indigestion is a great boon to the quacks.
It is responsible for that tired feeling and those spots
before the eyes that we notice as the chief items of
news in the daily papers. If I’d only had the
money to advertise ! Ah, well!
“Then came the Medical Act. I tell you, it
fluttered the duckponds. Some of the biggest of
us brought in a real M.D. who had fallen —drink
—cards—or —... Falling is so easy, and there are
so many ways ! The quack would run his business
under the poor devil’s name, and all would go
well. But for us ducklings it was a freeze-out. I
tried calling myself a ‘specialist,’ but it didn’t
seem to cut ice the way ‘doctor’ did. And,
anyway, I was too young.
“ Wherefore you behold me with old Burden,
druggist, Collins-street, Melbourne. Funny old
chap. Burden—snuffling, slipshod—wore an old
silk coat and talked about his long experience.
But he was a white man, and he liked me.
‘ You’re a good dispenser,’ he’d say, ‘but you lack
initiative, Bob.’ Funny how we strike others,
isn’t it? ‘Too literal’ was another criticism of
Burden’s.
“ My word, there are some funny doings in a
chemist’s shop !If the doctors only knew—or the
public ! I’ve seen Burden deliberately alter quan-
tities in a prescription—even going so far as to
take out an ingredient he considered dangerous.
As for me, I put ’em all in, hence the complaint
of literalness. This particular trouble started
with a badly-written prescription. I’ve kept it as
a memento of the worst time I ever spent. Here
it is.”
He took out the paper ; it was badly written—
almost as illegible as a Chinese sign, or a decadent
poet’s manuscript.
I handed it to Ford. Ford laughed. “ That
doesn’t mean anything?” he said questioningly..
“Yes it does,” replied Ellars. “ It’s quite in-
telligible—a sort of influenza tonic. It was the
last ingredient that I misunderstood. He has
written it as if it were Ag. Chlor. You notice
the way the tail of the second letter turns. _
“Well, Burden was out when this atrocity was
brought in. A navvy, half-seas over, handed it
across the counter to me and sat, owlishly regard-
ing the green and red bottles in the window, while
I made it up. ‘Ag chlor ’ gave me pause, though.
Seemed a lot of lunar caustic —well! It wasn’t
my funeral; the doctor ought to know; he had
put it there. In it went.
“‘Here you are, sir,’ I said. ‘Half-a-crown,
please.’ As he fumbled in his pocket he gave me
incoherent particulars of his ‘ missus’ ’ illness. I
got rid of him as quickly as I could. He called
me ‘ mate,’ and I didn’t like it, being still young
and sensitive. You laugh. Well, never mind. I
was, though.
“ I showed the copy of the prescription to Bur-
den when he came in. ‘ What’s this ?’ he said,
pointing to the last item. * That’s what it said,’
I answered.
“‘Good Lord!’ His eyes bulged out of his
head. ‘ You’ve never put that in ? ’
“ ‘ I have.’
“‘You must have read it wrong. Sure that
“ g ” wasn’t a “ q ”? ’
“*lf it was, it was a damned bad “ q,” ’ I said.
“ ‘But these other things. You ass, Bob ! Can’t
you see, it’s a tonic ? And what on earth would
silver chloride be doing in a tonic ? We’ll have to
get that bottle back, quick. Stay here, while I
go and see Dr. Layton. I’ll get the address from
him.’
“He came back with flag half-mast. The doctor
couldn’t give the address. It was a lodge patient;
he didn’t keep account of minor cases. But it was
a ‘ g.’ And, if one inquired at the lodge, &c., &c.
One did inquire. I hunted up the South Yarra
branch of the Aged Disorder of Incoherent Owls.
They couldn’t tell me. I was scared a whole lot.
The obscene George had moved quite recently, and
had not, so far, given his new address. Perhaps
if I called round that evening, about 7, and all the
rest of it. You know—nice and polite, but use-
less. Still, I gave it one more trial; I arrived on
schedule time, but George was unpleasantly absent.
I went back feeling pretty sick, and old Burden
and myself resigned ourselves to the worst. I
didn’t sleep much that night, and got up at day-
break to study the morning paper. But there
were no sudden deaths which seemed to indicate
that infernal medicine.
“At ten o’clock I was making up pdls, when
lo ! Mr. George. I dodged behind the dispensing
screen, and . nearly fainted. It s coming, I
thought, ‘Good heavens, it’s coming.’ Burden
faced the music—there is something in experience
after all. He was in a blue funk, but he put on
his best professional manner; sort of mixture of
profound knowledge and sympathetic interest. I
listened, shaking in my shoes ; I could hear my
teeth rattling. ,
“ ‘ I say, mister, began the unspeakable George,
‘ that medicine ’
•“Well. Anything wrong?’
“ The navvy shifted his weight from leg to leg.
‘Yes,’ he said. . . „ , ,
“Imagine my sensations. But Burden kept
cool, and only said ‘Well?’ _
‘“Ye s«e, mister, ’twas this way. The young
bloke here can tell you—well, I s’pose he saw I
was a bit shikkered yesterday when I come m for
the lotion. I’d bin out o’ work a couple o’ days—
you know ’ow a bloke meets ’is mates. Come-
an’-’ave-a-drink ... See ! ’Alf a dozen times.
An’ there you are.’
“Burden waited, all attention.
“‘An’ when I left ’ere—well, a few more
mates —an’ a few more wets. Fact is, I lost the
dashed bottle —beg pardon, mister. Seem to
remember droppin’ it an’ ’ —he scratched his head.
I could have embraced him. ‘I say, mister,’he
went on, ‘seein’ it warn’t used—’arf a crown is
’arf a crown these days.’
*• I came from behind the screen ; my relief left
me shaky at the knees.
“ ‘Bob,’ said Burden, ‘I think it’s worth half a
crown, don’t you ? ’
“ Burden was a white man—he didn’t even sack
me. Gave me an improving lecture on the
chemist as interpreter, drawing analogies from
that chap with the long hair who plays the piano.
‘Just,’said he, ‘as what’s-his-name stands mid-
way between the great composers and the music-
lovers, so we stand between the doctors and their
patients. And, by heavens, the patients need it. ’
“Well, he may have been right. He died a
year ago ; peace to his ashes. And that’s all the
story.”
“Five minutes past eleven, gentlemen,” said
the weary barmaid.

Samson’s Christening.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
ONE of the deftest things that had
marked the short career of Samson Bor ley
Brown was the naming of him—all were
agreed upon that.
And, like many another successful achievement,
this was the result of compromise ; for Samson had
been born to a social station, where strife and dis-
putation are breathed in the very air.
_ However, there it was : Samson from his sire’s
sire; Borley from his mother’s. It mattered
nothing to Samson that, before this felicitous
expedient had been adopted, his father, “Billy”
Brown, and his mother, Tilda, had been in an°ry
contention over the matter; that Tilda had
resisted Billy’s proposal to perpetuate his own
William as being common, and had desired
to substitute Belvidere, a prince in her novelette,
who had gathered her emotions as a bundle
of twigs in his beautiful white hands, and
set them blazing in her foolish heart. Serenely
passive, too, had Samson lain when Billy
smarting under opposition, disparagement and
jealousy, had promptly allayed his feelings
by laying out” Tilda with a right “hook?”
otil], despite all hindrances, matters had been finally
arranged; and only that very Sunday afternoon
had Samson been baptised by the Church, and
pledged to manfully fight against sin, the world,
and the devil to his life’s end.
Not that the residents of Sydney “Rocks”
were touchy on nice observance of such rites. On
the contrary, this signal distinction for Samson
had been scored for him by Tilda in the teeth of
powerful opposition, and had set some neighbors
staring open-eyed, and moved others to ribald
laughter and jest.
On the whole, the party had managed to assume
a tolerable air of decorum. Samson himself, gor-
geous in strong and violently-opposed colors;
Billy and his friend “ Dodo ” Betts, each adorned
with a narrow streak of red tie (minus collar),
which exactly bisected lengthwise a plaster of
white shirt-front; Billy’s maternal grandmother
smartened up with odds and ends; Lila Seers,
“friend” of Dodo’s, so redundantly wrought in
hues and gauds as to drive a short, sharp little
intake of breath through Tilda every time her
wistful gaze tore itself from them; and, finally,
Tilda herself, whom the demands of Samson had
left no more than creditably neat. The ugly
mark on her face from Billy’s fist was in some
manner cunningly put out of sight.
As the party straggled home from the church
through the devious paths of the “Rocks,” with
the sun turning his search-light upon many a
crime-knowing, wicked old wall, unrepentant in
its_ old age, Tilda had been conscious of a sub-
duing emotion she had not experienced since the
day of her marriage in the same church 12 months
before. Well, Samson had been started decently,
at any rate; and if luck favored them, he should
be put to something when he grew up that would
keep him respectable and honest—better, Tilda
reflected, than Billy’s own dubious calling.
This, by the way, was inherently elusive and
baffling of description. Billy did an occasional
fay out as crier with the bottles or the wild
rabbi ; was a plausible expounder at suburban
doorways of the horse’s plight and the bargain of
wood waiting in the cart; and did any odd jobs
for which he could prefer himself, or which he
could be induced to “take on.” But Tilda was
hopeful even for Billy himself—if he would only
work ! He was but twenty-two. There was an old
look about his face, though, which would have be-
lied this; moreover, she did not carry her own
twenty years any too well.
I here is a bad old custom which has strained
through the forebears of such as Billy, and
this custom demands that a christening should
be marked by the excessive imbibing of strong
drink; and though Tilda had mildly pro-
tested, the liquor had been produced as soon
as tea was over. However, she desired to
avoid being censorious, and for comfort her
eyes would stray now and then to Samson as
he lay asleep on the floor in his finery, which she
had not yet found it in her heart to remove.
There was never any difficulty in getting Samson
to sleep ; indeed his gravity and silent acquiescence
m all that befel him sat somewhat curiously on his
small face.
As the evening wore on and cards were aban-
doned, the conversation began to betray a tendency
to dangerous personalities, and Tilda’s hopes of
getting Billy to bed sober, and of closing the
festival without infraction of harmony, became a
little desperate. Gathered round the table, too,
as they were, the party had just that quality of
fixed proximity which was a menace in itself.
■u j u sheer ill-luck Tilda herself, who alone
had been worried by fears of discord, was the
first to unwittingly provoke it into flame.
* Well, if I can,” she had exclaimed earnestly
n;he conversation had turned to the future of
Samson), “ 111 keep ’im straight; any’ow, so’s ’e
don t see th’ inside o’ no gaol, please Gawd.”
Now this was a singularly infelicitous remark.
She saw it herself in a flash ; but unluckily it was
beyond recall.
Billy scowled and Lila laughed.
“Yus,” observed Lila; “’avin’ bin in yerself
yer can tell ’im wot it’s like, can’t yer,” lauehine
again. & &
“Shut yer bioomin’ mouth !” growled Dodo.
Shan t! ” snapped Lila, spite leaping to her
flushed face.
“ ’Ere, my Gawd, wot’s this ? ” demanded Billy,
looking round helplessly, his face suddenly lit
with stark surprise.
“There, there,” maudlinly soothed grandma,
wed agreed not ter tell yer, thinkin’ as you,
bem ead o the family, wouldn’t like it. ’Twas
on y a week, an’ it’s all over long ago. You be
quiet, Billy ; ’ave a little drop more.”
‘‘No. no!” anxiously cried Tilda; “no more
Billy.

Let im soothe ’is feelin’s, silly—can’t yer see

Work, yer call it,” laughed Lila, disparag-

Don tbe so fretful! On’y ’irn in ’is laundry so’s

‘‘Why didn’t you graft?” furiously inquired
Billy, turning on her with an oath. “ Wot am I

“Don’t be so fretful!” coquettishly repeated
grandma, coyly shaking her shoulders with
drunken pretence of offence.
“Dodo, Lila, go now,” pleaded Tilda, with
harassed face and desperate resolution. “I ain’t
unfriendly, but ”
“ I won’t ’ave ’em sent away,” hotly cried Billy,
smarting at this exclusion of his authority and
conscious that he hadn’t been showing to striking
advantage.
*'You wouldn’t’a’ got much work from Kong
Mo, venomously resumed Lila, smiling with de-

at her status, were still rankling. “He mostly ’as
Chows. P’r’aps ’e might ’a’ made up, though, by

insinuating malice.
Hell! ! ” rang out from Billy. In an instant
he was on his feet, his semi-drunken repining and
contemplation of his wrongs suddenly blazing into
ugly savagery.
“Is it true?” he shouted at Tilda, standing
over her with truculent and menacing mien. <£ If
it is, Gawd strike me dead, I’m goin’ ter settle
yer ! Chuck it out! ”
“No ! ” she answered, cowering and raising her
arm to her face.
“ Sit down ! ’’ cried Dodo, menacingly, to Billy,
his face tense with rising passion, “or I’ll make
yer. ” And, by sheer weight of strength, he forced
him down.
But Billy’s passion, now worked up to boiling
point, had been cheated of any appeasing, and he
stared about him, with doubt and hesitation, until
his roving eye fell on the sleeping Samson.
Look at is fine clobber, ” he exclaimed, with
bitter reflection; “ money as ought ter be in my
pocket. ”
And then, in a flash, an idea occurred to him.
Darting across the room, he picked up the child,
and turning it to the light, peered long and
•losely into its faee. Startled from his slumber,
Samson opened his eyes, and mildly and patiently
returned the burning* look of the two evil-looking
red orbs he saw shining down upon him. Gra-
dually, the shadow of some sinister conviction
clouded Billy’s faoe, until it transformed it to a
mask of insensate blood-lust and cruelty.
By Gawd, ’ he yelled, suddenly electrified to
action, it s true ! I done with both on yer now.
Take the little josser !”
And, raising Samson on high, he flung him
down with ferocious energy at Tilda’s feet.
One long piercing shriek rent the little room.
Then came the moans and anguish of a heart riven
in pieces. But Samson was silent and still.
Yer satisfied?” snarled Dodo, low-voiced to
Lua. And without a sign the pair slunk silently
away.
* * * *
Grandma had tottered to the kitchen, and the
twain remaining exchanged no word. Tilda held
Samson closely pressed to her breast, her down-
cast, dull eyes looking at nothing out of a tearless
face, in which the light was extinguished.
Billy rose from the chair on which he had flung
himself, and reached for a bottle standing on the
table. Finding it to be empty, he slung it fiercely
into the fireplace, where it crashed into pieces.
At the instant the door opened, and there
briskly entered two men in uniforms and helmets.
One laid compelling and practised hands on Billy,
and, despite his violent, oath-punctured struggles,
held him easily.
“If yer dare to ’urt ’im,” shrieked Tilda,
springing up and advancing from her seat, and
hitching her charge closer, “I’ll”
“Shut up!” jeered Billy as, cleverly freeing
one arm, he sent her reeling backwards with a
blow that covered her mouth with blood.
The policeman said something that wasn’t in
the regulations, and snatched Billy violently out
into the darkness.
Tilda felt weak and sick with the force of the
blow Billy had dealt her, while the pressure of
events that were searing themselves into her brain
left her dazed, inert, and helpless. Everything
now, and all that had happened, seemed to be
separated from her by a film. She remembered
the afternoon ; the church ; the sunshine; the
hopes, too, for Billy and Samson—hopes that now
seemed like the gibbering spawn of idiots, mock-
ing her. And Samson, of course, particularly;
the sign of the cross on his forehead, where the
big blue mark was now—the mark of the blow
that had stilled him as a “faithful soldier and
servant ” for ever... How monotonous the clock
was, with its constant ticking !. ..
And then she heard the cackling tones of
grandma crooning herself to sleep with creaking,
crazy melody about a “Mistletoe Bough”—her
habit when drunk. And that, for a moment,
brought her to mundane things, and to remember
that there was scarcely any food in the house, nor
even a supply of oil to replenish the flickering lamp.
She glanced stonily, with what seemed a dead face,
towards her bedroom. Well, she had often crept to
it alone, after waiting for Billy to return from the
“ two-up ” club ; but he wouldn’t return to-night.
And she thought, as she mused, that marriage
must be the greatest “two-up” game in the
world; wherein for such as she, at any rate, there
was no luck in the fall of the coins. All the coins
of her life which she had spun to chance had fallen
prone to the earth hopelessly wrong in their
results.
And then the clock ceased ticking, and the room
became very quiet.
And in the quiet the door opened, and a man
entered. He was big and powerfully built, and
when he spoke his voice sounded strong and deep
